10126{_}9{_}HOME LIFE OF THE LANCASHIRE FACTORY FOLK DURING THE COTTON FAMINE.
10126{_}10{_}(Reprinted from the Manchester Examiner and Times of 1862)    CHAPTER I.
10126{_}11{_}AMONG THE BLACKBURN OPERATIVES "Poor Tom's a-cold.
10126{_}12{_}Who gives anything to poor Tom?"
10126{_}13{_}--King Lear.
10126{_}14{_}Blackburn is one of the towns which has suffered more than the rest in the present crisis, and yet a stranger to the place would not see anything in its outward appearance indicative of this adverse nip of the times.
10126{_}15{_}But to any one familiar with the town in its prosperity, the first glance shows that there is now something different on foot there, as it did to me on Friday last.
10126{_}16{_}The morning was wet and raw, a state of weather in which Blackburn does not wear an Arcadian aspect, when trade is good.
10126{_}17{_}Looking round from the front of the railway station, the first thing which struck me was the great number of tall chimneys which were smokeless, and the unusual clearness of the air.
10126{_}18{_}Compared with the appearance of the town when in full activity, there is now a look of doleful holiday, an unnatural fast-day quietness about everything.
10126{_}19{_}There were few carts astir, and not so many people in the streets as usual, although so many are out of work there.
10126{_}20{_}Several, in the garb of factory operatives, were leaning upon the bridge, and others were trailing along in twos and threes, looking listless and cold; but nobody seemed in a hurry.
10126{_}21{_}Very little of the old briskness was visible.
10126{_}22{_}When the mills are in full work, the streets are busy with heavy loads of twist and cloth; and the workpeople hurry in blithe crowds to and from the factories, full of life and glee, for factory labour is not so hurtful to healthy life as it was thirty years ago, nor as some people think it now, who don't know much about it.
10126{_}23{_}There were few people at the shop windows, and fewer inside.
10126{_}24{_}I went into some of the shops to buy trifling things of different kinds, making inquiries about the state of trade meanwhile, and, wherever I went, I met with the same gloomy answers.
10126{_}25{_}They were doing nothing, taking nothing; and they didn't know how things would end.
10126{_}26{_}They had the usual expenses going on, with increasing rates, and a fearfully lessened income, still growing less.
10126{_}27{_}And yet they durst not complain; but had to contribute towards the relief of their starving neighbours, sometimes even when they themselves ought to be receiving relief, if their true condition was known.
10126{_}28{_}I heard of several shopkeepers who had not taken more across their counters for weeks past than would pay their rents, and some were not doing even so much as that.
10126{_}29{_}This is one painful bit of the kernel of life in Blackburn just now, which is concealed by the quiet shell of outward appearance.
10126{_}30{_}Beyond this unusual quietness, a stranger will not see much of the pinch of the times, unless he goes deeper; for the people of Lancashire never were remarkable for hawking their troubles much about the world.
10126{_}31{_}In the present untoward pass, their deportment, as a whole, has been worthy of themselves, and their wants have been worthily met by their own neighbours.
10126{_}32{_}What it may become necessary to do hereafter, does not yet appear.
10126{_}33{_}It is a calamity arising, partly from a wise national forbearance, which will repay itself richly in the long run.
10126{_}34{_}But, apart from that wide- spread poverty which is already known and relieved, there is, in times like the present, always a certain small proportion, even of the poorest, who will "eat their cake to th' edge," and then starve bitterly before they will complain.
10126{_}35{_}These are the flower of our working population; they are of finer stuff than the common staple of human nature.
10126{_}36{_}Amongst such there must be many touching cases of distress which do not come to light, even by accident.
10126{_}37{_}If they did, nobody can doubt the existence of a generous will to relieve them generously.
10126{_}38{_}To meet such cases, it is pleasant to learn, however, as I did, that there is a large amount of private benevolence at work in Blackburn, industriously searching out the most deserving cases of distress.
10126{_}39{_}Of course, this kind of benevolence never gets into the statistics of relief, but it will not the less meet with its reward.
10126{_}40{_}I heard also of one or two wealthy men whose names do not appear as contributors to the public relief fund, who have preferred to spend considerable sums of money in this private way.
10126{_}41{_}In my wanderings about the town I heard also of several instances of poor people holding relief tickets, who, upon meeting with some temporary employment, have returned their tickets to the committee for the benefit of those less fortunate than themselves.
10126{_}42{_}Waiving for the present all mention of the opposite picture; these things are alike honourable to both rich and poor.
10126{_}43{_}A little past noon, on Friday, I set out to visit the great stone quarries on the southern edge of the town, where upwards of six hundred of the more robust factory operatives are employed in the lighter work of the quarries.
10126{_}44{_}This labour consists principally of breaking up the small stone found in the facings of the solid rock, for the purpose of road-mending and the like.
10126{_}45{_}Some, also, are employed in agricultural work, on the ground belonging to the fine new workhouse there.
10126{_}46{_}These factory operatives, at the workhouse grounds, and in the quarries, are paid one shilling a day--not much, but much better than the bread of idleness; and for the most part, the men like it better, I am told.
10126{_}47{_}The first quarry I walked into was the one known by the name of "Hacking's Shorrock Delph."
10126{_}48{_}There I sauntered about, looking at the scene.
10126{_}49{_}It was not difficult to distinguish the trained quarrymen from the rest.
10126{_}50{_}The latter did not seem to be working very hard at their new employment, and it can hardly be expected that they should, considering the great difference between it and their usual labour.
10126{_}51{_}Leaning on their spades and hammers, they watched me with a natural curiosity, as if wondering whether I was a new ganger, or a contractor come to buy stone.
10126{_}52{_}There were men of all ages amongst them, from about eighteen years old to white-headed men past sixty.
10126{_}53{_}Most of them looked healthy and a little embrowned by recent exposure to the weather; and here and there was a pinched face which told its own tale.
10126{_}54{_}I got into talk with a quiet, hardy-looking man, dressed in soil-stained corduroy.
10126{_}55{_}He was a kind of overlooker.
10126{_}56{_}He told me that there were from eighty to ninety factory hands employed in that quarry.
10126{_}57{_}"But," said he, "it varies a bit, yo known.
10126{_}58{_}Some on 'em gets knocked up neaw an' then, an' they han to stop a-whoam a day or two; an' some on 'em connot ston gettin' weet through--it mays 'em ill; an' here an' theer one turns up at doesn't like the job at o'--they'd rayther clem.
10126{_}59{_}There is at's both willin' an' able; thoose are likely to get a better job, somewheer.
10126{_}60{_}There's othersome at's willin' enough, but connot ston th' racket.
10126{_}61{_}They dun middlin', tak 'em one wi' another, an' considerin' that they're noan use't to th' wark.
10126{_}62{_}Th' hommer fo's leet wi' 'em; but we dunnot like to push 'em so mich, yo known--for what's a shillin' a day?
10126{_}63{_}Aw know some odd uns i' this delph at never tastes fro mornin' till they'n done at neet,--an' says nought abeawt it, noather.
10126{_}64{_}But they'n families.
10126{_}65{_}Beside, fro wake lads, sick as yon, at's bin train't to nought but leet wark, an' a warm place to wortch in, what con yo expect?
10126{_}66{_}We'n had a deeal o' bother wi 'em abeawt bein' paid for weet days, when they couldn't wortch.
10126{_}67{_}They wur not paid for weet days at th' furst; an' they geet it into their yeds at Shorrock were to blame.
10126{_}68{_}Shorrock's th' paymaister, under th' Guardians, But, then, he nobbut went accordin' to orders, yo known.
10126{_}69{_}At last, th' Board sattle't that they mut be paid for weet and dry,- -an' there's bin quietness sin'.
10126{_}70{_}They wortchen fro eight till five; an', sometimes, when they'n done, they drilln o' together i'th road yon--just like sodiurs--an' then they walken away i' procession.
10126{_}71{_}But stop a bit;--just go in yon, an' aw'll come to yo in a two-thre minutes."
10126{_}72{_}He returned, accompanied by the paymaster, who offered to conduct me through the other delphs.
10126{_}73{_}Running over his pay-book, he showed me, by figures opposite each man's name, that, with not more than a dozen exceptions, they had all families of children, ranging in number from two to nine.
10126{_}74{_}He then pointed out the way over a knoll, to the next quarry, which is called "Hacking's Gillies' Delph," saying that he would follow me thither.
10126{_}75{_}I walked on, stopping for him on the nearest edge of the quarry, which commanded a full view of the men below.
10126{_}76{_}They seemed to be waiting very hard for something just then, and they stared at me, as the rest had done; but in a few minutes, just as I began to hear the paymaster's footsteps behind me, the man at the nearest end of the quarry called "Shorrock!"
10126{_}77{_}and a sudden activity woke up along the line.
10126{_}78{_}Shorrock then pointed to a corner of the delph where two of these poor fellows had been killed the week before, by stones thrown out from a fall of earth.
10126{_}79{_}We went down through the delph, and up the slope, by the place where the older men were at work in the poorhouse grounds.
10126{_}80{_}Crossing the Darwen road, we passed the other delphs, where the scene was much the same as in the rest, except that more men were employed there.
10126{_}81{_}As we went on, one poor fellow was trolling a snatch of song, as he hammered away at the stones.
10126{_}82{_}"Thir't merry, owd mon," said I, in passing.
10126{_}83{_}"Well," replied he, "cryin' 'll do nought, wilt?"
10126{_}84{_}And then, as I walked away, he shouted after me, with a sort of sad smile, "It's a poor heart at never rejoices, maister."
10126{_}85{_}Leaving the quarries, we waited below, until the men had struck work for the day, and the whole six hundred came trooping down the road, looking hard at me as they went by, and stopping here and there, in whispering groups.
10126{_}86{_}The paymaster told me that one-half of the men's wages was paid to them in tickets for bread--in each case given to the shopkeeper to whom the receiver of the ticket owed most money-- the other half was paid to them in money every Saturday.
10126{_}87{_}Before returning to town I learnt that twenty of the more robust men, who had worked well for their shilling a day in the quarries, had been picked out by order of the Board of Guardians, to be sent to the scene of the late disaster, in Lincolnshire, where employment had been obtained for them, at the rate of 3s.
10126{_}88{_}4d.
10126{_}89{_}per day.
10126{_}90{_}They were to muster at six o'clock next morning to breakfast at the soup kitchen, after which they were to leave town by the seven o'clock train.
10126{_}91{_}I resolved to be up and see them off.
10126{_}92{_}On retiring to bed at the "Old Bull," a good-tempered fellow, known by the name of "Stockings," from the fact of his being "under-boots," promised to waken me by six o'clock; and so I ended the day, after watching "Stockings" write "18" on the soles of my boots, with a lump of chalk.
10126{_}93{_}"Stockings" might as well have kept his bed on Saturday morning.
10126{_}94{_}My room was close to the ancient tower, left standing in the parish churchyard; and, at five o'clock, the beautiful bells of St Marie's struck up, filling my little chamber with that heart-stirring music, which, as somebody has well said, "sounds like a voice from the middle ages."
10126{_}95{_}I could not make out what all this early melody meant; for I had forgotten that it was the Queen's birthday.
10126{_}96{_}The old tower was in full view from my bed, and I lay there a while looking at it, and listening to the bells, and dreaming of Whalley Abbey, and of old features of life in picturesque Blackburnshire, now passed away.
10126{_}97{_}I felt no more inclination for sleep; and when the knock came to my door, I was dressed and ready.
10126{_}98{_}There were more people in the streets than I expected, and the bells were still ringing merrily.
10126{_}99{_}I found the soup kitchen a lively scene.
10126{_}100{_}The twenty men were busy at breakfast, and there was a crowd waiting outside to see them off.
10126{_}101{_}There were several members of the committee in the kitchen, and amongst them the Rev.
10126{_}102{_}Joseph V. Meaney, Catholic priest, went to and fro in cheerful chat.
10126{_}103{_}After breakfast, each man received four pounds of bread and one pound of cheese for the day's consumption.
10126{_}104{_}In addition to this, each man received one shilling; to which a certain active member of the committee added threepence in each case.
10126{_}105{_}Another member of the committee then handed a letter to each of the only three or four out of the twenty who were able to write, desiring each man to write back to the committee,--not all at once, but on different days, after their arrival.
10126{_}106{_}After this, he addressed them in the following words:--"Now, I hope that every man will conduct himself so as to be a credit to himself and an honour to Blackburn.
10126{_}107{_}This work may not prove to be such as you will like, and you must not expect it to be so.
10126{_}108{_}But, do your best; and, if you find that there is any chance of employment for more men of the same class as yourselves, you must write and let us know, so as to relieve the distress of others who are left behind you.
10126{_}109{_}There will be people waiting to meet you before you get to your journey's end; and, I have no doubt, you will meet with every fair encouragement.
10126{_}110{_}One-half of your wages will be paid over to each man there; the other half will be forwarded here, for the benefit of your families, as you all know.
10126{_}111{_}Now go, and do your duty to the best of your power, and you will never regret it.
10126{_}112{_}I wish you all success."
10126{_}113{_}At half-past six the men left the kitchen for the station.
10126{_}114{_}I lingered behind to get a basin of the soup, which I relished mightily.
10126{_}115{_}At the station I found a crowd of wives, children, and friends of those who were going away.
10126{_}116{_}Amongst the rest, Dr Rushton, the vicar of Blackburn, and his lady, had come to see them off.
10126{_}117{_}Here a sweet little young wife stood on the edge of the platform, with a pretty bareheaded child in her arms, crying as if her heart would break.
10126{_}118{_}Her husband now and then spoke a consoling word to her from the carriage window.
10126{_}119{_}They had been noticed sharing their breakfast together at the kitchen.
10126{_}120{_}A little farther on, a poor old Irishwoman was weeping bitterly.
10126{_}121{_}The Rev.
10126{_}122{_}Mr Meaney went up to her, and said, "Now, Mrs Davis, I thought you had more sense than to cry."
10126{_}123{_}"Oh," said a young Irishwoman, standing beside her, "sure, she's losin' her son from her."
10126{_}124{_}"Well," said the clergyman, cheeringly, "it's not your husband, woman."
10126{_}125{_}"Ah, thin," replied the young woman, "sure, it's all she has left of him."
10126{_}126{_}On the door of one compartment of the carriage there was the following written label:--"Fragile, with care."
10126{_}127{_}" How's this, Dennis?"
10126{_}128{_}said the Catholic priest to a young fellow nearest the door; "I suppose it's because you're all Irishmen inside there."
10126{_}129{_}In another compartment the lads kept popping their heads out, one after another, shouting farewells to their relatives and friends, after which they struck up, "There's a good time coming!"
10126{_}130{_}One wag of a fellow suddenly called out to his wife on the platform, "Aw say, Molly, just run for thoose tother breeches o' mine.
10126{_}131{_}They'n come in rarely for weet weather."
10126{_}132{_}One of his companions replied, "Thae knows hoo cannot get 'em, Jack.
10126{_}133{_}Th' pop-shops are noan oppen yet."
10126{_}134{_}One hearty cheer arose as the train started, after which the crowd dribbled away from the platform.
10126{_}135{_}I returned to the soup kitchen, where the wives, children, and mothers of the men who had gone were at breakfast in the inner compartment of the kitchen.
10126{_}136{_}On the outer side of the partition five or six pinched-looking men had straggled in to get their morning meal.
10126{_}137{_}When they had all done but one, who was left reared against the wooden partition finishing his soup, the last of those going away turned round and said, "Sam, theaw'rt noan as tickle abeawt thi mate as thae use't to be."
10126{_}138{_}"Naw," replied the other, "it'll not do to be nice these times, owd mon.
10126{_}139{_}But, thae use't to think thisel' aboon porritch, too, Jone.
10126{_}140{_}Aw'll shake honds wi' tho i' thae's a mind, owd dog."
10126{_}141{_}"Get forrud wi' that stuff, an' say nought," answered Jone.
10126{_}142{_}I left Sam at his soup, and went up into the town.
10126{_}143{_}In the course of the day I sat some hours in the Boardroom, listening to the relief cases; but of this, and other things, I will say more in my next.
10126{_}144{_}CHAPTER II.
10126{_}145{_}A little after ten o'clock on Saturday forenoon, I went into the Boardroom, in the hope of catching there some glimpses of the real state of the poor in Blackburn just now, and I was not disappointed; for amongst the short, sad complainings of those who may always be heard of in such a place, there was many a case presented itself which gave affecting proof of the pressure of the times.
10126{_}146{_}Although it is not here where one must look for the most enduring and unobtrusive of those who suffer; nor for the poor traders, who cannot afford to wear their distress upon their sleeves, so long as things will hold together with them at all; nor for that rare class which is now living upon the savings of past labour--yet, there were many persons, belonging to one or other of these classes, who applied for relief evidently because they had been driven unwillingly to this last bitter haven by a stress of weather which they could not bide any longer.
10126{_}147{_}There was a large attendance of the guardians; and they certainly evinced a strong wish to inquire carefully into each case, and to relieve every case of real need.
10126{_}148{_}The rate of relief given is this (as you will have seen stated by Mr Farnall elsewhere):--"To single able bodied men, 3s.
10126{_}149{_}for three days' work.
10126{_}150{_}To the man who had a wife and two children, 6s.
10126{_}151{_}for six days' work, and he would have 2s.
10126{_}152{_}6d.
10126{_}153{_}added to the 6s., and perhaps a pair of clogs for one of his children.
10126{_}154{_}To a man who had a wife and four children, 10s.
10126{_}155{_}was paid for six days' labour, and in addition 4s., and sometimes 4s.
10126{_}156{_}6d., was given to him, and also bits of clothing and other things which he absolutely wanted."
10126{_}157{_}Sitting at that Board I saw some curious--some painful things.
10126{_}158{_}It was, as one of the Board said to me, "Hard work being there."
10126{_}159{_}In one case, a poor, pale, clean-looking, and almost speechless woman presented herself.
10126{_}160{_}Her thin and sunken eyes, as well as her known circumstances, explained her want sufficiently, and I heard one of the guardians whisper to another, "That's a bad case.
10126{_}161{_}If it wasn't for private charity they'd die of starvation."
10126{_}162{_}"Yes," replied another; "that woman's punished, I can see."
10126{_}163{_}Now and then a case came on in which the guardians were surprised to see a man ask for relief whom everybody had supposed to be in good circumstances.
10126{_}164{_}The first applicant, after I entered the room, was a man apparently under forty years of age, a beerhouse keeper, who had been comparatively well off until lately.
10126{_}165{_}The tide of trouble had whelmed him over.
10126{_}166{_}His children were all factory operatives, and all out of work; and his wife was ill. "What; are you here, John?"
10126{_}167{_}said the chairman to a decent-looking man who stepped up in answer to his name.
10126{_}168{_}The poor fellow blushed with evident pain, and faltered out his story in few and simple words, as if ashamed that anything on earth should have driven him at last to such an extremity as this.
10126{_}169{_}In another case, a clean old decrepid man presented himself.
10126{_}170{_}"What's brought you here, Joseph?"
10126{_}171{_}said the chairman.
10126{_}172{_}"Why; aw've nought to do,--nor nought to tak to."
10126{_}173{_}"What's your daughter, Ellen, doing, Joseph?"
10126{_}174{_}"Hoo's eawt o' wark."
10126{_}175{_}"And what's your wife doing?"
10126{_}176{_}"Hoo's bin bed-fast aboon five year."
10126{_}177{_}The old man was relieved at once; but, as he walked away, he looked hard at his ticket, as if it wasn't exactly the kind of thing; and, turning round, he said, "Couldn't yo let me be a sweeper i'th streets, istid, Mr Eccles?"
10126{_}178{_}A clean old woman came up, with a snow- white nightcap on her head.
10126{_}179{_}"Well, Mary; what do you want?"
10126{_}180{_}"Aw could like yo to gi mo a bit o' summat, Mr Eccles,--for aw need it" "Well, but you've some lodgers, haven't you, Mary?"
10126{_}181{_}"Yigh; aw've three."
10126{_}182{_}"Well; what do they pay you?"
10126{_}183{_}"They pay'n mo nought.
10126{_}184{_}They'n no wark,--an' one connot turn 'em eawt."
10126{_}185{_}This was all quite true.
10126{_}186{_}"Well, but you live with your son; don't you?"
10126{_}187{_}continued the chairman.
10126{_}188{_}"Nay," replied the old woman, "HE lives wi' ME; an' he's eawt o' wark, too.
10126{_}189{_}Aw could like yo to do a bit o' summat for us.
10126{_}190{_}We're hard put to 't."
10126{_}191{_}"Don't you think she would be better in the workhouse?"
10126{_}192{_}said one of the guardians.
10126{_}193{_}"Oh, no," replied another; "don't send th' owd woman there.
10126{_}194{_}Let her keep her own little place together, if she can."
10126{_}195{_}Another old woman presented herself, with a threadbare shawl drawn closely round her gray head.
10126{_}196{_}"Well, Ann," said the chairman, "there's nobody but yourself and your John, is there?"
10126{_}197{_}"Nawe."
10126{_}198{_}"What age are you?"
10126{_}199{_}"Aw'm seventy."
10126{_}200{_}"Seventy!"
10126{_}201{_}"Aye, I am."
10126{_}202{_}"Well, and what age is your John?"
10126{_}203{_}"He's gooin' i' seventy-four."
10126{_}204{_}"Where is he, Ann ?"
10126{_}205{_}"Well, aw laft him deawn i' th' street yon; gettin' a load o' coals in."
10126{_}206{_}There was a murmur of approbation around the Board; and the old woman was sent away relieved and thankful.
10126{_}207{_}There were many other affecting cases of genuine distress arising from the present temporary severity of the times.
10126{_}208{_}Several applicants were refused relief on its being proved that they were already in receipt of considerably more income than the usual amount allowed by the Board to those who have nothing to depend upon.
10126{_}209{_}Of course there are always some who, having lost that fine edge of feeling to which this kind of relief is revolting, are not unwilling to live idly upon the rates as much and as long as possible at any time, and who will even descend to pitiful schemes to wring from this source whatever miserable income they can get.
10126{_}210{_}There are some, even, with whom this state of mind seems almost hereditary; and these will not be slow to take advantage of the present state of affairs.
10126{_}211{_}Such cases, however, are not numerous among the people of Lancashire.
10126{_}212{_}It was a curious thing to see the different demeanours and appearances of the applicants--curious to hear the little stories of their different troubles.
10126{_}213{_}There were three or four women whose husbands were away in the militia; others whose husbands had wandered away in search of work weeks ago, and had never been heard of, since.
10126{_}214{_}There were a few very fine, intelligent countenances among them.
10126{_}215{_}There were many of all ages, clean in person, and bashful in manner, with their poor clothing put into the tidiest possible trim; others were dirty, and sluttish, and noisy of speech, as in the case of one woman, who, after receiving her ticket for relief, partly in money and partly in kind, whipped a pair of worn clogs from under her shawl, and cried out, "Aw mun ha' some clogs afore aw go, too; look at thoose!
10126{_}216{_}They're a shame to be sin!"
10126{_}217{_}Clogs were freely given; and, in several cases, they were all that were asked for.
10126{_}218{_}In three or four instances, the applicants said, after receiving other relief, "Aw wish yo'd gi' me a pair o' clogs, Mr Eccles.
10126{_}219{_}Aw've had to borrow these to come in."
10126{_}220{_}One woman pleaded hard for two pair, saying, "Yon chylt's bar-fuut; an' HE'S witchod (wet-shod), an' as ill as he con be."
10126{_}221{_}"Who's witchod?"
10126{_}222{_}asked the chairman.
10126{_}223{_}"My husban' is," replied the woman; "an' he connot ston it just neaw, yo mun let HIM have a pair iv yo con."
10126{_}224{_}"Give her two pairs of clogs," said the chairman.
10126{_}225{_}Another woman took her clog off, and held it up, saying,  "Look at that.
10126{_}226{_}We're o' walkin' o'th floor; an' smoor't wi' cowds."
10126{_}227{_}One decent-looking old body, with a starved face, applied.
10126{_}228{_}The chairman said, "Why, what's your son doing now?
10126{_}229{_}Has he catched no rabbits lately?"
10126{_}230{_}"Nay, aw dunnot know 'at he does.
10126{_}231{_}Aw get nought; an' it's ME at wants summat, Mr Eccles," replied the old woman, in a tremulous tone, with the water rising in her eyes.
10126{_}232{_}"Well, come; we mustn't punish th' owd woman for her son," said one of the guardians.
10126{_}233{_}Various forms of the feebleness of age appeared before the Board that day.
10126{_}234{_}"What's your son John getting, Mary?"
10126{_}235{_}said the chairman to one old woman.
10126{_}236{_}"Whor?"
10126{_}237{_}replied she.
10126{_}238{_}"What's your son John getting?"
10126{_}239{_}The old woman put her hand up to her ear, and answered,  "Aw'm rayther deaf.
10126{_}240{_}What say'n yo?"
10126{_}241{_}It turned out that her son was taken ill, and they were relieved.
10126{_}242{_}In the course of inquiries I found that the working people of Blackburn, as elsewhere in Lancashire, nickname their workshops as well as themselves.
10126{_}243{_}The chairman asked a girl where she worked at last, and the girl replied, "At th' 'Puff-an'-dart.'"
10126{_}244{_}"And what made you leave there?"
10126{_}245{_}"Whau, they were woven up."
10126{_}246{_}One poor, pale fellow, a widower, said he had "worched" a bit at "Bang-the-nation," till he was taken ill, and then they had "shopped his place," that is, they had given his work to somebody else.
10126{_}247{_}Another, when asked where he had been working, replied, "At Se'nacre Bruck (Seven-acre Brook), wheer th' wild monkey were catched."
10126{_}248{_}It seems that an ourang-outang which once escaped from some travelling menagerie, was re-taken at this place.
10126{_}249{_}I sat until the last application had been disposed of, which was about half-past two in the afternoon.
10126{_}250{_}The business had taken up nearly four hours and a half.
10126{_}251{_}I had a good deal of conversation with people who were intimately acquainted with the town and its people; and I was informed that, in spite of the struggle for existence which is now going on, and not unlikely to continue for some time, there are things happening amongst the working people there, which do not seem wise, under existing circumstances.
10126{_}252{_}The people are much better informed now than they were twenty years ago; but, still, something of the old blindness lingers amongst them, here and there.
10126{_}253{_}For instance, at one mill, in Blackburn, where the operatives were receiving 11s.
10126{_}254{_}a week for two looms, the proprietor offered to give his workpeople three looms each, with a guarantee for constant employment until the end of next August, if they would accept one and a quarter pence less for the weaving of each piece.
10126{_}255{_}This offer, if taken, would have raised their wages to an average of 14s.
10126{_}256{_}6d.
10126{_}257{_}a week.
10126{_}258{_}It was declined, however, and they are now working, as before, only on two looms each, with uncertainty of employment, at lls.
10126{_}259{_}a week.
10126{_}260{_}Perhaps it is too much to expect that such things should die out all at once.
10126{_}261{_}But I heard also that the bricklayers' labourers at Blackburn struck work last week for an advance of wages from 3s.
10126{_}262{_}6d.
10126{_}263{_}a day to 4s.
10126{_}264{_}a day.
10126{_}265{_}This seems very untimely, to say the least of it.
10126{_}266{_}Apart from these things, there is, amongst all classes, a kind of cheery faith in the return of good times, although nobody can see what they may have to go through yet, before the clouds break.
10126{_}267{_}It is a fact that there are more than forty new places ready, or nearly ready, for starting, in and about Blackburn, when trade revives.
10126{_}268{_}After dinner, I walked down Darwen Street.
10126{_}269{_}Stopping to look at a music-seller's window, a rough-looking fellow, bareheaded and without coat, came sauntering across the road from a shop opposite.
10126{_}270{_}As he came near he shouted out, "Nea then Heaw go!"
10126{_}271{_}I turned round; and, seeing that I was a stranger, he said, "Oh; aw thought it had bin another chap."
10126{_}272{_}"Well," said I, "heaw are yo gettin' on, these times?"
10126{_}273{_}"Divulish ill," replied he.
10126{_}274{_}"Th' little maisters are runnin' a bit, some three, some four days.
10126{_}275{_}T'other are stopt o' together, welly.
10126{_}276{_}.
10126{_}277{_}.
10126{_}278{_}.
10126{_}279{_}It's thin pikein' for poor folk just neaw.
10126{_}280{_}But th' shopkeepers an' th' ale-heawses are in for it as ill as ony mak.
10126{_}281{_}There'll be crashin' amung some on 'em afore lung."
10126{_}282{_}After this, I spent a few minutes in the market-place, which was "slacker" than usual, as might be expected, for, as the Scotch proverb says, "Sillerless folk gang fast through the market."
10126{_}283{_}Later on, I went up to Bank Top, on the eastern edge of the town, where many factory operatives reside.
10126{_}284{_}Of course, there is not any special quarter where they are clustered in such a manner as to show their condition as a whole.
10126{_}285{_}They are scattered all round the town, living as near as possible to the mills in which they are employed.
10126{_}286{_}Here I talked with some of the small shopkeepers, and found them all more or less troubled with the same complaint.
10126{_}287{_}One owner of a provision shop said to me, "Wi'n a deeal o' brass owin'; but it's mostly owin' by folk at'll pay sometime.
10126{_}288{_}An' then, th' part on 'em are doin' a bit yo known; an' they bring'n their trifle o' ready brass to us; an' so we're trailin' on.
10126{_}289{_}But folk han to trust us a bit for their stuff, dunnot yo see,--or else it would be 'Wo-up!'
10126{_}290{_}soon."
10126{_}291{_}I heard of one beerhouse, the owner of which had only drawn ls.
10126{_}292{_}6d.
10126{_}293{_}during a whole week.
10126{_}294{_}His children were all factory operatives, and all out of work.
10126{_}295{_}They were very badly off, and would have been very glad of a few soup tickets; but, as the man said, "Who'd believe me if aw were to go an' ax for relief?"
10126{_}296{_}I was told of two young fellows, unemployed factory hands, meeting one day, when one said to the other, "Thae favvurs hungry, Jone."
10126{_}297{_}"Nay, aw's do yet, for that," replied Jone.
10126{_}298{_}"Well," continued the other; "keep thi heart eawt of thi clogs, iv thi breeches dun eawt-thrive thi carcass a bit, owd lad."
10126{_}299{_}"Aye," said Jone, "but what mun I do when my clogs gi'n way?"
10126{_}300{_}"Whaw, thae mun go to th' Guardians; they'n gi tho a pair in a minute."
10126{_}301{_}"Nay, by __," replied Jone, "aw'll dee furst!"
10126{_}302{_}In the evening, I ran down to the beautiful suburb called Pleasington, in the hope of meeting a friend of mine there; not finding him, I came away by the eight o'clock train.
10126{_}303{_}The evening was splendid, and it was cheering to see the old bounty of nature gushing forth again in such unusual profusion and beauty, as if in pitiful charity for the troubles of mankind.
10126{_}304{_}I never saw the country look so rich in its spring robes as it does now.
10126{_}305{_}CHAPTER III.
10126{_}306{_}AMONG THE PRESTON OPERATIVES.
10126{_}307{_}Proud Preston, or Priest-town, on the banks of the beautiful Ribble, is a place of many quaint customs, and of great historic fame.
10126{_}308{_}Its character for pride is said to come from the fact of its having been, in the old time, a favourite residence of the local nobles and gentry, and of many penniless folk with long pedigrees.
10126{_}309{_}It was here that Richard Arkwright shaved chins at a halfpenny each, in the meantime working out his bold and ingenious schemes, with patient faith in their ultimate success.
10126{_}310{_}It was here, too, that the teetotal movement first began, with Anderson for its rhyme-smith.
10126{_}311{_}Preston has had its full share of the changeful fortunes of England, and, like our motherland, it has risen strongly out of them all.
10126{_}312{_}War's mad havoc has swept over it in many a troubled period of our history.
10126{_}313{_}Plague, pestilence, and famine have afflicted it sorely; and it has suffered from trade riots, "plug-drawings," panics, and strikes of most disastrous kinds.
10126{_}314{_}Proud Preston--the town of the Stanleys and the Hoghtons, and of "many a crest that is famous in story"--the town where silly King Jamie disported himself a little, with his knights and nobles, during the time of his ruinous visit to Hoghton Tower,--Proud Preston has seen many a black day.
10126{_}315{_}But, from the time when Roman sentinels kept watch and ward in their old camp at Walton, down by the Ribble side, it has never seen so much wealth and so much bitter poverty together as now.
10126{_}316{_}The streets do not show this poverty; but it is there.
10126{_}317{_}Looking from Avenham Walks, that glorious landscape smiles in all the splendour of a rich spring- tide.
10126{_}318{_}In those walks the nursemaids and children, and dainty folk, are wandering as usual airing their curls in the fresh breeze; and only now and then a workless operative trails by with chastened look.
10126{_}319{_}The wail of sorrow is not heard in Preston market-place; but destitution may be found almost anywhere there just now, cowering in squalid corners, within a few yards of plenty--as I have seen it many a time this week.
10126{_}320{_}The courts and alleys behind even some of the main streets swarm with people who have hardly a whole nail left to scratch themselves with.
10126{_}321{_}Before attempting to tell something of what I saw whilst wandering amongst the poor operatives of Preston, I will say at once, that I do not intend to meddle with statistics.
10126{_}322{_}They have been carefully gathered, and often given elsewhere, and there is no need for me to repeat them.
10126{_}323{_}But, apart from these, the theme is endless, and full of painful interest.
10126{_}324{_}I hear on all hands that there is hardly any town in Lancashire suffering so much as Preston.
10126{_}325{_}The reason why the stroke has fallen so heavily here, lies in the nature of the trade.
10126{_}326{_}In the first place, Preston is almost purely a cotton town.
10126{_}327{_}There are two or three flax mills, and two or three ironworks, of no great extent; but, upon the whole, there is hardly any variety of employment there to lighten the disaster which has befallen its one absorbing occupation.
10126{_}328{_}There is comparatively little weaving in Preston; it is a town mostly engaged in spinning.
10126{_}329{_}The cotton used there is nearly all what is called "Middling American," the very kind which is now most scarce and dear.
10126{_}330{_}The yarns of Preston are known by the name of "Blackburn Counts."
10126{_}331{_}They range from 28's up to 60's, and they enter largely into the manufacture of goods for the India market.
10126{_}332{_}These things partly explain why Preston is more deeply overshadowed by the particular gloom of the times than many other places in Lancashire.
10126{_}333{_}About half-past nine on Tuesday morning last, I set out with an old acquaintance to call upon a certain member of the Relief Committee, in George's Ward.
10126{_}334{_}He is the manager of a cotton mill in that quarter, and he is well known and much respected among the working people.
10126{_}335{_}When we entered the mill-yard, all was quiet there, and the factory was still and silent.
10126{_}336{_}But through the office window we could see the man we wanted.
10126{_}337{_}He was accompanied by one of the proprietors of the mill, turning over the relief books of the ward.
10126{_}338{_}I soon found that he had a strong sense of humour, as well as a heart welling over with tenderness.
10126{_}339{_}He pointed to some of the cases in his books.
10126{_}340{_}The first was that of an old man, an overlooker of a cotton mill.
10126{_}341{_}His family was thirteen in number; three of the children were under ten years of age; seven of the rest were factory operatives; but the whole family had been out of work for several months.
10126{_}342{_}When in full employment the joint earnings of the family amounted to 80s.
10126{_}343{_}a week; but, after struggling on in the hope of better times, and exhausting the savings of past labour, they had been brought down to the receipt of charity at last, and for sixteen weeks gone by the whole thirteen had been living upon 6s.
10126{_}344{_}a week from the relief fund.
10126{_}345{_}They had no other resource.
10126{_}346{_}I went to see them at their own house afterwards, and it certainly was a pattern of cleanliness, with the little household gods there still.
10126{_}347{_}Seeing that house, a stranger would never dream that the family was living on an average income of less than sixpence a head per week.
10126{_}348{_}But I know how hard some decent folk will struggle with the bitterest poverty before they will give in to it.
10126{_}349{_}The old man came in whilst I was there.
10126{_}350{_}He sat down in one corner, quietly tinkering away at something he had in his hands.
10126{_}351{_}His old corduroy trousers were well patched, and just new washed.
10126{_}352{_}He had very little to say to us, except that "He could like to get summat to do; for he wur tired o' walkin' abeawt."
10126{_}353{_}Another case was that of a poor widow woman, with five young children.
10126{_}354{_}This family had been driven from house to house, by increasing necessity, till they had sunk at last into a dingy little hovel, up a dark court, in one of the poorest parts of the town, where they huddled together about a fireless grate to keep one another warm.
10126{_}355{_}They had nothing left of the wreck of their home but two rickety chairs, and a little deal table reared against the wall, because one of the legs was gone.
10126{_}356{_}In this miserable hole-- which I saw afterwards--her husband died of sheer starvation, as was declared by the jury on the inquest.
10126{_}357{_}The dark, damp hovel where they had crept to was scarcely four yards square; and the poor woman pointed to one corner of the floor, saying, "He dee'd i' that nook."
10126{_}358{_}He died there, with nothing to lie upon but the ground, and nothing to cover him, in that fireless hovel.
10126{_}359{_}His wife and children crept about him, there, to watch him die; and to keep him as warm as they could.
10126{_}360{_}When the relief committee first found this family out, the entire clothing of the family of seven persons weighed eight pounds, and sold for fivepence, as rags.
10126{_}361{_}I saw the family afterwards, at their poor place; and will say more about them hereafter.
10126{_}362{_}He told me of many other cases of a similar kind.
10126{_}363{_}But, after agreeing to a time when we should visit them personally, we set out together to see the "Stone Yard," where there are many factory hands at work under the Board of Guardians.
10126{_}364{_}The "Stone Yard" is close by the Preston and Lancaster Canal.
10126{_}365{_}Here there are from one hundred and seventy to one hundred and eighty, principally young men, employed in breaking, weighing, and wheeling stone, for road mending.
10126{_}366{_}The stones are of a hard kind of blue boulder, gathered from the land between Kendal and Lancaster.
10126{_}367{_}The "Labour Master" told me that there were thousands of tons of these boulders upon the land between Kendal and Lancaster.
10126{_}368{_}A great deal of them are brought from a place called "Tewhitt Field," about seven mile on "t' other side o' Lancaster."
10126{_}369{_}At the "Stone Yard" it is all piece-work, and the men can come and go when they like.
10126{_}370{_}As one of the Guardians told me, "They can oather sit an' break 'em, or kneel an' break 'em, or lie deawn to it, iv they'n a mind."
10126{_}371{_}The men can choose whether they will fill three tons of the broken stone, and wheel it to the central heap, for a shilling, or break one ton for a shilling.
10126{_}372{_}The persons employed here are mostly "lads an' leet- timber't chaps."
10126{_}373{_}The stronger men are sent to work upon Preston Moor.
10126{_}374{_}There are great varieties of health and strength amongst them.
10126{_}375{_}"Beside," as the Labour Master said, "yo'd hardly believe what a difference there it i'th wark o' two men wortchin' at the same heap, sometimes.
10126{_}376{_}There's a great deal i'th breaker, neaw; some on 'em's more artful nor others.
10126{_}377{_}They finden out that they can break 'em as fast again at after they'n getten to th' wick i'th inside.
10126{_}378{_}I have known an' odd un or two, here, that could break four ton a day,--an' many that couldn't break one,--but then, yo' know, th' men can only do accordin' to their ability.
10126{_}379{_}There is these differences, and there always will be."
10126{_}380{_}As we stood talking together, one of my friends said that he wished "Radical Jack" had been there.
10126{_}381{_}The latter gentleman is one of the guardians of the poor, and superintendent of the "Stone Yard."
10126{_}382{_}The men are naturally jealous of misrepresentation; and, the other day, as "Radical Jack" was describing the working of the yard to a gentleman who had come to look at the scene, some of the men overheard his words, and, misconceiving their meaning, gathered around the superintendent, clamorously protesting against what he had been saying.
10126{_}383{_}"He's lying!"
10126{_}384{_}said one.
10126{_}385{_}"Look at these honds!"
10126{_}386{_}cried another; "Wi'n they ever be fit to go to th' factory wi' again?"
10126{_}387{_}Others turned up the soles of their battered shoon, to show their cut and stockingless feet.
10126{_}388{_}They were pacified at last; but, after the superintendent had gone away, some of the men said much and more, and "if ever he towd ony moor lies abeawt 'em, they'd fling him into th' cut."
10126{_}389{_}The "Labour Master" told me there was a large wood shed for the men to shelter in when rain came on.
10126{_}390{_}As we were conversing, one of my friends exclaimed, "He's here now!"
10126{_}391{_}"Who's here?"
10126{_}392{_}"Radical Jack."
10126{_}393{_}The superintendent was coming down the road.
10126{_}394{_}He told me some interesting things, which I will return to on another occasion.
10126{_}395{_}But our time was up.
10126{_}396{_}We had other places to see.
10126{_}397{_}As we came away, three old Irishwomen leaned against the wall at the corner of the yard, watching the men at work inside.
10126{_}398{_}One of them was saying, "Thim guardians is the awfullest set o' min in the world!
10126{_}399{_}A man had better be transpoorted than come under 'em.
10126{_}400{_}An' thin, they'll try you, an' try you, as if you was goin' to be hanged."
10126{_}401{_}The poor old soul had evidently only a narrow view of the necessities and difficulties which beset the labours of the Board of Guardians at a time like this.
10126{_}402{_}On our way back to town one of my friends told me that he "had met a sexton the day before, and had asked him how trade was with him.
10126{_}403{_}The sexton replied that it was "Varra bad--nowt doin', hardly."
10126{_}404{_}"Well, how's that?"
10126{_}405{_}asked the other.
10126{_}406{_}"Well, thae sees," answered the sexton, "Poverty seldom dees.
10126{_}407{_}There's far more kilt wi' o'er-heytin' an' o'er-drinkin' nor there is wi' bein' pinched."
10126{_}408{_}CHAPTER IV.
10126{_}409{_}Leaving the "Stone Yard," to fulfil an engagement in another part of the town, we agreed to call upon three or four poor folk, who lived by the way; and I don't know that I could do better than say something about what I saw of them.
10126{_}410{_}As we walked along, one of my companions told me of an incident which happened to one of the visitors in another ward, a few days before.
10126{_}411{_}In the course of his round, this visitor called upon a certain destitute family which was under his care, and he found the husband sitting alone in the house, pale and silent.
10126{_}412{_}His wife had been "brought to bed" two or three days before; and the visitor inquired how she was getting on.
10126{_}413{_}"Hoo's very ill," said the husband.
10126{_}414{_}"And the child," continued the visitor, "how is it?"
10126{_}415{_}"It's deeod," replied the man; "it dee'd yesterday."
10126{_}416{_}He then rose, and walked slowly into the next room, returning with a basket in his hands, in which the dead child was decently laid out.
10126{_}417{_}"That's o' that's laft on it neaw," said the poor fellow.
10126{_}418{_}Then, putting the basket upon the floor, he sat down in front of it, with his head between his hands, looking silently at the corpse.
10126{_}419{_}Such things as these were the theme of our conversation as we went along, and I found afterwards that every visitor whom it was my privilege to meet, had some special story of distress to relate, which came within his own appointed range of action.
10126{_}420{_}In my first flying visit to that great melancholy field, I could only glean such things as lay nearest to my hand, just then; but wherever I went, I heard and saw things which touchingly testify what noble stuff the working population of Lancashire, as a whole, is made of.
10126{_}421{_}One of the first cases we called upon, after leaving the "Stone Yard," was that of a family of ten--man and wife, and eight children.
10126{_}422{_}Four of the children were under ten years of age,--five were capable of working; and, when the working part of the family was in full employment, their joint earnings amounted to 61s.
10126{_}423{_}per week.
10126{_}424{_}But, in this case, the mother's habitual ill-health had been a great expense in the household for several years.
10126{_}425{_}This family belonged to a class of operatives--a much larger class than people unacquainted with the factory districts are likely to suppose--a class of operatives which will struggle, in a dumb, enduring way, to the death, sometimes, before they will sacrifice that "immediate jewel of their souls"-- their old independence, and will keep up a decent appearance to the very last.
10126{_}426{_}These suffer more than the rest; for, in addition to the pains of bitter starvation, they feel a loss which is more afflicting to them even than the loss of food and furniture ; and their sufferings are less heard of than the rest, because they do not like to complain.
10126{_}427{_}This family of ten persons had been living, during the last nine weeks, upon relief amounting to 5s.
10126{_}428{_}a week.
10126{_}429{_}When we called, the mother and one or two of her daughters were busy in the next room, washing their poor bits of well-kept clothing.
10126{_}430{_}The daughters kept out of sight, as if ashamed.
10126{_}431{_}It was a good kind of cottage, in a clean street, called "Maudland Bank," and the whole place had a tidy, sweet look, though it was washing-day.
10126{_}432{_}The mother told me that she had been severely afflicted with seven successive attacks of inflammation, and yet, in spite of her long-continued ill-health, and in spite of the iron teeth of poverty which had been gnawing at them so long, for the first time, I have rarely seen a more frank and cheerful countenance than that thin matron's, as she stood there, wringing her clothes, and telling her little story.
10126{_}433{_}The house they lived in belonged to their late employer, whose mill stopped some time ago.
10126{_}434{_}We asked her how they managed to pay the rent, and she said, "Why, we dunnot pay it; we cannot pay it, an' he doesn't push us for it.
10126{_}435{_}Aw guess he knows he'll get it sometime.
10126{_}436{_}But we owe'd a deal o' brass beside that.
10126{_}437{_}Just look at this shop book.
10126{_}438{_}Aw'm noan freetend ov onybody seein' my acceawnts.
10126{_}439{_}An' then, there's a great lot o' doctor's-bills i' that pot, theer.
10126{_}440{_}Thoose are o' for me.
10126{_}441{_}There'll ha' to be some wark done afore things can be fotched up again.
10126{_}442{_}.
10126{_}443{_}.
10126{_}444{_}.
10126{_}445{_}Eh; aw'll tell yo what, William, (this was addressed to the visitor,) it went ill again th' grain wi' my husband to goo afore th' Board.
10126{_}446{_}An' when he did goo, he wouldn't say so mich. Yo known, folk doesn't like brastin' off abeawt theirsel' o' at once, at a shop like that.
10126{_}447{_}.
10126{_}448{_}.
10126{_}449{_}.
10126{_}450{_}Aw think sometimes it's very weel that four ov eawrs are i' heaven,--we'n sich hard tewin' (toiling), to poo through wi' tother, just neaw.
10126{_}451{_}But, aw guess it'll not last for ever."
10126{_}452{_}As we came away, talking of the reluctance shown by the better sort of working people to ask for relief, or even sometimes to accept it when offered to them, until thoroughly starved to it, I was told of a visitor calling upon a poor woman in another ward; no application had been made for relief, but some kind neighbour had told the committee that the woman and her husband were "ill off."
10126{_}453{_}The visitor, finding that they were perishing for want, offered the woman some relief tickets for food; but the poor soul began to cry, and said; "Eh, aw dar not touch 'em; my husban' would sauce me so!
10126{_}454{_}Aw dar not take 'em; aw should never yer the last on't!"
10126{_}455{_}When we got to the lower end of Hope Street, my guide stopped suddenly, and said, "Oh, this is close to where that woman lives whose husband died of starvation.
10126{_}456{_}"Leading a few yards up the by-street, he turned into a low, narrow entry, very dark and damp.
10126{_}457{_}Two turns more brought us to a dirty, pent-up corner, where a low door stood open.
10126{_}458{_}We entered there.
10126{_}459{_}It was a cold, gloomy-looking little hovel.
10126{_}460{_}In my allusion to the place last week I said it was "scarcely four yards square."
10126{_}461{_}It is not more than three yards square.
10126{_}462{_}There was no fire in the little rusty grate.
10126{_}463{_}The day was sunny, but no sunshine could ever reach that nook, nor any fresh breezes disturb the pestilent vapours that harboured there, festering in the sluggish gloom.
10126{_}464{_}In one corner of the place a little worn and broken stair led up to a room of the same size above, where, I was told, there was now some straw for the family to sleep upon.
10126{_}465{_}But the only furniture in the house, of any kind, was two rickety chairs and a little broken deal table, reared against the stairs, because one leg was gone.
10126{_}466{_}A quiet- looking, thin woman, seemingly about fifty years of age, sat there, when we went in.
10126{_}467{_}She told us that she had buried five of her children, and that she had six yet alive, all living with her in that poor place.
10126{_}468{_}They had no work, no income whatever, save what came from the Relief Committee.
10126{_}469{_}Five of the children were playing in and out, bare-footed, and, like the mother, miserably clad; but they seemed quite unconscious that anything ailed them.
10126{_}470{_}I never saw finer children anywhere.
10126{_}471{_}The eldest girl, about fourteen, came in whilst we were there, and she leaned herself bashfully against the wall for a minute or two, and then slunk slyly out again, as if ashamed of our presence.
10126{_}472{_}The poor widow pointed to the cold corner where her husband died lately.
10126{_}473{_}She said that "his name was Tim Pedder.
10126{_}474{_}His fadder name was Timothy, an' his mudder name was Mary.
10126{_}475{_}He was a driver (a driver of boat-horses on the canal); but he had bin oot o' wark a lang time afore he dee'd."
10126{_}476{_}I found in this case, as in some others, that the poor body had not much to say about her distress; but she did not need to say much.
10126{_}477{_}My guide told me that when he first called upon the family, in the depth of last winter, he found the children all clinging round about their mother in the cold hovel, trying in that way to keep one another warm.
10126{_}478{_}The time for my next appointment was now hard on, and we hurried towards the shop in Fishergate, kept by the gentleman I had promised to meet.
10126{_}479{_}He is an active member of the Relief Committee, and a visitor in George's ward.
10126{_}480{_}We found him in.
10126{_}481{_}He had just returned from the "Cheese Fair," at Lancaster.
10126{_}482{_}My purpose was to find out what time on the morrow we could go together to see some of the cases he was best acquainted with.
10126{_}483{_}But, as the evening was not far spent, he proposed that we should go at once to see a few of those which were nearest.
10126{_}484{_}We set out together to Walker's Court, in Friargate.
10126{_}485{_}The first place we entered was at the top of the little narrow court.
10126{_}486{_}There we found a good-tempered Irish-woman sitting without fire, in her feverish hovel.
10126{_}487{_}"Well, missis," said the visitor, "how is your husband getting on?"
10126{_}488{_}"Ah, well, now, Mr. T----," replied she, "you know, he's only a delicate little man, an' a tailor; an' he wint to work on the moor, an' he couldn't stand it.
10126{_}489{_}Sure, it was draggin' the bare life out of him.
10126{_}490{_}So, he says to me, one morning, "Catharine," says he, "I'll lave off this a little while, till I see will I be able to get a job o' work at my own trade; an' maybe God will rise up some thin' to put a dud o' clothes on us all, an' help us to pull through till the black time is over us."
10126{_}491{_}So, I told him to try his luck, any way; for he was killin' himself entirely on the moor.
10126{_}492{_}An' so he did try; for there's not an idle bone in that same boy's skin.
10126{_}493{_}But, see this, now; there's nothin' in the world to be had to do just now--an' a dale too many waitin' to do it--so all he got by the change was losin' his work on the moor.
10126{_}494{_}There is himself, an' me, an' the seven childer.
10126{_}495{_}Five o' the childer is under tin year old.
10126{_}496{_}We are all naked; an' the house is bare; an' our health is gone wi' the want o' mate.
10126{_}497{_}Sure it wasn't in the likes o' this we wor livin' when times was good."
10126{_}498{_}Three of the youngest children were playing about on the floor.
10126{_}499{_}"That's a very fine lad," said I, pointing to one of them.
10126{_}500{_}The little fellow blushed, and smiled, and then became very still and attentive.
10126{_}501{_}"Ah, thin," said his mother, "that villain's the boy for tuckin' up soup!
10126{_}502{_}The Lord be about him, an' save him alive to me,--the crayter !
10126{_}503{_}.
10126{_}504{_}.
10126{_}505{_}.
10126{_}506{_}An' there's little curly there,-- the rogue!
10126{_}507{_}Sure he'll take as much soup as any wan o' them.
10126{_}508{_}Maybe he wouldn't laugh to see a big bowl forninst him this day."
10126{_}509{_}"It's very well they have such good spirits," said the visitor.
10126{_}510{_}"So it is," replies the woman, "so it is, for God knows it's little else they have to keep them warm thim bad times."
10126{_}511{_}CHAPTER V.    The next house we called at in Walker's Court was much like the first in appearance--very little left but the walls, and that little, such as none but the neediest would pick up, if it was thrown out to the streets.
10126{_}512{_}The only person in the place was a pale, crippled woman; her sick head, lapped in a poor white clout, swayed languidly to and fro.
10126{_}513{_}Besides being a cripple, she had been ill six years, and now her husband, also, was taken ill.
10126{_}514{_}He had just crept off to fetch medicine for the two.
10126{_}515{_}We did not stop here long.
10126{_}516{_}The hand of the Ancient Master was visible in that pallid face; those sunken eyes, so full of deathly langour, seemed to be wandering about in dim, flickering gazes, upon the confines of an unknown world.
10126{_}517{_}I think that woman will soon be "where the weary are at rest."
10126{_}518{_}As we came out, she said, slowly, and in broken, painful utterances, that "she hoped the Lord would open the heavens for those who had helped them."
10126{_}519{_}A little lower down the court, we peeped in at two other doorways.
10126{_}520{_}The people were well known to my companion, who has the charge of visiting this part of the ward.
10126{_}521{_}Leaning against the door-cheek of one of these dim, unwholesome hovels, he said, "Well, missis; how are you getting on?"
10126{_}522{_}There was a tall, thin woman inside.
10126{_}523{_}She seemed to be far gone in some exhausting illness.
10126{_}524{_}With slow difficulty she rose to her feet, and, setting her hands to her sides, gasped out, "My coals are done."
10126{_}525{_}He made a note, and said, I'll send you some more."
10126{_}526{_}Her other wants were regularly seen to on a certain day every week.
10126{_}527{_}Ours was an accidental visit.
10126{_}528{_}We now turned up to another nook of the court, where my companion told me there was a very bad case.
10126{_}529{_}He found the door fast.
10126{_}530{_}We looked through the window into that miserable man- nest.
10126{_}531{_}It was cold, gloomy, and bare.
10126{_}532{_}As Corrigan says, in the "Colleen Bawn," "There was nobody in--but the fire--and that was gone out."
10126{_}533{_}As we came away, a stalwart Irishman met us at a turn of the court, and said to my companion, "Sure, ye didn't visit this house."
10126{_}534{_}" Not to-day;" replied the visitor.
10126{_}535{_}"I'll come and see you at the usual time."
10126{_}536{_}The people in this house were not so badly off as some others.
10126{_}537{_}We came down the steps of the court into the fresher air of Friargate again.
10126{_}538{_}Our next walk was to Heatley Street.
10126{_}539{_}As we passed by a cluster of starved loungers, we overheard one of them saying to another, "Sitho, yon's th' soup-maister, gooin' a-seein' somebry."
10126{_}540{_}Our time was getting short, so we only called at one house in Heatley Street, where there was a family of eleven--a decent family, a well-kept and orderly household, though now stript almost to the bare ground of all worldly possession, sold, bitterly, piecemeal, to help to keep the bare life together, as sweetly as possible, till better days.
10126{_}541{_}The eldest son is twenty-seven years of age.
10126{_}542{_}The whole family has been out of work for the last seventeen weeks, and before that, they had been working only short time for seven months.
10126{_}543{_}For thirteen weeks they had lived upon less than one shilling a head per week, and I am not sure that they did not pay the rent out of that; and now the income of the whole eleven is under 16s., with rent to pay.
10126{_}544{_}In this house they hold weekly prayer-meetings.
10126{_}545{_}Thin picking--one shilling a week, or less--for all expenses, for one person.
10126{_}546{_}It is easier to write about it than to feel what it means, unless one has tried it for three or four months.
10126{_}547{_}Just round the corner from Heatley Street, we stopped at the open door of a very little cottage.
10126{_}548{_}A good-looking young Irishwoman sat there, upon a three- legged stool, suckling her child.
10126{_}549{_}She was clean; and had an intelligent look.
10126{_}550{_}"Let's see, missis," said the visitor, "what do you pay for this nook?"
10126{_}551{_}"We pay eighteenpence a week--and they WILL have it--my word."
10126{_}552{_}"Well, an' what income have you now?"
10126{_}553{_}"We have eighteenpence a head in the week, an' the rent to pay out o' that, or else they'll turn us out."
10126{_}554{_}Of course, the visitor knew that this was true; but he wanted me to hear the people speak for themselves.
10126{_}555{_}"Let's see, Missis Burns, your husband's name is Patrick, isn't it?"
10126{_}556{_}" Yes, sir; Patrick Burns."
10126{_}557{_}"What!
10126{_}558{_}Patrick Burns, the famous foot- racer?"
10126{_}559{_}The little woman smiled bashfully, and replied, "Yes, sir; I suppose it is."
10126{_}560{_}With respect to what the woman said about having to pay her rent or turn out, I may remark, in passing, that I have not hitherto met with an instance in which any millowner, or wealthy man, having cottage property, has pressed the unemployed poor for rent.
10126{_}561{_}But it is well to remember that there is a great amount of cottage property in Preston, as in other manufacturing towns, which belongs to the more provident class of working men.
10126{_}562{_}These working men, now hard pressed by the general distress, have been compelled to fall back upon their little rentals, clinging to them as their last independent means of existence.
10126{_}563{_}They are compelled to this, for, if they cannot get work, they cannot get anything else, having property.
10126{_}564{_}These are becoming fewer, however, from day to day.
10126{_}565{_}The poorest are hanging a good deal upon those a little less poor than themselves; and every link in the lengthening chain of neediness is helping to pull down the one immediately above it.
10126{_}566{_}There is, also, a considerable amount of cottage property in Preston, belonging to building societies, which have enough to do to hold their own just now.
10126{_}567{_}And then there is always some cottage property in the hands of agents.
10126{_}568{_}Leaving Heatley Street, we went to a place called "Seed's Yard."
10126{_}569{_}Here we called upon a clean old stately widow, with a calm, sad face.
10126{_}570{_}She had been long known, and highly respected, in a good street, not far off, where she had lived for twenty-four years, in fair circumstances, until lately.
10126{_}571{_}She had always owned a good houseful of furniture; but, after making bitter meals upon the gradual wreck of it, she had been compelled to break up that house, and retire with her five children to lodge with a lone widow in this little cot, not over three yards square, in "Seed's Yard," one of those dark corners into which decent poverty is so often found now, creeping unwillingly away from the public eye, in the hope of weathering the storm of adversity, in penurious independence.
10126{_}572{_}The old woman never would accept relief from the parish, although the whole family had been out of work for many months.
10126{_}573{_}One of the daughters, a clean, intelligent-looking young woman, about eighteen, sat at the table, eating a little bread and treacle to a cup of light-coloured tea, when we went in; but she blushed, and left off until we had gone--which was not long after.
10126{_}574{_}It felt almost like sacrilege to peer thus into the privacies of such people; but I hope they did not feel as if it had been done offensively.
10126{_}575{_}We called next at the cottage of a hand-loom weaver--a poor trade now in the best of times--a very poor trade--since the days when tattered old "Jem Ceawp" sung his pathetic song of "Jone o' Greenfeelt"--  "Aw'm a poor cotton weighver, as ony one knows; We'n no meight i'th heawse, an' we'n worn eawt er clothes; We'n live't upo nettles, while nettles were good; An' Wayterloo porritch is th' most of er food; This clemmin' and starvin', Wi' never a farthin'-- It's enough to drive ony mon mad."
10126{_}576{_}This family was four in number--man, wife, and two children.
10126{_}577{_}They had always lived near to the ground, for the husband's earnings at the loom were seldom more than 7s.
10126{_}578{_}for a full week.
10126{_}579{_}The wife told us that they were not receiving any relief, for she said that when her husband "had bin eawt o' wark a good while he turn't his hond to shaving;" and in this way the ingenious struggling fellow had scraped a thin living for them during many months.
10126{_}580{_}"But," said she, " it brings varra little in, we hev to trust so much.
10126{_}581{_}He shaves four on 'em for a haw-penny, an' there's a deal on 'em connot pay that.
10126{_}582{_}Yo know, they're badly off--(the woman seemed to think her circumstances rather above the common kind); an' then," continued she, "when they'n run up a shot for three-hawpence or twopence or so, they cannot pay it o' no shap, an' so they stoppen away fro th' shop.
10126{_}583{_}They cannot for shame come, that's heaw it is; so we lose'n their custom till sich times as summat turns up at they can raise a trifle to pay up wi'.
10126{_}584{_}.
10126{_}585{_}.
10126{_}586{_}.
10126{_}587{_}He has nobbut one razzor, but it'll be like to do."
10126{_}588{_}Hearken this, oh, ye spruce Figaros of the city, who trim the clean, crisp whiskers of the well-to-do!
10126{_}589{_}Hearken this, ye dainty perruquiers, "who look so brisk, and smell so sweet," and have such an exquisite knack of chirruping, and lisping, and sliding over the smooth edge of the under lip,--and, sometimes, agreeably too,--"an infinite deal of nothing,"--ye who clip and anoint the hair of Old England's curled darlings!
10126{_}590{_}Eight chins a penny; and three months' credit!
10126{_}591{_}A bodle a piece for mowing chins overgrown with hair like pin-wire, and thick with dust; how would you like that?
10126{_}592{_}How would you get through it all, with a family of four, and only one razor?
10126{_}593{_}The next place we called at was what my friend described, in words that sounded to me, somehow, like melancholy irony,--as "a poor provision shop."
10126{_}594{_}It was, indeed, a poor shop for provender.
10126{_}595{_}In the window, it is true, there were four or five empty glasses, where children's spice had once been.
10126{_}596{_}There was a little deal shelf here and there; but there were neither sand, salt, whitening, nor pipes.
10126{_}597{_}There was not the ghost of a farthing candle, nor a herring, nor a marble, nor a match, nor of any other thing, sour or sweet, eatable or saleable for other uses, except one small mug full of buttermilk up in a corner--the last relic of a departed trade, like the "one rose of the wilderness, left on its stalk to mark where a garden has been."
10126{_}598{_}But I will say more about this in the next chapter.
10126{_}599{_}CHAPTER VI.
10126{_}600{_}Returning to the little shop mentioned in my last--the "little provision shop," where there was nothing left to eat--nothing, indeed, of any kind, except one mug of buttermilk, and a miserable remnant of little empty things, which nobody would buy; four or five glass bottles in the window, two or three poor deal shelves, and a doleful little counter, rudely put together, and looking as if it felt, now, that there was nothing in the world left for it but to become chips at no distant date.
10126{_}601{_}Everything in the place had a sad, subdued look, and seemed conscious of having come down in the world, without hope of ever rising again; even the stript walls appeared to look at one another with a stony gaze of settled despair.
10126{_}602{_}But there was a clean, matronly woman in the place, gliding about from side to side with a cloth in her hands, and wiping first one, then another, of these poor little relics of better days in a caressing way.
10126{_}603{_}The shop had been her special care when times were good, and she clung affectionately to its ruins still.
10126{_}604{_}Besides, going about cleaning and arranging the little empty things in this way looked almost like doing business.
10126{_}605{_}But, nevertheless, the woman had a cheerful, good- humoured countenance.
10126{_}606{_}The sunshine of hope was still warm in her heart; though there was a touch of pathos in the way she gave the little rough counter another kindly wipe now and then, as if she wished to keep its spirits up; and in the way she looked, now at the buttermilk mug, then at the open door, and then at the four glass bottles in the window, which had been gazed at so oft and so eagerly by little children outside, in the days when spice was in them.
10126{_}607{_}.
10126{_}608{_}.
10126{_}609{_}.
10126{_}610{_}The husband came in from the little back room.
10126{_}611{_}He was a hardy, frank-looking man, and, like his wife, a trifle past middle age, I thought; but he had nothing to say, as he stood there with his wife, by the counter side.
10126{_}612{_}She answered our questions freely and simply, and in an uncomplaining way, not making any attempt to awaken sympathy by enlarging upon the facts of their condition.
10126{_}613{_}Theirs was a family of seven--man, wife, and five children.
10126{_}614{_}The man was a spinner; and his thrifty wife had managed the little shop, whilst he worked at the mill.
10126{_}615{_}There are many striving people among the factory operatives, who help up the family earnings by keeping a little shop in this way.
10126{_}616{_}But this family was another of those instances in which working people have been pulled down by misfortune before the present crisis came on.
10126{_}617{_}Just previous to the mills beginning to work short time, four of their five children had been lying ill, all at once, for five months; and, before that trouble befell them, one of the lads had two of his fingers taken off, whilst working at the factory, and so was disabled a good while.
10126{_}618{_}It takes little additional weight to sink those whose chins are only just above water; and these untoward circumstances oiled the way of this struggling family to the ground, before the mills stopped.
10126{_}619{_}A few months' want of work, with their little stock of shop stuff oozing away--partly on credit to their poor neighbours, and partly to live upon themselves --and they become destitute of all, except a few beggarly remnants of empty shop furniture.
10126{_}620{_}Looking round the place, I said," Well, missis, how's trade?"
10126{_}621{_}"Oh, brisk," said she; and then the man and his wife smiled at one another.
10126{_}622{_}"Well," said I, "yo'n sowd up, I see, heawever."
10126{_}623{_}"Ay," answered she, "we'n sowd up, for sure--a good while sin';" and then she smiled again, as if she thought she had said a clever thing.
10126{_}624{_}They had been receiving relief from the parish several weeks; but she told me that some ill-natured neighbour had "set it eawt," that they had sold off their stock out of the shop, and put the money into the bank.
10126{_}625{_}Through this report, the Board of Guardians had "knocked off" their relief for a fortnight, until the falsity of the report was made clear.
10126{_}626{_}After that, the Board gave orders for the man and his wife and three of the children to be admitted to the workhouse, leaving the other two lads, who were working at the "Stone Yard," to "fend for theirsels," and find new nests wherever they could.
10126{_}627{_}This, however, was overruled afterwards; and the family is still holding together in the empty shop,--receiving from all sources, work and relief, about 13s.
10126{_}628{_}a week for the seven,--not bad, compared with the income of very many others.
10126{_}629{_}It is sad to think how many poor families get sundered and scattered about the world in a time like this, never to meet again.
10126{_}630{_}And the false report respecting this family in the little shop, reminds me that the poor are not always kind to the poor.
10126{_}631{_}I learnt, from a gentleman who is Secretary to the Relief Committee of one of the wards, that it is not uncommon for the committees to receive anonymous letters, saying that so and so is unworthy of relief, on some ground or other.
10126{_}632{_}These complaints were generally found to be either wholly false, or founded upon some mistake.
10126{_}633{_}I have three such letters now before me.
10126{_}634{_}The first, written on a torn scrap of ruled paper, runs thus:--"May 19th, 1862.--If you please be so kind as to look after __ Back Newton Street Formerly a Resident of __ as i think he is not Deserving Relief.--A Ratepayer."
10126{_}635{_}In each case I give the spelling, and everything else, exactly as in the originals before me, except the names.
10126{_}636{_}The next of these epistles says:-- "Preston, May 29th.--Sir, I beg to inform you that __, of Park Road, in receipt from the Relief Fund, is a very unworthy person, having worked two days since the 16 and drunk the remainder and his wife also; for the most part, he has plenty of work for himself his wife and a journeyman but that is their regular course of life.
10126{_}637{_}And the S___s have all their family working full time.
10126{_}638{_}Yours respectfully."
10126{_}639{_}These last two are anonymous.
10126{_}640{_}The next is written in a very good hand, upon a square piece of very blue writing paper.
10126{_}641{_}It has a name attached, but no address:--"Preston, June 2nd, 1862.--Mr.
10126{_}642{_}Dunn,-- Dear Sir, Would you please to inquire into the case of __, of __.
10126{_}643{_}the are a family of 3 the man work four or more days per week on the moor the woman works 6 days per week at Messrs Simpsons North Road the third is a daughter 13 or 14 should be a weaver but to lasey she has good places such as Mr. Hollins and Horrocks and Millers as been sent a way for being to lasey.
10126{_}644{_}the man and woman very fond of drink.
10126{_}645{_}I as a Nabour and a subscriber do not think this a proper case for your charity.
10126{_}646{_}Yours truly, __."
10126{_}647{_}The committee could not find out the writer of this, although a name is given.
10126{_}648{_}Such things as these need no comment.
10126{_}649{_}The next house we called at was inhabited by an old widow and her only daughter.
10126{_}650{_}The daughter had been grievously afflicted with disease of the heart, and quite incapable of helping herself during the last eleven years.
10126{_}651{_}The poor worn girl sat upon an old tattered kind of sofa, near the fire, panting for breath in the close atmosphere.
10126{_}652{_}She sat there in feverish helplessness, sallow and shrunken, and unable to bear up her head.
10126{_}653{_}It was a painful thing to look at her.
10126{_}654{_}She had great difficulty in uttering a few words.
10126{_}655{_}I can hardly guess what her age may be now; I should think about twenty- five.
10126{_}656{_}Mr Toulmin, one of the visitors who accompanied me to the place, reminded the young woman of his having called upon them there more than four years ago, to leave some bedding which had been bestowed upon an old woman by a certain charity in the town.
10126{_}657{_}He saw no more of them after that, until the present hard times began, when he was deputed by the Relief Committee to call at that distressed corner amongst others in his own neighbourhood; and when he first opened the door, after a lapse of four years, he was surprised to find the same young woman, sitting in the same place, gasping painfully for breath, as he had last seen her.
10126{_}658{_}The old widow had just been able to earn what kept soul and body together in her sick girl and herself, during the last eleven years, by washing and such like work.
10126{_}659{_}But even this resource had fallen away a good deal during these bad times; there are so many poor creatures like herself, driven to extremity, and glad to grasp at any little bit of employment which can be had.
10126{_}660{_}In addition to what the old woman could get by a day's washing now and then, she received 1s.
10126{_}661{_}6d.
10126{_}662{_}a week from the parish.
10126{_}663{_}Think of the poor old soul trailing about the world, trying to "scratch a living" for herself and her daughter by washing; and having to hurry home from her labour to attend to that sick girl through eleven long years.
10126{_}664{_}Such a life is a good deal like a slow funeral.
10126{_}665{_}It is struggling for a few breaths more, with the worms crawling over you.
10126{_}666{_}And yet I am told that the old woman was not accustomed to "make a poor mouth," as the saying goes.
10126{_}667{_}How true it is that "a great many people in this world have only one form of rhetoric for their profoundest experiences, namely--to waste away and die."
10126{_}668{_}Our next visit was to an Irish family.
10126{_}669{_}There was an old woman in, and a flaxen-headed lad about ten years of age.
10126{_}670{_}She was sitting upon a low chair,--the only seat in the place,--and the tattered lad was kneeling on the ground before her, whilst she combed his hair out.
10126{_}671{_}"Well, missis, how are you getting on amongst it?"
10126{_}672{_}"Oh, well, then, just middlin', Mr T. Ye see, I am busy combin' this boy's hair a bit, for 'tis gettin' like a wisp o' hay."
10126{_}673{_}There was not a vestige of furniture in the cottage, except the chair the old woman sat on.
10126{_}674{_}She said, "I did sell the childer's bedstead for 2s.
10126{_}675{_}6d.
10126{_}676{_}; an' after that I sold the bed from under them for 1s.
10126{_}677{_}6d., just to keep them from starvin' to death.
10126{_}678{_}The childer had been two days without mate then, an' faith I couldn't bear it any longer.
10126{_}679{_}After that I did sell the big pan, an' then the new rockin' chair, an' so on, one thing after another, till all wint entirely, barrin' this I am sittin' on, an' they wint for next to nothin' too.
10126{_}680{_}Sure, I paid 9s.
10126{_}681{_}6d.
10126{_}682{_}for the bed itself, which was sold for 1s.
10126{_}683{_}6d.
10126{_}684{_}We all sleep on straw now."
10126{_}685{_}This family was seven in number.
10126{_}686{_}The mill at which they used to work had been stopped about ten months.
