10126{_}687{_}One of the family had found employment at another mill, three months out of the ten, and the old man himself had got a few days' work in that time.
10126{_}688{_}The rest of the family had been wholly unemployed, during the ten months.
10126{_}689{_}Except the little money this work brought in, and a trifle raised now and then by the sale of a bit of furniture when hunger and cold pressed them hard, the whole family had been living upon 5s.
10126{_}690{_}a week for the last ten months.
10126{_}691{_}The rent was running on.
10126{_}692{_}The eldest daughter was twenty- eight years of age.
10126{_}693{_}As we came away Mr Toulmin said to me, "Well, I have called at that house regularly for the last sixteen weeks, and this is the first time I ever saw a fire in the place.
10126{_}694{_}But the old man has got two days' work this week--that may account for the fire."
10126{_}695{_}It was now close upon half-past seven in the evening, at which time I had promised to call upon the Secretary of the Trinity Ward Relief Committee, whose admirable letter in the London Times, attracted so much attention about a month ago.
10126{_}696{_}I met several members of the committee at his lodgings, and we had an hour's interesting conversation.
10126{_}697{_}I learnt that, in cases of sickness arising from mere weakness, from poorness of diet, or from unsuitableness of the food commonly provided by the committee, orders were now issued for such kind of "kitchen physic" as was recommended by the doctors.
10126{_}698{_}The committee had many cases of this kind.
10126{_}699{_}One instance was mentioned, in which, by the doctor's advice, four ounces of mutton chop daily had been ordered to be given to a certain sick man, until further notice.
10126{_}700{_}The thing went on and was forgotten, until one day, when the distributor of food said to the committeeman who had issued the order, "I suppose I must continue that daily mutton chop to so-and- so?"
10126{_}701{_}"Eh, no; he's been quite well two months?"
10126{_}702{_}The chop had been going on for ninety-five days.
10126{_}703{_}We had some talk with that class of operatives who are both clean, provident, and "heawse-preawd," as Lancashire folk call it.
10126{_}704{_}The Secretary told me that he was averse to such people living upon the sale of their furniture; and the committee had generally relieved the distress of such people, just as if they had no furniture, at all.
10126{_}705{_}He mentioned the case of a family of factory operatives, who were all fervent lovers of music, as so many of the working people of Lancashire are.
10126{_}706{_}Whilst in full work, they had scraped up money to buy a piano; and, long after the ploughshare of ruin had begun to drive over the little household, they clung to the darling instrument, which was such a source of pure pleasure to them, and they were advised to keep it by the committee which relieved them.
10126{_}707{_}"Yes," said another member of the committee," but I called there lately, and the piano's gone at last."
10126{_}708{_}Many interesting things came out in the course of our conversation.
10126{_}709{_}One mentioned a house he had called at, where there was neither chair, table, nor bed; and one of the little lads had to hold up a piece of board for him to write upon.
10126{_}710{_}Another spoke of the difficulties which "lone women" have to encounter in these hard times.
10126{_}711{_}"I knocked so-and-so off my list," said one of the committee, "till I had inquired into an ill report I heard of her.
10126{_}712{_}But she came crying to me; and I found out that the woman had been grossly belied."
10126{_}713{_}Another (Mr Nowell) told of a house on his list, where they had no less than one hundred and fifty pawn tickets.
10126{_}714{_}He told, also, of a moulder's family, who had been all out of work and starving so long, that their poor neighbours came at last and recommended the committee to relieve them, as they would not apply for relief themselves.
10126{_}715{_}They accepted relief just one week, and then the man came and said that he had a PROSPECT of work; and he shouldn't need relief tickets any longer.
10126{_}716{_}It was here that I heard so much about anonymous letters, of which I have given you three samples.
10126{_}717{_}Having said that I should like to see the soup kitchen, one of the committee offered to go with me thither at six o'clock the next morning; and so I came away from the meeting in the cool twilight.
10126{_}718{_}Old Preston looked fine to me in the clear air of that declining day.
10126{_}719{_}I stood a while at the end of the "Bull" gateway.
10126{_}720{_}There was a comical-looking little knock-kneed fellow in the middle of the street --a wandering minstrel, well known in Preston by the name of "Whistling Jack."
10126{_}721{_}There he stood, warbling and waving his band, and looking from side to side,--in vain.
10126{_}722{_}At last I got him to whistle the "Flowers of Edinburgh."
10126{_}723{_}He did it, vigorously; and earned his penny well.
10126{_}724{_}But even "Whistling Jack" complained of the times.
10126{_}725{_}He said Preston folk had "no taste for music."
10126{_}726{_}But he assured me the time would come when there would be a monument to him in that town.
10126{_}727{_}CHAPTER VII.
10126{_}728{_}About half-past six I found my friend waiting at the end of the "Bull" gateway.
10126{_}729{_}It was a lovely morning.
10126{_}730{_}The air was cool and clear, and the sky was bright.
10126{_}731{_}It was easy to see which was the way to the soup kitchen, by the stragglers going and coming.
10126{_}732{_}We passed the famous "Orchard," now a kind of fairground, which has been the scene of so many popular excitements in troubled times.
10126{_}733{_}All was quiet in the "Orchard" that morning, except that, here, a starved-looking woman, with a bit of old shawl tucked round her head, and a pitcher in her hand, and there, a bare-footed lass, carrying a tin can, hurried across the sunny space towards the soup kitchen.
10126{_}734{_}We passed a new inn, called "The Port Admiral."
10126{_}735{_}On the top of the building there were three life-sized statues--Wellington and Nelson, with the Greek slave between them--a curious companionship.
10126{_}736{_}These statues reminded me of a certain Englishman riding through Dublin, for the first time, upon an Irish car.
10126{_}737{_}"What are the three figures yonder?"
10126{_}738{_}said he to the car-boy, pointing to the top of some public building.
10126{_}739{_}"Thim three is the twelve apostles, your honour," answered the driver.
10126{_}740{_}"Nay, nay," said the traveller,"that'll not do.
10126{_}741{_}How do you make twelve out of three?"
10126{_}742{_}"Bedad," replied the driver, "your honour couldn't expect the whole twelve to be out at once such a murtherin' wet day as this."
10126{_}743{_}But we had other things than these to think of that day.
10126{_}744{_}As we drew near the baths and washhouses, where the soup kitchen is, the stream of people increased.
10126{_}745{_}About the gate there was a cluster of melancholy loungers, looking cold and hungry.
10126{_}746{_}They were neither going in nor going away.
10126{_}747{_}I was told afterwards that many of these were people who had neither money nor tickets for food--some of them wanderers from town to town; anybody may meet them limping, footsore and forlorn, upon the roads in Lancashire, just now-- houseless wanderers, who had made their way to the soup kitchen to beg a mouthful from those who were themselves at death's door.
10126{_}748{_}In the best of times there are such wanderers; and, in spite of the generous provision made for the relief of the poor, there must be, in a time like the present, a great number who let go their hold of home (if they have any), and drift away in search of better fortune, and, sometimes, into irregular courses of life, never to settle more.
10126{_}749{_}Entering the yard, we found the wooden sheds crowded with people at breakfast--all ages, from white-haired men, bent with years, to eager childhood, yammering over its morning meal, and careless till the next nip of hunger came.
10126{_}750{_}Here and there a bonny lass had crept into the shade with her basin; and there was many a brown-faced man, who had been hardened by working upon the moor or at the "stone-yard."
10126{_}751{_}"Theer, thae's shap't that at last, as how?"
10126{_}752{_}said one of these to his friend, who had just finished and stood wiping his mouth complacently.
10126{_}753{_}"Shap't that," replied the other, "ay, lad, aw can do a ticket and a hafe (three pints of soup) every morning."
10126{_}754{_}Five hundred people breakfast in the sheds alone, every day.
10126{_}755{_}The soup kitchen opens at five in the morning, and there is always a crowd waiting to get in.
10126{_}756{_}This looks like the eagerness of hunger.
10126{_}757{_}I was told that they often deliver 3000 quarts of soup at this kitchen in two hours.
10126{_}758{_}The superintendent of the bread department informed me that, on that morning, he had served out two thousand loaves, of 3lb.
10126{_}759{_}11oz.
10126{_}760{_}each.
10126{_}761{_}There was a window at one end, where soup was delivered to such as brought money for it instead of tickets.
10126{_}762{_}Those who came with tickets--by far the greatest number-- had to pass in single file through a strong wooden maze, which restrained their eagerness, and compelled them to order.
10126{_}763{_}I noticed that only a small proportion of men went through the maze; they were mostly women and children.
10126{_}764{_}There was many a fine, intelligent young face hurried blushing through that maze--many a bonny lad and lass who will be heard of honourably hereafter.
10126{_}765{_}The variety of utensils presented showed that some of the poor souls had been hard put to it for things to fetch their soup in.
10126{_}766{_}One brought a pitcher; another a bowl; and another a tin can, a world too big for what it had to hold.
10126{_}767{_}"Yo mun mind th' jug," said one old woman; "it's cracked, an' it's noan o' mine."
10126{_}768{_}"Will ye bring me some?"
10126{_}769{_}said a little, light- haired lass, holding up her rosy neb to the soupmaster.
10126{_}770{_}"Aw want a ha'poth," said a lad with a three-quart can in his hand.
10126{_}771{_}The benevolent-looking old gentleman who had taken the superintendence of the soup department as a labour of love, told me that there had been a woman there by half-past five that morning, who had come four miles for some coffee.
10126{_}772{_}There was a poor fellow breakfasting in the shed at the same time; and he gave the woman a thick shive of his bread as she went away.
10126{_}773{_}He mentioned other instances of the same humane feeling; and he said, "After what I have seen of them here, I say, 'Let me fall into the hands of the poor.'"
10126{_}774{_}"They who, half-fed, feed the breadless, in the travail of distress; They who, taking from a little, give to those who still have less; They who, needy, yet can pity when they look on greater need; These are Charity's disciples,--these are Mercy's sons indeed."
10126{_}775{_}We returned to the middle of the town just as the shopkeepers in Friargate were beginning to take their shutters down.
10126{_}776{_}I had another engagement at half-past nine.
10126{_}777{_}A member of the Trinity Ward Relief Committee, who is master of the Catholic school in that ward, had offered to go with me to visit some distressed people who were under his care in that part of the town.
10126{_}778{_}We left Friargate at the appointed time.
10126{_}779{_}As we came along there was a crowd in front of Messrs Wards', the fishmongers.
10126{_}780{_}A fine sturgeon had just been brought in.
10126{_}781{_}It had been caught in the Ribble that morning.
10126{_}782{_}We went in to look at the royal fish.
10126{_}783{_}It was six feet long, and weighed above a hundred pounds.
10126{_}784{_}I don't know that I ever saw a sturgeon before.
10126{_}785{_}But we had other fish to fry; and so we went on.
10126{_}786{_}The first place we called at was a cellar in Nile Street.
10126{_}787{_}"Here," said my companion, "let us have a look at old John."
10126{_}788{_}A gray-headed little man, of seventy, lived down in this one room, sunken from the street.
10126{_}789{_}He had been married forty years, and if I remember aright, he lost his wife about four years ago.
10126{_}790{_}Since that time, he had lived in this cellar, all alone, washing and cooking for himself.
10126{_}791{_}But I think the last would not trouble him much, for "they have no need for fine cooks who have only one potato to their dinner."
10126{_}792{_}When a lad, he had been apprenticed to a bobbin turner.
10126{_}793{_}Afterwards he picked up some knowledge of engineering; and he had been "well off in his day."
10126{_}794{_}He now got a few coppers occasionally from the poor folk about, by grinding knives, and doing little tinkering jobs.
10126{_}795{_}Under the window he had a rude bench, with a few rusty tools upon it, and in one corner there was a low, miserable bedstead, without clothing upon it.
10126{_}796{_}There was one cratchinly chair in the place, too; but hardly anything else.
10126{_}797{_}He had no fire; be generally went into neighbours' houses to warm himself.
10126{_}798{_}He was not short of such food as the Relief Committees bestow.
10126{_}799{_}There was a piece of bread upon the bench, left from his morning meal; and the old fellow chirruped about, and looked as blithe as if he was up to the middle in clover.
10126{_}800{_}He showed us a little thing which he had done "for a bit ov a prank."
10126{_}801{_}The number of his cellar was 8, and he had cut out a large tin figure of 8, a foot long, and nailed it upon his door, for the benefit of some of his friends that were getting bad in their eyesight, and "couldn't read smo' print so low deawn as that."
10126{_}802{_}"Well, John," said my companion, when we went in, "how are you getting on?"
10126{_}803{_}"Oh, bravely," replied he, handing a piece of blue paper to the inquirer, "bravely; look at that!"
10126{_}804{_}Why, this is a summons," said my companion.
10126{_}805{_}"Ay, bigad is't, too," answered the old man.
10126{_}806{_}"Never had sich a thing i' my life afore!
10126{_}807{_}Think o' me gettin' a summons for breakin' windows at seventy year owd.
10126{_}808{_}A bonny warlock, that, isn't it?
10126{_}809{_}Why, th' whole street went afore th' magistrates to get mo off."
10126{_}810{_}"Then you did get off, John?"
10126{_}811{_}"Get off!
10126{_}812{_}Sure, aw did.
10126{_}813{_}It wur noan o' me.
10126{_}814{_}It wur a keaw jobber, at did it.
10126{_}815{_}.
10126{_}816{_}.
10126{_}817{_}.
10126{_}818{_}Aw'll tell yo what, for two pins aw'd frame that summons, an' hang it eawt o' th' window; but it would look so impudent."
10126{_}819{_}Old John's wants were inquired into, and we left him fiddling among his rusty tools.
10126{_}820{_}We next went to a place called Hammond's Row--thirteen poor cottages, side by side.
10126{_}821{_}Twelve of the thirteen were inhabited by people living, almost entirely, upon relief, either from the parish or from the Relief Committee.
10126{_}822{_}There was only one house where no relief was needed.
10126{_}823{_}As we passed by, the doors were nearly all open, and the interiors all presented the same monotonous phase of destitution.
10126{_}824{_}They looked as if they had been sacked by bum-bailiffs.
10126{_}825{_}The topmost house was the only place where I saw a fire.
10126{_}826{_}A family of eight lived there.
10126{_}827{_}They were Irish people.
10126{_}828{_}The wife, a tall, cheerful woman, sat suckling her child, and giving a helping hand now and then to her husband's work.
10126{_}829{_}He was a little, pale fellow, with only one arm, and he had an impediment in his speech.
10126{_}830{_}He had taken to making cheap boxes of thin, rough deal, afterwards covered with paper.
10126{_}831{_}With the help of his wife he could make one in a day, and he got ninepence profit out of it--when the box was sold.
10126{_}832{_}He was working at one when we went in, and he twirled it proudly about with his one arm, and stammered out a long explanation about the way it had been made; and then he got upon the lid, and sprang about a little, to let us see how much it would bear.
10126{_}833{_}As the brave little tattered man stood there upon the box-lid, springing, and sputtering, and waving his one arm, his wife looked up at him with a smile, as if she thought him "the greatest wight on ground."
10126{_}834{_}There was a little curly-headed child standing by, quietly taking in all that was going on.
10126{_}835{_}I laid my hand upon her head; and asked her what her name was.
10126{_}836{_}She popped her thumb into her mouth, and looked shyly about from one to another, but never a word could I get her to say.
10126{_}837{_}"That's Lizzy," said the woman; "she is a little visitor belongin' to one o' the neighbours.
10126{_}838{_}They are badly off, and she often comes in.
10126{_}839{_}Sure, our childer is very fond of her, an' so she is of them.
10126{_}840{_}She is fine company wid ourselves, but always very shy wid strangers.
10126{_}841{_}Come now, Lizzy, darlin'; tell us your name, love, won't you, now?"
10126{_}842{_}But it was no use; we couldn't get her to speak.
10126{_}843{_}In the next cottage where we called, in this row, there was a woman washing.
10126{_}844{_}Her mug was standing upon a stool in the middle of the floor; and there was not any other thing in the place in the shape of furniture or household utensil.
10126{_}845{_}The walls were bare of everything, except a printed paper, bearing these words:  "The wages of sin is death.
10126{_}846{_}But the gift of God is eternal life, through Jesus Christ our Lord."
10126{_}847{_}We now went to another street, and visited the cottage of a blind chairmaker, called John Singleton.
10126{_}848{_}He was a kind of oracle among the poor folk of the neighbourhood.
10126{_}849{_}The old chairmaker was sitting by the fire when we went in; and opposite to him sat "Old John," the hero of the broken windows in Nile Street.
10126{_}850{_}He had come up to have a crack with his blind crony.
10126{_}851{_}The chairmaker was seventy years of age, and he had benefited by the advantage of good fundamental instruction in his youth.
10126{_}852{_}He was very communicative.
10126{_}853{_}He said he should have been educated for the priesthood, at Stonyhurst College.
10126{_}854{_}"My clothes were made, an' everything was ready for me to start to Stonyhurst.
10126{_}855{_}There was a stagecoach load of us going; but I failed th' heart, an' wouldn't go--an' I've forethought ever sin'.
10126{_}856{_}Mr Newby said to my friends at the same time, he said, 'You don't need to be frightened of him; he'll make the brightest priest of all the lot--an' I should, too.
10126{_}857{_}.
10126{_}858{_}.
10126{_}859{_}.
10126{_}860{_}I consider mysel' a young man yet, i' everything, except it be somethin' at's uncuth to me."
10126{_}861{_}And now, old John, the grinder, began to complain again of how badly he had been used about the broken windows in Nile Street.
10126{_}862{_}But the old chairmaker stopped him; and, turning up his blind eyes, he said, "John, don't you be foolish.
10126{_}863{_}Bother no moor abeawt it.
10126{_}864{_}All things has but a time."
10126{_}865{_}CHAPTER VIII.
10126{_}866{_}A man cannot go wrong in Trinity Ward just now, if he wants to see poor folk.
10126{_}867{_}He may find them there at any time, but now he cannot help but meet them; and nobody can imagine how badly off they are, unless he goes amongst them.
10126{_}868{_}They are biding the hard time out wonderfully well, and they will do so to the end.
10126{_}869{_}They certainly have not more than a common share of human frailty.
10126{_}870{_}There are those who seem to think that when people are suddenly reduced to poverty, they should become suddenly endowed with the rarest virtues; but it never was so, and, perhaps, never will be so long as the world rolls.
10126{_}871{_}In my rambles about this ward, I was astonished at the dismal succession of destitute homes, and the number of struggling owners of little shops, who were watching their stocks sink gradually down to nothing, and looking despondingly at the cold approach of pauperism.
10126{_}872{_}I was astonished at the strings of dwellings, side by side, stript, more or less, of the commonest household utensils--the poor little bare houses, often crowded with lodgers, whose homes had been broken up elsewhere; sometimes crowded, three or four families of decent working people in a cottage of half-a-crown a-week rental; sleeping anywhere, on benches or on straw, and afraid to doff their clothes at night time because they had no other covering.
10126{_}873{_}Now and then the weekly visitor comes to the door of a house where he has regularly called.
10126{_}874{_}He lifts the latch, and finds the door locked.
10126{_}875{_}He looks in at the window.
10126{_}876{_}The house is empty, and the people are gone- -the Lord knows where.
10126{_}877{_}Who can tell what tales of sorrow will have their rise in the pressure of a time like this--tales that will never be written, and that no statistics will reveal.
10126{_}878{_}Trinity Ward swarms with factory operatives; and, after our chat with blind John, the chairmaker, and his ancient crony the grinder from Nile Street, we set off again to see something more of them.
10126{_}879{_}Fitful showers came down through the day, and we had to shelter now and then.
10126{_}880{_}In one cottage, where we stopped a few minutes, the old woman told us that, in addition to their own family, they had three young women living with them--the orphan daughters of her husband's brother.
10126{_}881{_}They had been out of work thirty-four weeks, and their uncle--a very poor man--had been obliged to take them into his house, "till sich times as they could afford to pay for lodgin's somewheer else."
10126{_}882{_}My companion asked whether they were all out of work still.
10126{_}883{_}"Naw," replied the old woman, "one on 'em has getten on to wortch a few days for t' sick (that is, in the place of some sick person).
10126{_}884{_}Hoo's wortchin' i' th' cardreawn at 'Th' Big-un.'"
10126{_}885{_}(This is the name they give to Messrs Swainson and Birley's mill.)
10126{_}886{_}The next place we called at was the house of an old joiner.
10126{_}887{_}He was lying very ill upstairs.
10126{_}888{_}As we drew up to the door, my companion said, "Now, this is a clean, respectable family.
10126{_}889{_}They have struggled hard and suffered a great deal, before they would ask for relief."
10126{_}890{_}When we went in, the wife was cleaning her well-nigh empty house.
10126{_}891{_}"Eh," said she," I thought it wur th' clubman comin', an' I wur just goin' to tell him that I had nothin' for him."
10126{_}892{_}The family was seven in number--man, wife, and five children.
10126{_}893{_}The husband, as I have said, was lying ill.
10126{_}894{_}The wife told me that they had only 6s.
10126{_}895{_}a-week coming in for the seven to live upon.
10126{_}896{_}My companion was the weekly visitor who relieved them.
10126{_}897{_}She told me that her husband was sixty- eight years old; she was not forty.
10126{_}898{_}She said that her husband was not strong, and he had been going nearly barefoot and "clemmed" all through last winter, and she was afraid he had got his death of cold.
10126{_}899{_}They had not a bed left to lie upon.
10126{_}900{_}"My husband," said she,"was a master joiner once, an' was doin' very well.
10126{_}901{_}But you see how we are now."
10126{_}902{_}There were two portraits--oil paintings--hanging against the wall.
10126{_}903{_}"Whose portraits are these?"
10126{_}904{_}said I.
10126{_}905{_}"Well; that's my master--an' this is me," replied she.
10126{_}906{_}"He would have 'em taken some time since.
10126{_}907{_}I couldn't think o' sellin' 'em; or else, yo see, we've sold nearly everything we had.
10126{_}908{_}I did try to pawn 'em, too, thinkin' we could get 'em back again when things came round; but, I can assure yo, I couldn't find a broker anywhere that would tak' 'em in."
10126{_}909{_}"Well, Missis," said my companion, "yo have one comfort; you are always clean."
10126{_}910{_}"Eh, bless yo!"
10126{_}911{_}replied she, "I couldn't live among dirt!
10126{_}912{_}My husban' tells me that I clean all the luck away; but aw'm sure there's no luck i' filth; if there is, anybody may tak' it for me."
10126{_}913{_}The rain had stopt again; and after my friend had made a note respecting some additional relief for the family, we bade the woman good day.
10126{_}914{_}We had not gone far before a little ragged lass looked up admiringly at two pinks I had stuck in my buttonhole, and holding up her hand, said, "Eh, gi' me a posy!"
10126{_}915{_}My friend pointed to one of the cottages we passed, and said that the last time he called there, he found the family all seated round a large bowl of porridge, made of Indian meal.
10126{_}916{_}This meal is sold at a penny a pound.
10126{_}917{_}He stopped at another cottage and said, "Here's a house where I always find them reading when I call.
10126{_}918{_}I know the people very well."
10126{_}919{_}He knocked and tried the latch, but there was nobody in.
10126{_}920{_}As we passed an open door, the pleasant smell of oatcake baking came suddenly upon me.
10126{_}921{_}It woke up many memories of days gone by.
10126{_}922{_}I saw through the window a stout, meal-dusted old woman, busy with her wooden ladle and baking-shovel at a brisk oven.
10126{_}923{_}"Now, I should like to look in there for a minute or two, if it can be done," said I.
10126{_}924{_}"Well," replied my friend, "this woman is not on our books; she gets her own living in the way you see.
10126{_}925{_}But come in; it will be all right; I know her very well."
10126{_}926{_}I was glad of that, for I wanted to have a chat with her, and to peep at the baking.
10126{_}927{_}"Good morning, Missis," said he; "how are you?"
10126{_}928{_}"Why, just in a middlin' way."
10126{_}929{_}"How long is this wet weather going to last, think you?"
10126{_}930{_}"Nay, there ye hev me fast;--but what brings ye here this mornin'?"
10126{_}931{_}said the old woman, resting the end of her ladle on the little counter; "I never trouble sic like chaps as ye."
10126{_}932{_}"No, no," replied my friend; "we have not called about anything of that kind."
10126{_}933{_}"What, then, pray ye?"
10126{_}934{_}"Well, my friend, here, is almost a stranger in Preston; and as soon as ever he smelt the baking, he said he should like to see it, so I took the liberty of bringing him in."
10126{_}935{_}"Oh, ay; come in, an' welcome.
10126{_}936{_}Ye're just i' time, too; for I've bin sat at t' back to sarra (serve) t' pigs."
10126{_}937{_}"You're not a native of Lancashire, Missis," said I.
10126{_}938{_}"Why, wheer then?
10126{_}939{_}come, now; let's be knowin', as ye're so sharp."
10126{_}940{_}"Cumberland," said I.
10126{_}941{_}"Well, now; ye're reight, sewer enough.
10126{_}942{_}But how did ye find it out, now?"
10126{_}943{_}"Why, you said that you had been out to sarra t' pigs.
10126{_}944{_}A native of Lancashire would have said 'serve' instead of 'sarra.'"
10126{_}945{_}"Well, that's varra queer; for I've bin a lang time away from my awn country.
10126{_}946{_}But, whereivver do ye belang to, as ye're so bowd wi' me?"
10126{_}947{_}said she, smiling, and turning over a cake which was baking upon the oven.
10126{_}948{_}I told her that I was born a few miles from Manchester.
10126{_}949{_}"Manchester!
10126{_}950{_}never, sewer;" said she, resting her ladle again; "why, I lived ever so long i' Manchester when I was young.
10126{_}951{_}I was cook at th' Swan i' Shudehill, aboon forty year sin."
10126{_}952{_}She said that, in those days, the Swan, in Shudehill, was much frequented by the commercial men of Manchester.
10126{_}953{_}It was a favourite dining house for them.
10126{_}954{_}Many of them even brought their own beefsteak on a skewer; and paid a penny for the cooking of it.
10126{_}955{_}She said she always liked Manchester very well; but she had not been there for a good while.
10126{_}956{_}"But," said she, "ye'll hev plenty o' oatcake theer--sartin."
10126{_}957{_}"Not much, now," replied I; "it's getting out o' fashion."
10126{_}958{_}I told her that we had to get it once a week from a man who came all the way from Stretford into Manchester, with a large basketful upon his head, crying "Woat cakes, two a penny!"
10126{_}959{_}"Two a penny!"
10126{_}960{_}said she; "why, they'll not be near as big as these, belike."
10126{_}961{_}"Not quite," replied I.
10126{_}962{_}"Not quite!
10126{_}963{_}naw; not hauf t' size, aw warnd!
10126{_}964{_}Why, th' poor fellow desarves his brass iv he niver gev a farthin' for th' stuff to mak 'eni on.
10126{_}965{_}What!
10126{_}966{_}I knaw what oatcake bakin' is."
10126{_}967{_}Leaving the canny old Cumberland woman at her baking, we called at a cottage in Everton Gardens.
10126{_}968{_}It was as clean as a gentleman's parlour; but there was no furniture in sight except a table, and, upon the table, a fine bush of fresh hawthorn blossom, stuck in a pint jug full of water.
10126{_}969{_}Here, I heard again the common story--they had been several months out of work; their household goods had dribbled away in ruinous sales, for something to live upon; and now, they had very little left but the walls.
10126{_}970{_}The little woman said to me, "Bless yo, there is at thinks we need'n nought, becose we keepen a daycent eawtside.
10126{_}971{_}But, I know my own know abeawt that.
10126{_}972{_}Beside, one doesn't like to fill folk's meawths, iv one is ill off."
10126{_}973{_}It was now a little past noon, and we spent a few minutes looking through the Catholic schoolhouse, in Trinity Ward--a spacious brick building.
10126{_}974{_}The scholars were away at dinner.
10126{_}975{_}My friend is master of the school.
10126{_}976{_}His assistant offered to go with us to one or two Irish families in a close wynd, hard by, called Wilkie's Court.
10126{_}977{_}In every case I had the great advantage of being thus accompanied by gentlemen who were friendly and familiar with the poor we visited.
10126{_}978{_}This was a great facility to me.
10126{_}979{_}Wilkie's Court is a little cul de sac, with about half-a-dozen wretched cottages in it, fronted by a dead wall.
10126{_}980{_}The inhabitants of the place are all Irish.
10126{_}981{_}They were nearly all kept alive by relief from one source or other; but their poverty was not relieved by that cleanliness which I had witnessed in so many equally poor houses, making the best use of those simple means of comfort which are invaluable, although they cost little or nothing.
10126{_}982{_}In the first house we called at, a middle-aged woman was pacing slowly about the unwholesome house with a child in her arms.
10126{_}983{_}My friend inquired where the children were.
10126{_}984{_}"They are in the houses about; all but the one poor boy."
10126{_}985{_}"And where is he?"
10126{_}986{_}said I.
10126{_}987{_}"Well, he comes home now an' agin; he comes an' goes; sure, we don't know how.
10126{_}988{_}.
10126{_}989{_}.
10126{_}990{_}.
10126{_}991{_}Ah, thin, sir," continued she, beginning to cry, "I'll tell ye the rale truth, now.
10126{_}992{_}He was drawn away by some bad lads, an' he got three months in the New Bailey; that's God's truth.
10126{_}993{_}.
10126{_}994{_}.
10126{_}995{_}.
10126{_}996{_}Ah, what'll I do wid him," said she, bursting into tears afresh; "what'll I do wid him?
10126{_}997{_}sure, he is my own!"
10126{_}998{_}We did not stop long to intrude upon such trouble as this.
10126{_}999{_}She called out as we came away to tell us that the poor crayter next door was quite helpless.
10126{_}1000{_}The next house was, in some respects, more comfortable than the last, though it was quite as poor in household goods.
10126{_}1001{_}There was one flimsy deal table, one little chair, and two half-penny pictures of Catholic saints pinned against the wall.
10126{_}1002{_}"Sure, I sold the other table since you wor here before," said the woman to my friend; "I sold it for two-an'-aightpence, an' bought this one for sixpence."
10126{_}1003{_}At the house of another Irish family, my friend inquired where all the chairs were gone.
10126{_}1004{_}"Oh," said a young woman," the baillies did fetch uvverything away, barrin' the one sate, when we were livin' in Lancaster Street."
10126{_}1005{_}"Where do you all sit now, then?"
10126{_}1006{_}"My mother sits there," replied she, "an' we sit upon the flure."
10126{_}1007{_}"I heard they were goin' to sell these heawses," said one of the lads, "but, begorra," continued he, with a laugh, "I wouldn't wonder did they sell the ground from under us next."
10126{_}1008{_}In the course of our visitation a thunder storm came on, during which we took shelter with a poor widow woman, who had a plateful of steeped peas for sale, in the window.
10126{_}1009{_}She also dealt in rags and bones in a small way, and so managed to get a living, as she said, "beawt troublin' onybody for charity."
10126{_}1010{_}She said it was a thing that folk had to wait a good deal out in the cold for.
10126{_}1011{_}It was market-day, and there were many country people in Preston.
10126{_}1012{_}On my way back to the middle of the town, I called at an old inn, in Friargate, where I listened with pleasure a few minutes to the old- fashioned talk of three farmers from the Fylde country.
10126{_}1013{_}Their conversation was principally upon cow-drinks.
10126{_}1014{_}One of them said there was nothing in the world like "peppermint tay an' new butter" for cows that had the belly-ache.
10126{_}1015{_}"They'll be reet in a varra few minutes at after yo gotten that into 'em," said he.
10126{_}1016{_}As evening came on the weather settled into one continuous shower, and I left Preston in the heavy rain, weary, and thinking of what I had seen during the day.
10126{_}1017{_}Since then I have visited the town again, and I shall say something about that visit hereafter.
10126{_}1018{_}CHAPTER IX.
10126{_}1019{_}The rain had been falling heavily through the night.
10126{_}1020{_}It was raw and gusty, and thick clouds were sailing wildly overhead, as I went to the first train for Preston.
10126{_}1021{_}It was that time of morning when there is a lull in the streets of Manchester, between six and eight.
10126{_}1022{_}The "knocker-up" had shouldered his long wand, and paddled home to bed again; and the little stalls, at which the early workman stops for his half-penny cup of coffee, were packing up.
10126{_}1023{_}A cheerless morning, and the few people that were about looked damp and low spirited.
10126{_}1024{_}I bought the day's paper, and tried to read it, as we flitted by the glimpses of dirty garret-life, through the forest of chimneys, gushing forth their thick morning fumes into the drizzly air, and over the dingy web of Salford streets.
10126{_}1025{_}We rolled on through Pendleton, where the country is still trying to look green here and there, under increasing difficulties; but it was not till we came to where the green vale of Clifton open out, that I became quite reconciled to the weather.
10126{_}1026{_}Before we were well out of sight of the ancient tower of Prestwich Church, the day brightened a little.
10126{_}1027{_}The shifting folds of gloomy cloud began to glide asunder, and through the gauzy veils which lingered in the interspaces, there came a dim radiance which lighted up the rain-drops "lingering on the pointed thorns;" and the tall meadow grasses were swaying to and fro with their loads of liquid pearls, in courtesies full of exquisite grace, as we whirled along.
10126{_}1028{_}I enjoyed the ride that raw morning, although the sky was all gloom again long before we came in sight of the Ribble.
10126{_}1029{_}I met my friend, in Preston, at half-past nine; and we started at once for another ramble amongst the poor, in a different part of Trinity Ward.
10126{_}1030{_}We went first to a little court, behind Bell Street.
10126{_}1031{_}There is only one house in the court, and it is known as "Th' Back Heawse."
10126{_}1032{_}In this cottage the little house-things had escaped the ruin which I had witnessed in so many other places.
10126{_}1033{_}There were two small tables, and three chairs; and there were a few pots and a pan or two.
10126{_}1034{_}Upon the cornice there were two pot spaniels, and two painted stone apples; and, between them, there was a sailor waving a union jack, and a little pudgy pot man, for holding tobacco.
10126{_}1035{_}On the windowsill there was a musk-plant; and, upon the table by the staircase, there was a rude cage, containing three young throstles.
10126{_}1036{_}The place was tidy; and there was a kind-looking old couple inside.
10126{_}1037{_}The old man stood at the table in the middle of the floor, washing the pots, and the old woman was wiping them, and putting them away.
10126{_}1038{_}A little lad sat by the fire, thwittling at a piece of stick.
10126{_}1039{_}The old man spoke very few words the whole time we were there, but he kept smiling and going on with his washing.
10126{_}1040{_}The old woman was very civil, and rather shy at first; but we soon got into free talk together.
10126{_}1041{_}She told me that she had borne thirteen children.
10126{_}1042{_}Seven of them were dead; and the other six were all married, and all poor.
10126{_}1043{_}"I have one son," said she; "he's a sailmaker.
10126{_}1044{_}He's th' best off of any of 'em.
10126{_}1045{_}But, Lord bless yo; he's not able to help us.
10126{_}1046{_}He gets very little, and he has to pay a woman to nurse his sick wife.
10126{_}1047{_}.
10126{_}1048{_}.
10126{_}1049{_}.
10126{_}1050{_}This lad that's here,--he's a little grandson o' mine; he's one of my dowter's childer.
10126{_}1051{_}He brings his meight with him every day, an' sleeps with us.
10126{_}1052{_}They han bod one bed, yo see.
10126{_}1053{_}His father hasn't had a stroke o' work sin Christmas.
10126{_}1054{_}They're badly off.
10126{_}1055{_}As for us--my husband has four days a week on th' moor,--that's 4s., an' we've 2s.
10126{_}1056{_}a week to pay out o' that for rent.
10126{_}1057{_}Yo may guess fro that, heaw we are.
10126{_}1058{_}He should ha' been workin' on the moor today, but they've bin rain't off.
10126{_}1059{_}We've no kind o' meight i' this house bod three-ha'poth o' peas; an' we've no firin'.
10126{_}1060{_}He's just brokken up an owd cheer to heat th' watter wi'.
10126{_}1061{_}(The old man smiled at this, as if he thought it was a good joke.)
10126{_}1062{_}He helps me to wesh, an' sick like; an' yo' know, it's a good deal better than gooin' into bad company, isn't it?
10126{_}1063{_}(Here the old man gave her a quiet, approving look, like a good little lad taking notice of his mother's advice.)
10126{_}1064{_}Aw'm very glad of a bit o' help," continued she,"for aw'm not so terrible mich use, mysel'.
10126{_}1065{_}Yo see; aw had a paralytic stroke seven year sin, an' we've not getten ower it.
10126{_}1066{_}For two year aw hadn't a smite o' use all deawn this side.
10126{_}1067{_}One arm an' one leg trail't quite helpless.
10126{_}1068{_}Aw drunk for ever o' stuff for it.
10126{_}1069{_}At last aw gat somethin' ov a yarb doctor.
10126{_}1070{_}He said that he could cure me for a very trifle, an' he did me a deal o' good, sure enough.
10126{_}1071{_}He nobbut charged me hauve-a-creawn.
10126{_}1072{_}.
10126{_}1073{_}.
10126{_}1074{_}.
10126{_}1075{_}We never knowed what it was to want a meal's meight till lately.
10126{_}1076{_}We never had a penny off th' parish, nor never trouble't anybody till neaw.
10126{_}1077{_}Aw wish times would mend, please God!
10126{_}1078{_}.
10126{_}1079{_}.
10126{_}1080{_}.
10126{_}1081{_}We once had a pig, an' was in a nice way o' gettin' a livin'.
10126{_}1082{_}.
10126{_}1083{_}.
10126{_}1084{_}.
10126{_}1085{_}When things began o' gooin' worse an' worse with us, we went to live in a cellar, at sixpence a week rent; and we made it very comfortable, too.
10126{_}1086{_}We didn't go there because we liked th' place; but we thought nobody would know; an, we didn't care, so as we could put on till times mended, an' keep aat o' debt.
10126{_}1087{_}But th' inspectors turned us out, an' we had to come here, an' pay 2s.
10126{_}1088{_}a week.
10126{_}1089{_}.
10126{_}1090{_}.
10126{_}1091{_}.
10126{_}1092{_}Aw do NOT like to ask for charity, iv one could help it.
10126{_}1093{_}They were givin' clothin' up at th' church a while sin', an' some o' th' neighbours wanted me to go an' ax for some singlets, ye see aw cannot do without flannels,--but aw couldn't put th' face on."
10126{_}1094{_}Now, the young throstles in the cage by the staircase began to chirp one after another.
10126{_}1095{_}"Yer yo at that!
10126{_}1096{_}"said the old man, turning round to the cage; "yer yo at that!
10126{_}1097{_}Nobbut three week owd!"
10126{_}1098{_}"Yes," replied the old woman; "they belong to my grandson theer.
10126{_}1099{_}He brought 'em in one day --neest an' all; an' poor nake't crayters they were.
10126{_}1100{_}He's a great lad for birds."
10126{_}1101{_}"He's no worse nor me for that," answered the old man; "aw use't to be terrible fond o' brids when aw wur yung."
10126{_}1102{_}After a little more talk, we bade the old couple good day, and went to peep at the cellar where they had crept stealthily away, for the sake of keeping their expenses close to their lessening income.
10126{_}1103{_}The place was empty, and the door was open.
10126{_}1104{_}It was a damp and cheerless little hole, down in the corner of a dirty court.
10126{_}1105{_}We went next into Pole Street, and tried the door of a cottage where a widow woman lived with her children less than a week before.
10126{_}1106{_}They were gone, and the house was cleared out.
10126{_}1107{_}"They have had neither fire nor candle in that house for weeks past," said my companion.
10126{_}1108{_}We then turned up a narrow entry, which was so dark and low overhead that my companion only told me just in time to "mind my hat!"
10126{_}1109{_}There are several such entries leading out of Pole Street to little courts behind.
10126{_}1110{_}Here we turned into a cold and nearly empty cottage, where a middle-aged woman sat nursing a sick child.
10126{_}1111{_}She looked worn and ill herself, and she had sore eyes.
10126{_}1112{_}She told me that the child was her daughter's.
10126{_}1113{_}Her daughter's husband had died of asthma in the workhouse, about six weeks before.
10126{_}1114{_}He had not "addled" a penny for twelve months before he died.
10126{_}1115{_}She said, "We hed a varra good heawse i' Stanley Street once; but we hed to sell up an' creep hitherto.
10126{_}1116{_}This heawse is 2s.
10126{_}1117{_}3d.
10126{_}1118{_}a week; an' we mun pay it, or go into th' street.
10126{_}1119{_}Aw nobbut owed him for one week, an' he said, 'Iv yo connot pay yo mun turn eawt for thoose 'at will do.'
10126{_}1120{_}Aw did think o' gooin' to th' Board," continued she, "for a pair o' clogs.
10126{_}1121{_}My een are bad; an' awm ill all o'er, an' it's wi' nought but gooin' weet o' my feet.
10126{_}1122{_}My daughter's wortchin'.
10126{_}1123{_}Hoo gets 5s.
10126{_}1124{_}6d.
10126{_}1125{_}a week.
10126{_}1126{_}We han to live an' pay th' rent, too, eawt o' that."
10126{_}1127{_}I guessed, from the little paper pictures on the wall, that they were Catholics.
10126{_}1128{_}In another corner behind Pole Street, we called at a cottage of two rooms, each about three yards square.
10126{_}1129{_}A brother and sister lived together here.
10126{_}1130{_}They were each about fifty years of age.
10126{_}1131{_}They had three female lodgers, factory operatives, out of work.
10126{_}1132{_}The sister said that her brother had been round to the factories that morning, "Thinking that as it wur a pastime, there would haply be somebody off; but he couldn't yer o' nought."
10126{_}1133{_}She said she got a trifle by charing, but not much now; for folks were "beginnin' to do it for theirsels."
10126{_}1134{_}We now turned into Cunliffe Street, and called upon an Irish family there.
10126{_}1135{_}It was a family of seven--an old tailor, and his wife and children.
10126{_}1136{_}They had "dismissed the relief," as he expressed it, "because they got a bit o' work."
10126{_}1137{_}The family was making a little living by ripping up old clothes, and turning the cloth to make it up afresh into lads' caps and other cheap things.
10126{_}1138{_}The old man had had a great deal of trouble with his family.
10126{_}1139{_}"I have one girl," said he, "who has bothered my mind a dale.
10126{_}1140{_}She is under the influence o' bad advice.
10126{_}1141{_}I had her on my hands for many months; an', after that, the furst week's wages she got, she up, an' cut stick, an' left me.
10126{_}1142{_}I have another daughter, now nigh nineteen years of age.
10126{_}1143{_}The trouble I have with her I am content with; because it can't be helped.
10126{_}1144{_}The poor crayter hasn't the use of all her faculties.
10126{_}1145{_}I have taken no end o' pains with her, but I can't get her to count twenty on her finger ends wid a whole life's tachein'.
10126{_}1146{_}Fortune has turned her dark side to me this long time, now; and, bedad, iv it wasn't for contrivin', an' workin' hard to boot, I wouldn't be able to keep above the flood.
10126{_}1147{_}I assure ye it goes agin me to trouble the gentlemen o' the Board; an' so long as I am able, I will not.
10126{_}1148{_}I was born in King's County; an' I was once well off in the city of Waterford I once had 400 pounds in the bank.
10126{_}1149{_}I seen the time I didn't drame of a cloudy day; but things take quare turns in this world.
10126{_}1150{_}How-an-ever, since it's no better, thank God it's no worse.
10126{_}1151{_}Sure, it's a long lane that has never a turn in it."
10126{_}1152{_}CHAPTER X.
10126{_}1153{_}"There's nob'dy but the Lord an' me That knows what I've to bide."
10126{_}1154{_}--NATTERIN NAN.
10126{_}1155{_}The slipshod old tailor shuffled after us to the door, talking about the signs of the times.
10126{_}1156{_}His frame was bowed with age and labour, and his shoulders drooped away.
10126{_}1157{_}It was drawing near the time when the grasshopper would be a burden to him.
10126{_}1158{_}A hard life had silently engraved its faithful records upon that furrowed face; but there was a cheerful ring in his voice which told of a hopeful spirit within him still.
10126{_}1159{_}The old man's nostrils were dusty with snuff, and his poor garments hung about his shrunken form in the careless ease which is common to the tailor's shopboard.
10126{_}1160{_}I could not help admiring the brave old wrinkled workman as he stood in the doorway talking about his secondhand trade, whilst the gusty wind fondled about in his thin gray hair.
10126{_}1161{_}I took a friendly pinch from his little wooden box at parting, and left him to go on struggling with his troublesome family to "keep above the flood," by translating old clothes into new.
10126{_}1162{_}We called at some other houses, where the features of life were so much the same that it is not necessary to say more than that the inhabitants were all workless, or nearly so, and all living upon the charitable provision which is the only thin plank between so many people and death, just now.
10126{_}1163{_}In one house, where the furniture had been sold, the poor souls had brought a great stone into the place, and this was their only seat.
10126{_}1164{_}In Cunliffe Street, we passed the cottage of a boilermaker, whom I had heard of before.
10126{_}1165{_}His family was four in number.
10126{_}1166{_}This was one of those cases of wholesome pride in which the family had struggled with extreme penury, seeking for work in vain, but never asking for charity, until their own poor neighbours were at last so moved with pity for their condition, that they drew the attention of the Relief Committee to it.
10126{_}1167{_}The man accepted relief for one week, but after that, he declined receiving it any longer, because he had met with a promise of employment.
10126{_}1168{_}But the promise failed him when the time came.
10126{_}1169{_}The employer, who had promised, was himself disappointed of the expected work.
10126{_}1170{_}After this; the boilermaker's family was compelled to fall back upon the Relief Committee's allowance.
10126{_}1171{_}He who has never gone hungry about the world, with a strong love of independence in his heart, seeking eagerly for work from day to day, and coming home night after night to a foodless, fireless house, and a starving family, disappointed and desponding, with the gloom of destitution deepening around him, can never fully realise what the feelings of such a man may be from anything that mere words can tell.
10126{_}1172{_}In Park Road, we called at the house of a hand-loom weaver.
10126{_}1173{_}I learnt, before we went in, that two families lived here, numbering together eight persons; and, though it was well known to the committee that they had suffered as severely as any on the relief list, yet their sufferings had been increased by the anonymous slanders of some ill-disposed neighbours.
10126{_}1174{_}They were quiet, well- conducted working people; and these slanders had grieved them very much.
10126{_}1175{_}I found the poor weaver's wife very sensitive on this subject.
10126{_}1176{_}Man's inhumanity to man may be found among the poor sometimes.
10126{_}1177{_}It is not every one who suffers that learns mercy from that suffering.
10126{_}1178{_}As I have said before, the husband was a calico weaver on the hand- loom.
10126{_}1179{_}He had to weave about seventy-three yards of a kind of check for 3s., and a full week's work rarely brought him more than 5s.
10126{_}1180{_}It seems astonishing that a man should stick year after year to such labour as this.
10126{_}1181{_}But there is a strong adhesiveness, mingled with timidity, in some men, which helps to keep them down.
10126{_}1182{_}In the front room of the cottage there was not a single article of furniture left, so far as I can remember.
10126{_}1183{_}The weaver's wife was in the little kitchen, and, knowing the gentleman who was with me, she invited us forward.
10126{_}1184{_}She was a wan woman, with sunken eyes, and she was not much under fifty years of age.
10126{_}1185{_}Her scanty clothing was whole and clean.
10126{_}1186{_}She must have been a very good-looking woman sometime, though she seemed to me as if long years of hard work and poor diet had sapped the foundations of her constitution; and there was a curious changeful blending of pallor and feverish flush upon that worn face.
10126{_}1187{_}But, even in the physical ruins of her countenance, a pleasing expression lingered still.
10126{_}1188{_}She was timid and quiet in her manner at first, as if wondering what we had come for; but she asked me to sit down.
10126{_}1189{_}There was no seat for my friend, and he stood leaning against the wall, trying to get her into easy conversation.
10126{_}1190{_}The little kitchen looked so cheerless and bare that dull morning that it reminded me again of a passage in that rude, racy song of the Lancashire weaver, "Jone o' Greenfeelt"--  "Owd Bill o' Dan's sent us th' baillies one day, For a shop-score aw owed him, at aw couldn't pay; But, he were too lat, for owd Billy at th' Bent Had sent th' tit an' cart, an' taen th' goods off for rent,-- They laft nought but th' owd stoo; It were seats for us two, An' on it keawr't Margit an' me.
10126{_}1191{_}"Then, th' baillies looked reawnd 'em as sly as a meawse, When they see'd at o'th goods had bin taen eawt o' th' heawse; Says tone chap to tother, 'O's gone,--thae may see,'-- Says aw, 'Lads, ne'er fret, for yo're welcome to me!'
10126{_}1192{_}Then they made no moor do, But nipt up wi' owd stoo, An' we both letten thwack upo' th' flags.
10126{_}1193{_}"Then aw said to eawr Margit, while we're upo' the floor, 'We's never be lower i' this world, aw'm sure; Iv ever things awtern they're likely to mend, For aw think i' my heart that we're both at th' fur end; For meight we ban noan, Nor no looms to weighve on, An' egad, they're as good lost as fund.'"
10126{_}1194{_}We had something to do to get the weaver's wife to talk to us freely, and I believe the reason was, that, after the slanders they had been subject to, she harboured a sensitive fear lest anything like doubt should be cast upon her story.
10126{_}1195{_}"Well, Mrs," said my friend, "let's see; how many are you altogether in this house?"
10126{_}1196{_}"We're two families, yo know," replied she; "there's eight on us all altogether."
10126{_}1197{_}"Well," continued he,"and how much have you coming in, now?"
10126{_}1198{_}He had asked this question so oft before, and had so often received the same answer, that the poor soul began to wonder what was the meaning of it all.
10126{_}1199{_}She looked at us silently, her wan face flushed, and then, with tears rising in her eyes, she said, tremulously, "Well, iv yo' cannot believe folk--" My friend stopped her at once, and said, "Nay, Mrs_, you must not think that I doubt your story.
10126{_}1200{_}I know all about it; but my friend wanted me to let you tell it your own way.
10126{_}1201{_}We have come here to do you good, if possible, and no harm.
10126{_}1202{_}You don't need to fear that."
10126{_}1203{_}"Oh, well," said she, slowly wiping her moist forehead, and looking relieved," but yo know, aw was very much put about o'er th' ill-natur't talk as somebody set eawt."
10126{_}1204{_}"Take no notice of them," said my friend; "take no notice.
10126{_}1205{_}I meet with such things every day."
10126{_}1206{_}"Well," continued she," yo know heaw we're situated.
10126{_}1207{_}We were nine months an' hesn't a stroke o' wark.
10126{_}1208{_}Eawr wenches are gettin' a day for t' sick, neaw and then, but that's all.
10126{_}1209{_}There's a brother o' mine lives with us,--he'd a been clemmed into th' grave but for th' relief; an' aw've been many a time an' hesn't put a bit i' my meawth fro mornin' to mornin' again.
10126{_}1210{_}We've bin married twenty-four year; an' aw don't think at him an' me together has spent a shillin' i' drink all that time.
10126{_}1211{_}Why, to tell yo truth, we never had nought to stir on.
10126{_}1212{_}My husband does bod get varra little upo th' hand-loom i' th' best o' times--5s.
10126{_}1213{_}a week or so.
10126{_}1214{_}He weighves a sort o' check--seventy-three yards for 3s."
10126{_}1215{_}The back door opened into a little damp yard, hemmed in by brick walls.
10126{_}1216{_}Over in the next yard we could see a man bustling about, and singing in a loud voice, "Hard times come again no more."
10126{_}1217{_}"Yon fellow doesn't care much about th' hard times, I think," said I.
10126{_}1218{_}"Eh, naw," replied she.
10126{_}1219{_}"He'll live where mony a one would dee, will yon.
10126{_}1220{_}He has that little shop, next dur; an' he keeps sellin' a bit o' toffy, an' then singin' a bit, an' then sellin' a bit moor toffy,--an' he's as happy as a pig amung slutch."
10126{_}1221{_}Leaving the weaver's cottage, the rain came on, and we sat a few minutes with a young shoemaker, who was busy at his bench, doing a cobbling job.
10126{_}1222{_}His wife was lying ill upstairs.
10126{_}1223{_}He had been so short of work for some time past that he had been compelled to apply for relief.
10126{_}1224{_}He complained that the cheap gutta percha shoes were hurting his trade.
10126{_}1225{_}He said a pair of men's gutta percha shoes could be bought for 5s.
10126{_}1226{_}6d., whilst it would cost him 7s.
10126{_}1227{_}6d.
10126{_}1228{_}for the materials alone to make a pair of men's shoes of.
10126{_}1229{_}When the rain was over, we left his house, and as we went along I saw in a cottage window a printed paper containing these words, "Bitter beer.
10126{_}1230{_}This beer is made of herbs and roots of the native country."
10126{_}1231{_}I know that there are many poor people yet in Lancashire who use decoctions of herbs instead of tea--mint and balm are the favourite herbs for this purpose; but I could not imagine what this herb beer could be, at a halfpenny a bottle, unless it was made of nettles.
10126{_}1232{_}At the cottage door there was about four-pennyworth of mauled garden stuff upon an old tray.
10126{_}1233{_}There was nobody inside but a little ragged lass, who could not tell us what the beer was made of.
10126{_}1234{_}She had only one drinking glass in the place, and that had a snip out of the rim.
10126{_}1235{_}The beer was exceedingly bitter.
10126{_}1236{_}We drank as we could, and then went into Pump Street, to the house of a "core-maker," a kind of labourer for moulders.
10126{_}1237{_}The core-maker's wife was in.
10126{_}1238{_}They had four children.
10126{_}1239{_}The whole six had lived for thirteen weeks on 3s.
10126{_}1240{_}6d.
10126{_}1241{_}a week.
10126{_}1242{_}When work first began to fall off, the husband told the visitors who came to inquire into their condition, that he had a little money saved up, and he could manage a while.
10126{_}1243{_}The family lived upon their savings as long as they lasted, and then were compelled to apply for relief, or "clem."
10126{_}1244{_}It was not quite noon when we left this house, and my friend proposed that before we went farther we should call upon Mrs G_, an interesting old woman, in Cunliffe Street.
10126{_}1245{_}We turned back to the place, and there we found  "In lowly shed, and mean attire, A matron old, whom we schoolmistress name, Who boasts unruly brats with birch to tame."
10126{_}1246{_}In a small room fronting the street, the mild old woman sat, with her bed in one corner, and her simple vassals ranged upon the forms around.
10126{_}1247{_}Here, "with quaint arts," she swayed the giddy crowd of little imprisoned elves, whilst they fretted away their irksome schooltime, and unconsciously played their innocent prelude to the serious drama of life.
10126{_}1248{_}As we approach the open door--  "The noises intermix'd, which thence resound, Do learning's little tenement betray; Where sits the dame disguised in look profound, And eyes her fairy throng, and turns her wheel around."
10126{_}1249{_}The venerable little woman had lived in this house fourteen years.
10126{_}1250{_}She was seventy-three years of age, and a native of Limerick.
10126{_}1251{_}She was educated at St Ann's School, in Dublin, and she had lived fourteen years in the service of a lady in that city.
10126{_}1252{_}The old dame made an effort to raise her feeble form when we entered, and she received us as courteously as the finest lady in the land could have done.
10126{_}1253{_}She told us that she charged only a penny a-week for her teaching; but, said she, "some of them can't pay it."
10126{_}1254{_}"There's a poor child," continued she, "his father has been out of work eleven months, and they are starving but for the relief.
10126{_}1255{_}Still, I do get a little, and I like to have the children about me.
10126{_}1256{_}Oh, my case is not the worst, I know.
10126{_}1257{_}I have people lodging in the house who are not so well off as me.
10126{_}1258{_}I have three families living here.
10126{_}1259{_}One is a family of four; they have only 3s.
10126{_}1260{_}a-week to live upon.
10126{_}1261{_}Another is a family of three; they have 6s.
10126{_}1262{_}a-week from a club, but they pay me 2s.
10126{_}1263{_}a- week.
10126{_}1264{_}for rent out of that.
10126{_}1265{_}.
10126{_}1266{_}.
10126{_}1267{_}.
10126{_}1268{_}.
10126{_}1269{_}I am very much troubled with my eyes; my sight is failing fast.
10126{_}1270{_}If I drop a stitch when I'm knitting, I can't see to take it up again.
10126{_}1271{_}If I could buy a pair of spectacles, they would help me a good dale; but I cannot afford till times are better."
10126{_}1272{_}I could not help thinking how many kind souls there are in the world who would be glad to give the old woman a pair of spectacles, if they knew her.
10126{_}1273{_}CHAPTER XI.
10126{_}1274{_}We talked with the old schoolmistress in Cunliffe Street till it was "high twelve" at noon, and then the kind jailer of learning's little prison-house let all her fretful captives go.
10126{_}1275{_}The clamorous elves rushed through the doorway into the street, like a stream too big for its vent, rejoicing in their new-found freedom and the open face of day.
10126{_}1276{_}The buzz of the little teaching mill was hushed once more, and the old dame laid her knitting down, and quietly wiped her weak and weary eyes.
10126{_}1277{_}The daughters of music were brought low with her, but, in the last thin treble of second childhood, she trembled forth mild complaints of her neighbours' troubles, but very little of her own.
10126{_}1278{_}We left her to enjoy her frugal meal and her noontide reprieve in peace, and came back to the middle of the town.
10126{_}1279{_}On our way I noticed again some features of street life which are more common in manufacturing towns just now than when times are good.
10126{_}1280{_}Now and then one meets with a man in the dress of a factory worker selling newspapers, or religious tracts, or back numbers of the penny periodicals, which do not cost much.
10126{_}1281{_}It is easy to see, from their shy and awkward manner, that they are new to the trade, and do not like it.
10126{_}1282{_}They are far less dexterous, and much more easily "said," than the brisk young salesmen who hawk newspapers in the streets of Manchester.
10126{_}1283{_}I know that many of these are unemployed operatives trying to make an honest penny in this manner till better days return.
10126{_}1284{_}Now and then, too, a grown-up girl trails along the street, "with wandering steps and slow," ragged, and soiled, and starved, and looking as if she had travelled far in the rainy weather, houseless and forlorn.
10126{_}1285{_}I know that such sights may be seen at any time, but not near so often as just now; and I cannot help thinking that many of these are poor sheep which have strayed away from the broken folds of labour.
10126{_}1286{_}Sometimes it is an older woman that goes by, with a child at the breast, and one or two holding by the skirt of her tattered gown, and perhaps one or two more limping after, as she crawls along the pavement, gazing languidly from side to side among the heedless crowd, as if giving her last look round the world for help, without knowing where to get it, and without heart to ask for it.
10126{_}1287{_}It is easy to give wholesale reasons why nobody needs to be in such a condition as this; but it is not improbable that there are some poor souls who, from no fault of their own, drop through the great sieve of charity into utter destitution.
10126{_}1288{_}"They are well kept that God keeps."
10126{_}1289{_}May the continual dew of Heaven's blessing gladden the hearts of those who deal kindly with them!
10126{_}1290{_}After dinner I fell into company with some gentlemen who were talking about the coming guild--that ancient local festival, which is so clear to the people of Preston, that they are not likely to allow it to go by wholly unhonoured, however severe the times may be.
10126{_}1291{_}Amongst them was a gray-haired friend of mine, who is a genuine humorist.
10126{_}1292{_}He told us many quaint anecdotes.
10126{_}1293{_}One of them was of a man who went to inquire the price of graves in a certain cemetery.
10126{_}1294{_}The sexton told him that they were 1 pound on this side, and 2 pounds on the other side of the knoll.
10126{_}1295{_}"How is it that they are 2 pounds on the other side?"
10126{_}1296{_}inquired the man.
10126{_}1297{_}"Well, becose there's a better view there," replied the sexton.
10126{_}1298{_}There were three or four millowners in the company, and, when the conversation turned upon the state of trade, one of them said, "I admit that there is a great deal of distress, but we are not so badly off yet as to drive the operatives to work for reasonable wages.
10126{_}1299{_}For instance, I had a labourer working for me at 10s.
10126{_}1300{_}a-week; he threw up my employ, and went to work upon the moor for 1s.
10126{_}1301{_}a-day.
10126{_}1302{_}How do you account for that?
10126{_}1303{_}And then, again, I had another man employed as a watchman and roller coverer, at 18s.
10126{_}1304{_}a-week.
10126{_}1305{_}I found that I couldn't afford to keep him on at 18s., so I offered him 15s.
10126{_}1306{_}a-week; but he left it, and went to work on the moor at 1s.
10126{_}1307{_}a-day; and, just now, I want a man to take his place, and cannot get one."
10126{_}1308{_}Another said, "I am only giving low wages to my workpeople, but they get more with me than they can make on the moor, and yet I cannot keep them."
10126{_}1309{_}I heard some other things of the same kind, for which there might be special reasons; but these gentlemen admitted the general prevalence of severe distress, and the likelihood of its becoming much worse.
10126{_}1310{_}At two o'clock I sallied forth again, under convoy of another member of the Relief Committee, into the neighbourhood of Messrs Horrocks, Miller, and Co.'s works.
10126{_}1311{_}Their mill is known as "Th' Yard Factory."
10126{_}1312{_}Hereabouts the people generally are not so much reduced as in some parts of the town, because they have had more employment, until lately, than has been common elsewhere.
10126{_}1313{_}But our business lay with those distressed families who were in receipt of relief, and, even here, they were very easy to find.
10126{_}1314{_}The first house we called at was inhabited by a family of five--man and wife and three children.
10126{_}1315{_}The man was working on the moor at one shilling a-day.
10126{_}1316{_}The wife was unwell, but she was moving about the house.
10126{_}1317{_}They had buried one girl three weeks before; and one of the three remaining children lay ill of the measles.
10126{_}1318{_}They had suffered a great deal from sickness.
10126{_}1319{_}The wife said, "My husband is a peawer-loom weighver.
10126{_}1320{_}He had to come whoam ill fro' his wark; an' then they shopped his looms, (gave his work to somebody else,) an' he couldn't get 'em back again.
10126{_}1321{_}He'll get 'em back as soon as he con, yo may depend; for we don't want to bother folk for no mak o' relief no lunger than we can help."
10126{_}1322{_}In addition to the husband's pay upon the moor, they were receiving 2s.
10126{_}1323{_}a week from the Committee, making altogether 8s.
10126{_}1324{_}a week for the five, with 2s.
10126{_}1325{_}6d.
10126{_}1326{_}to pay out of it for rent.
10126{_}1327{_}She said, "We would rayther ha' soup than coffee, becose there's moor heytin' in it."
10126{_}1328{_}My friend looked in at the door of a cottage in Barton Street.
10126{_}1329{_}There was a sickly-looking woman inside.
10126{_}1330{_}"Well, missis," said my friend, jocularly, "how are you?
10126{_}1331{_}because, if you're ill, I've brought a doctor here."
10126{_}1332{_}"Eh," replied she, "aw could be ill in a minute, if aw could afford, but these times winnot ston doctors' bills.
10126{_}1333{_}Besides, aw never were partial to doctors' physic; it's kitchen physic at aw want.
10126{_}1334{_}Han yo ony o' that mak' wi' yo?"
10126{_}1335{_}She said," My husban' were th' o'erlooker o' th'weighvers at "Owd Tom's.'
10126{_}1336{_}They stopt to fettle th' engine a while back, an' they'n never started sin'.
10126{_}1337{_}But aw guess they wi'n do some day."
10126{_}1338{_}We had not many yards to go to the next place, which was a poor cottage in Fletcher's Row, where a family of eight persons resided.
10126{_}1339{_}There was very little furniture in the place, but I noticed a small shelf of books in a corner by the window.
10126{_}1340{_}A feeble woman, upwards of seventy years old, sat upon a stool tending the cradle of a sleeping infant.
10126{_}1341{_}This infant was the youngest of five children, the oldest of the five was seven years of age.
10126{_}1342{_}The mother of the three-weeks-old infant had just gone out to the mill to claim her work from the person who had been filling her place during her confinement.
10126{_}1343{_}The old woman said that the husband was "a grinder in a card-room when they geet wed, an' he addled about 8s.
10126{_}1344{_}a week; but, after they geet wed, his wife larn't him to weighve upo' th' peawer-looms."
10126{_}1345{_}She said that she was no relation to them, but she nursed, and looked after the house for them.
10126{_}1346{_}"They connot afford to pay mo nought," continued she, "but aw fare as they fare'n, an' they dunnot want to part wi' me.
10126{_}1347{_}Aw'm not good to mich, but aw can manage what they wanten, yo see'n.
10126{_}1348{_}Aw never trouble't noather teawn nor country i' my life, an' aw hope aw never shall for the bit o' time aw have to do on."
10126{_}1349{_}She said that the Board of Guardians had allowed the family 10s.
10126{_}1350{_}a week for the two first weeks of the wife's confinement, but now their income amounted to a little less than one shilling a head per week.
10126{_}1351{_}Leaving this house, we turned round the corner into St Mary's Street North.
10126{_}1352{_}Here we found a clean-looking young working man standing shivering by a cottage door, with his hands in his pockets.
10126{_}1353{_}He was dressed in well-mended fustian, and he had a cloth cap on his head.
10126{_}1354{_}His face had a healthy hunger-nipt look.
10126{_}1355{_}"Hollo," said my friend, "I thought you was working on the moor."
10126{_}1356{_}"Ay," replied the young man, "Aw have bin, but we'n bin rain't off this afternoon."
10126{_}1357{_}"Is there nobody in?"
10126{_}1358{_}said my friend.
10126{_}1359{_}"Naw, my wife's gone eawt; hoo'll not be mony minutes.
10126{_}1360{_}Hoo's here neaw."
10126{_}1361{_}A clean little pale woman came up, with a child in her arms, and we went in.
10126{_}1362{_}They had not much furniture in the small kitchen, which was the only place we saw, but everything was sweet and orderly.
10126{_}1363{_}Their income was, as usual in relief cases, about one shilling a head per week.
10126{_}1364{_}"You had some lodgers," said my friend.
10126{_}1365{_}"Ay," said she,"but they're gone."
10126{_}1366{_}"How's that?"
10126{_}1367{_}"We had a few words.
10126{_}1368{_}Their little lad was makin' a great noise i' the passage theer, an' aw were very ill o' my yed, an' aw towd him to go an' play him at tother side o' th' street,--so, they took it amiss, an' went to lodge wi' some folk i' Ribbleton Lone."
10126{_}1369{_}We called at another house in this street.
10126{_}1370{_}A family of six lived there.
10126{_}1371{_}The only furniture I saw in the place was two chairs, a table, a large stool, a cheap clock, and a few pots.
10126{_}1372{_}The man and his wife were in.
10126{_}1373{_}She was washing.
10126{_}1374{_}The man was a stiff built, shock- headed little fellow, with a squint in his eye that seemed to enrich the good-humoured expression of his countenance.
10126{_}1375{_}Sitting smiling by the window, he looked as if he had lots of fun in him, if he only had a fair chance of letting it off.
10126{_}1376{_}He told us that he was a "tackler" by trade.
10126{_}1377{_}A tackler is one who fettles looms when they get out of order.
10126{_}1378{_}"Couldn't you get on at Horrocks's?"
10126{_}1379{_}said my friend.
10126{_}1380{_}"Naw," replied he; "they'n not ha' men-weighvers theer."
10126{_}1381{_}The wife said," We're a deal better off than some.
10126{_}1382{_}He has six days a week upo th' moor, an' we'n 3s.
10126{_}1383{_}a week fro th' Relief Committee.
10126{_}1384{_}We'n 2s.
10126{_}1385{_}6d.
10126{_}1386{_}a week to pay eawt on it for rent; but then, we'n a lad that gets 4d.
10126{_}1387{_}a day neaw an' then for puttin' bobbins on; an' every little makes a mickle, yo known."
10126{_}1388{_}"How is it that your clock's stopt?"
10126{_}1389{_}said I.
10126{_}1390{_}"Nay," said the little fellow; "aw don't know.
10126{_}1391{_}Want o' cotton, happen,--same as everything else is stopt for."
10126{_}1392{_}Leaving this house we met with another member of the Relief Committee, who was overlooker of a mill a little way off.
10126{_}1393{_}I parted here with the gentleman who had accompanied me hitherto, and the overlooker went on with me.
10126{_}1394{_}In Newton Street he stopped, and said, "Let's look in here."
10126{_}1395{_}We went up two steps, and met a young woman coming out at the cottage door.
10126{_}1396{_}"How's Ruth?"
10126{_}1397{_}said my friend.
10126{_}1398{_}"Well, hoo is here.
10126{_}1399{_}Hoo's busy bakin' for Betty."
10126{_}1400{_}We went in.
10126{_}1401{_}"You're not bakin' for yourselves, then?"
10126{_}1402{_}said he.
10126{_}1403{_}"Eh, naw," replied the young woman," it's mony a year sin' we had a bakin' o' fleawr, isn't it, Ruth?"
10126{_}1404{_}The old woman who was baking turned round and said, "Ay; an' it'll be mony another afore we han one aw deawt."
10126{_}1405{_}There were three dirty-looking hens picking and croodling about the cottage floor.
10126{_}1406{_}"How is it you don't sell these, or else eat 'em?"
10126{_}1407{_}said he.
10126{_}1408{_}"Eh, dear," replied the old woman, "dun yo want mo kilt?
10126{_}1409{_}He's had thoose hens mony a year; an' they rooten abeawt th' heawse just th' same as greadley Christians.
10126{_}1410{_}He did gi' consent for one on 'em to be kilt yesterday; but aw'll be hanged iv th' owd cracky didn't cry like a chylt when he see'd it beawt yed.
10126{_}1411{_}He'd as soon part wi' one o'th childer as one o'th hens.
10126{_}1412{_}He says they're so mich like owd friends, neaw.
10126{_}1413{_}He's as quare as Dick's hat-bant 'at went nine times reawnd an' wouldn't tee.
10126{_}1414{_}.
10126{_}1415{_}.
10126{_}1416{_}.
10126{_}1417{_}We thought we'd getten a shop for yon lad o' mine t'other day.
10126{_}1418{_}We yerd ov a chap at Lytham at wanted a lad to tak care o' six jackasses an' a pony.
10126{_}1419{_}Th' pony were to tak th' quality to Blackpool, and such like.
10126{_}1420{_}So we fettled th' lad's bits o' clooas up and made him ever so daycent, and set him off to try to get on wi' th' chap at Lytham.
10126{_}1421{_}Well, th' lad were i' good heart abeawt it; an' when he geet theer th' chap towd him at he thought he wur very likely for th' job, so that made it better,--an' th' lad begun o' wearin' his bit o' brass o' summat to eat, an' sich like, thinkin' he're sure o' th' shop.
10126{_}1422{_}Well, they kept him there, dallyin', aw tell yo, an' never tellin' him a greadley tale, fro Sunday till Monday o' th' neet, an' then,--lo an' behold,--th' mon towd him that he'd hire't another; and th' lad had to come trailin' whoam again, quite deawn i'th' meawth.
10126{_}1423{_}Eh, aw wur some mad!
10126{_}1424{_}Iv aw'd been at th' back o' that chap, aw could ha' punce't him, see yo!"
10126{_}1425{_}"Well," said my friend, "there's no work yet, Ruth, is there?"
10126{_}1426{_}"Wark!
10126{_}1427{_}naw; nor never will be no moor, aw believe."
10126{_}1428{_}"Hello, Ruth!"
10126{_}1429{_}said the young woman, pointing through the window, "dun yo know who yon is?"
10126{_}1430{_}"Know?
10126{_}1431{_}ay," replied the old woman; "He's getten aboon porritch neaw, has yon.
10126{_}1432{_}He walks by me i'th street, as peart as a pynot, an' never cheeps.
10126{_}1433{_}But, he's no 'casion.
10126{_}1434{_}Aw know'd him when his yure stickt out at top ov his hat; and his shurt would ha' hanged eawt beheend, too,--like a Wigan lantron,--iv he'd had a shurt."
10126{_}1435{_}CHAPTER XII.
10126{_}1436{_}"Oh, reason not the deed; our basest beggars Are in the poorest things superfluous: Allow not nature more than nature needs, Man's life is cheap as beast's."
10126{_}1437{_}--King Lear.
10126{_}1438{_}A short fit of rain came on whilst we were in the cottage in Newton Street, so we sat a little while with Ruth, listening to her quaint tattle about the old man and his feathered pets; about the children, the hard times, and her own personal ailments;--for, though I could not help thinking her a very good-hearted, humorous old woman, bravely disposed to fight it out with the troubles of her humble lot, yet it was clear that she was inclined to ease her harassed mind now and then by a little wholesome grumbling; and I dare say that sometimes she might lose her balance so far as to think, like "Natterin' Nan," "No livin' soul atop o't earth's bin tried as I've bin tried:  there's nob'dy but the Lord an' me that knows what I've to bide."
10126{_}1439{_}Old age and infirmity, too, had found Ruth out, in her penurious obscurity; and she was disposed to complain a little, like Nan, sometimes, of "the ills that flesh is heir to:"-  "Fro' t' wind i't stomach, rheumatism, Tengin pains i't gooms, An' coughs, an' cowds, an' t' spine o't back, I suffer martyrdom.
10126{_}1440{_}"Yet nob'dy pities mo, or thinks I'm ailin' owt at all; T' poor slave mun tug an' tew wi't wark, Wolivver shoo can crawl."
10126{_}1441{_}Old Ruth was far from being as nattle and querulous as the famous ill-natured grumbler so racily pictured by Benjamin Preston, of Bradford; but, like most of the dwellers upon earth, she was a little bit touched with the same complaint.
10126{_}1442{_}When the rain was over, we came away.
10126{_}1443{_}I cannot say that the weather ever "cleared up" that day; for, at the end of every shower, the dark, slow-moving clouds always seemed to be mustering for another downfall.
10126{_}1444{_}We came away, and left the "cant" old body "busy bakin' for Betty," and "shooing" the hens away from her feet, and she shuffled about the house.
10126{_}1445{_}A few yards lower in Newton Street, we turned up a low, dark entry, which led to a gloomy little court behind.
10126{_}1446{_}This was one of those unhealthy, pent-up cloisters, where misery stagnates and broods among the "foul congregation of pestilential vapours" which haunt the backdoor life of the poorest parts of great towns.
10126{_}1447{_}Here, those viewless ministers of health--the fresh winds of heaven--had no free play; and poor human nature inhaled destruction from the poisonous effluvia that festered there.
10126{_}1448{_}And, in such nooks as this, there may be found many decent working people, who have been accustomed to live a cleanly life in their humble way in healthy quarters, now reduced to extreme penury, pinching, and pining, and nursing the flickering hope of better days, which may enable them to flee from the foul harbour which strong necessity has driven them to.
10126{_}1449{_}The dark aspect of the day filled the court with a tomb-like gloom.
10126{_}1450{_}If I remember aright, there were only three or four cottages in it.
10126{_}1451{_}We called at two of them.
10126{_}1452{_}Before we entered the first, my friend said, "A young couple lives here.
10126{_}1453{_}They are very decent people.
10126{_}1454{_}They have not been here long; and they have gone through a great deal before they came here."
10126{_}1455{_}There were two or three pot ornaments on the cornice; but there was no furniture in the place, save one chair, which was occupied by a pale young woman, nursing her child.
10126{_}1456{_}Her thin, intelligent face looked very sad.
