10126{_}2113{_}Four rough lads were gathered round a brown dish, which stood upon a little deal table in the middle of the floor.
10126{_}2114{_}These four were round-headed little fellows, all teeming with life.
10126{_}2115{_}"Yon catched us eawt o'flunters, (out of order,)" said the poor woman when we entered; "but what con a body do?"
10126{_}2116{_}We were begging that she would not disturb herself, when one of the lads at the table called out, "Mother; look at eawr John.
10126{_}2117{_}He keeps pushin' me off th' cheer!"
10126{_}2118{_}"Eh, John," replied she; "I wish thy feyther were here!
10126{_}2119{_}Thae'rt olez tormentin' that lad.
10126{_}2120{_}Do let him alone, wilto--or else aw'll poo that toppin' o' thine, smartly--aw will!
10126{_}2121{_}An' do see iv yo connot behave yorsels!"
10126{_}2122{_}"Well," said John; "he keeps takkin' my puddin'!"
10126{_}2123{_}"Eh, what a story," replied the other little fellow; "it wur thee, neaw!"
10126{_}2124{_}" Aw'll tell yo what it is," said the mother, "iv yo two connot agree, an' get your dinner quietly, aw'll tak that dish away; an' yo'st not have another bite this day.
10126{_}2125{_}Heaw con yo for shame!"
10126{_}2126{_}This quietened the lads a little, and they went on with their dinner.
10126{_}2127{_}At another little table under the back window, two girls stood, dining off one plate.
10126{_}2128{_}The children were all eating a kind of light pudding, known in Lancashire by the name of "Berm-bo," or, "Berm-dumplin'," made of flour and yeast, mixed with a little suet.
10126{_}2129{_}The poor woman said that her children were all "hearty-etten," (all hearty eaters,) especially the lads; and she hardly knew what to make for them, so as to have enough for the whole.
10126{_}2130{_}"Berm-dumplin'," was as satisfying as anything that she could get, and it would "stick to their ribs" better than "ony mak o' swill;" besides, the children liked it.
10126{_}2131{_}Speaking of her husband, she said, "He were eawt o' wark a good while; but he geet a shop at last, at Blackrod, abeawt four mile off Wigan.
10126{_}2132{_}When he went a-wortchin' to Blackrod, at first, nought would sarve but he would walk theer an' back every day, so as to save lodgin' brass,--an sich like.
10126{_}2133{_}Aw shouldn't ha' care't iv it had nobbut bin a mile, or two even; for aw'd far rayther that he had his meals comfortable awhoam, an' his bits o' clooas put reet; but Lord bless yo,--eight mile a day, beside a hard day's wark,--it knocked him up at last,--it were so like.
10126{_}2134{_}He kept sayin', 'Oh, he could do it,' an' sich like; but aw could see that he were fair killin' hissel', just for the sake o' comin' to his own whoam ov a neet; an' for th' sake o' savin' two or three shillin'; so at last aw turned Turk, an' made him tak lodgin's theer.
10126{_}2135{_}Aw'd summut to do to persuade him at first, an' aw know that he's as whoam-sick as a chylt that's lost its mother, just this minute; but then, what's th' matter o' that,--it wouldn't do for mo to have him laid up, yo known.
10126{_}2136{_}.
10126{_}2137{_}.
10126{_}2138{_}.
10126{_}2139{_}Oh, he's a very feelin' mon.
10126{_}2140{_}Aw've sin him when he couldn't finish his bit o' dinner for thinkin' o' somebody that were clemmin'."
10126{_}2141{_}Speaking of the hardships the family had experienced, she said, "Eh, bless yo!
10126{_}2142{_}There's some folk can sit i'th heawse an' send their childer to prow eawt a-beggin' in a mornin', regilar,--but eawr childer wouldn't do it,--an', iv they would, aw wouldn' let 'em,-- naw, not iv we were clemmin' to deeoth,--to my thinkin'."
10126{_}2143{_}The woman was quite right.
10126{_}2144{_}Among the hard-tried operatives of Lancashire I have seen several instances in which they have gone out daily to beg; and some rare cases, even, in which they have stayed moodily at home themselves and sent their children forth to beg; and anybody living in this county will have noticed the increase of mendicancy there, during the last few months.
10126{_}2145{_}No doubt professional beggars have taken large advantage of this unhappy time to work upon the sympathies of those easy givers who cannot bear to hear the wail of distress, however simulated--who prefer giving at once, because it "does their own hearts good," to the trouble of inquiring or the pain of refusing,--who would rather relieve twenty rogues than miss the blessing of one honest soul who was ready to perish,--those kind-hearted, free-handed scatterers of indiscriminate benevolence who are the keen-eyed, whining cadger's chief support, his standing joke, and favourite prey; and who are more than ever disposed to give to whomsoever shall ask of them in such a season as this.
10126{_}2146{_}All the mendicancy which appears on our streets does not belong to the suffering operatives of Lancashire.
10126{_}2147{_}But, apart from those poor, miserable crawlers in the gutters of life, who live by habitual and unnecessary beggary, great and continued adversity is a strong test of the moral tone of any people.
10126{_}2148{_}Extreme poverty, and the painful things which follow in its train--these are "bad to bide" with the best of mankind.
10126{_}2149{_}Besides, there are always some people who, from causes within themselves, are continually at their wits' end to keep the wolf from the door, even when employment is plentiful with them; and there are some natures too weak to bear any long strain of unusual poverty without falling back upon means of living which, in easy circumstances, they would have avoided, if not despised.
10126{_}2150{_}It is one evil of the heavy pressure of the times; for there is fear that among such as these, especially the young and plastic, some may become so familiar with that beggarly element which was offensive to their minds at first--may so lose the tone of independent pride, and become "subdued to what they work in, like the dyer's hand,"--that they may learn to look upon mendicancy as an easy source of support hereafter, even in times of less difficulty than the present.
10126{_}2151{_}Happily, such weakness as this is not characteristic of the English people; but "they are well kept that God keeps," and perhaps it would not be wise to cramp the hand of relief too much at a time like this, to a people who have been, and will be yet, the hope and glory of the land.
10126{_}2152{_}CHAPTER XX.
10126{_}2153{_}"Poor Tom's a-cold!
10126{_}2154{_}Who gives anything to poor Tom?"
10126{_}2155{_}--King Lear.
10126{_}2156{_}One sometimes meets with remarkable differences of condition in the households of poor folk, which stand side by side in the same street.
10126{_}2157{_}I am not speaking of the uncertain shelters of those who struggle upon the skirts of civilisation, in careless, uncared-for wretchedness, without settled homes, or regular occupation,--the miserable camp followers of life's warfare,--living habitually from hand to mouth, in a reckless wrestle with the world, for mere existence.
10126{_}2158{_}I do not mean these, but the households of our common working people.
10126{_}2159{_}Amongst the latter one sometimes meets with striking differences, in cleanliness, furniture, manners, intellectual acquirements, and that delicate compound of mental elements called taste.
10126{_}2160{_}Even in families whose earnings have been equal in the past, and who are just now subject alike to the same pinch of adversity, these disparities are sometimes very great.
10126{_}2161{_}And, although there are cases in which the immediate causes of these differences are evident enough in the habits of the people, yet, in others, the causes are so obscure, that the wisest observer would be most careful in judging respecting them.
10126{_}2162{_}I saw an example of this in a little bye- street, at the upper end of Scholes--a quarter of Wigan where the poorest of the poor reside, and where many decent working people have lately been driven for cheap shelter by the stress of the times.
10126{_}2163{_}Scholes is one of those ash-pits of human life which may be found in almost any great town; where, among a good deal of despised stuff, which by wise treatment might possibly be made useful to the world, many a jewel gets accidentally thrown away, and lost.
10126{_}2164{_}This bye-street of mean brick cottages had an unwholesome, outcast look; and the sallow, tattered women, lounging about the doorways, and listlessly watching the sickly children in the street, evinced the prevalence of squalor and want there.
10126{_}2165{_}The very children seemed joyless at their play; and everything that met the eye foretold that there was little chance of finding anything in that street but poverty in its most prostrate forms.
10126{_}2166{_}But, even in this unpromising spot, I met with an agreeable surprise.
10126{_}2167{_}The first house we entered reminded me of those clean, lone dwellings, up in the moorland nooks of Lancashire, where the sweet influences of nature have free play; where the people have a hereditary hatred of dirt and disorder; and where, even now, many of the hardy mountain folk are half farmers, half woollen weavers, doing their weaving in their own quiet houses, where the smell of the heather and the song of the wild bird floats in at the workman's window, blent with the sounds of rindling waters,--doing their weaving in green sequestered nooks, where the low of kine, and the cry of the moorfowl can be heard; and bearing the finished "cuts" home upon their backs to the distant town.
10126{_}2168{_}All was so bright in this little cottage,--so tidy and serene,--that the very air seemed clearer there than in the open street.
10126{_}2169{_}The humble furniture, good of its kind, was all shiny with "elbow grease," and some parts of it looked quaint and well-preserved, like the heirlooms of a careful cottage ancestry.
10126{_}2170{_}The well polished fire-irons, and other metal things, seemed to gather up the diffuse daylight and fling it back in concentrated radiances that illuminated the shady cottage with cheerful beauty.
10126{_}2171{_}The little shelf of books, the gleaming window, with its healthy pot flowers, the perfect order, and the trim sweetness of everything, reminded me, as I have said, of the better sort of houses where simple livers dwell, up among the free air of the green hills--those green hills of Lancashire, the remembrance of which will always stir my heart as long as it can stir to anything.
10126{_}2172{_}This cottage, in comparison with most of those which I had seen in Scholes, looked like a glimpse of the star-lit blue peeping through the clouds on a gloomy night.
10126{_}2173{_}I found that it was the house of a widower, a weaver of diaper, who was left with a family of eight children to look after.
10126{_}2174{_}Two little girls were in the house, and they were humbly but cleanly clad.
10126{_}2175{_}One of them called her father up from the cellar, where he was working at his looms.
10126{_}2176{_}He was a mild, thoughtful-looking man, something past middle age.
10126{_}2177{_}I could not help admiring him as he stood in the middle of the floor with his unsleeved arms folded, uttering quiet jets of simple speech to my friend, who had known him before.
10126{_}2178{_}He said that he hardly ever got anything to do now, but when he was at work he could make about 7s.
10126{_}2179{_}2d.
10126{_}2180{_}a week by weaving two cuts.
10126{_}2181{_}He was receiving six tickets weekly from the Relief Committee, which, except the proceeds of a little employment now and then, was all that the family of nine had to depend upon for food, firing, clothes, and rent.
10126{_}2182{_}He said that he was forced to make every little spin out as far as it would; but it kept him bare and busy, and held his nose "everlastingly deawn to th' grindlestone."
10126{_}2183{_}But he didn't know that it was any use complaining about a thing that neither master nor man could help.
10126{_}2184{_}He durst say that he could manage to grin and bide till things came round, th' same as other folk had to do.
10126{_}2185{_}Grumbling, in a case like this, was like "fo'in eawt wi' th' elements," (quarrelling with a storm.)
10126{_}2186{_}One of his little girls was on her knees, cleaning the floor.
10126{_}2187{_}She stopped a minute, to look at my friend and me.
10126{_}2188{_}"Come, my lass," said her father, "get on wi' thi weshin'."
10126{_}2189{_}"I made application for th' watchman's place at Leyland Mill," continued he, "but I wur to lat.
10126{_}2190{_}.
10126{_}2191{_}.
10126{_}2192{_}.
10126{_}2193{_}There's nought for it," continued he, as we came out of the house, "there's nought for it but to keep one's een oppen, an' do as weel as they con, till it blows o'er."
10126{_}2194{_}A few yards from this house, we looked in at a slip of a cottage, at the corner of the row.
10126{_}2195{_}It was like a slice off some other cottage, stuck on at the end of the rest, to make up the measure of the street; for it was less than two yards wide, by about four yards long.
10126{_}2196{_}There was only one small window, close to the door, and it was shrouded by a dingy cotton blind.
10126{_}2197{_}When we first entered, I could hardly see what there was in that gloomy cell; but when the eyes became acquainted with the dimness within, we found that there was neither fire nor furniture in the place, except at the far end, where an old sick woman lay gasping upon three chairs, thinly covered from the cold.
10126{_}2198{_}She was dying of asthma.
10126{_}2199{_}At her right hand there was another rickety chair, by the help of which she raised herself up from her hard bed.
10126{_}2200{_}She said that she had never been up stairs during the previous twelve months, but had lain there, at the foot of the stairs, all that time.
10126{_}2201{_}She had two daughters.
10126{_}2202{_}They were both out of the house; and they had been out of work a long time.
10126{_}2203{_}One of them had gone to Miss B_'s to learn to sew.
10126{_}2204{_}"She gets her breakfast before she starts," said the old woman, "an' she takes a piece o' bread with her, to last for th' day."
10126{_}2205{_}It was a trouble to her to talk much, so we did not stop long; but I could not help feeling sorry that the poor old soul had not a little more comfort to smooth her painful passage to the grave.
10126{_}2206{_}On our way from this place, we went into a cottage near the "Coal Yard," where a tall, thin Irishwoman was washing some tattered clothes, whilst her children played about the gutter outside.
10126{_}2207{_}This was a family of seven, and they were all out of work, except the father, who was away, trying to make a trifle by hawking writing-paper and envelopes.
10126{_}2208{_}This woman told us that she was in great trouble about one of her children--the eldest daughter, now grown up to womanhood.
10126{_}2209{_}"She got married to a sailor about two year ago," said she, "an' he wint away a fortnit after, an' never was heard of since.
10126{_}2210{_}She never got the scrape ov a pen from him to say was he alive or dead.
10126{_}2211{_}She never heard top nor tail of him since he wint from her; an' the girl is just pinin' away."
10126{_}2212{_}Poor folk have their full share of the common troubles of life, apart from the present distress.
10126{_}2213{_}The next place we visited was the "Fleece Yard," another of those unhealthy courts, of which there are so many in Scholes--where poverty and dirt unite to make life doubly miserable.
10126{_}2214{_}In this yard we went up three or four steps into a little disorderly house, where a family of eleven was crowded.
10126{_}2215{_}Not one of the eleven was earning anything except the father, who was working for ls.
10126{_}2216{_}3d.
10126{_}2217{_}a day.
10126{_}2218{_}In addition to this the family received four tickets weekly from the Relief Committee.
10126{_}2219{_}There were several of the children in, and they looked brisk and healthy, in spite of the dirt and discomfort of the place; but the mother was sadly "torn down" by the cares of her large family.
10126{_}2220{_}The house had a sickly smell.
10126{_}2221{_}Close to the window, a little, stiff built, bullet-headed lad stood, stript to the waist, sputtering and splashing as he washed himself in a large bowl of water, placed upon a stool.
10126{_}2222{_}By his side there was another lad three or four years older, and the two were having a bit of famous fun together, quite heedless of all else.
10126{_}2223{_}The elder kept ducking the little fellow's head into the water, upon which the one who was washing himself sobbed, and spat, and cried out in great glee, "Do it again, Jack!"
10126{_}2224{_}The mother, seeing us laugh at the lads, said, "That big un's been powin' tother, an' th' little monkey's gone an' cut every smite o' th' lad's toppin' off. ""
10126{_}2225{_}Well," said the elder lad, "Aw did it so as nobody can lug him.
10126{_}2226{_}"And it certainly was a close clip.
10126{_}2227{_}We could see to the roots of the little fellow's hair all over his round, hard head.
10126{_}2228{_}"Come," said the mother, "yo two are makin' a nice floor for mo.
10126{_}2229{_}Thae'll do, mon; arto beawn to lother o' th' bit o' swoap away that one has to wash wi'; gi's howd on't this minute, an' go thi ways an' dry thisel', thae little pouse, thae."
10126{_}2230{_}We visited several other places in Scholes that day, but of these I will say something hereafter.
10126{_}2231{_}In the evening I returned home, and the thing that I best remember hearing on the way was an anecdote of two Lancashire men, who had been disputing a long time about something that one of them knew little of.
10126{_}2232{_}At last the other turned to him, and said, "Jem; does thae know what it is that makes me like thee so weel, owd brid?"
10126{_}2233{_}"Naw; what is it?"
10126{_}2234{_}"Why; it's becose thae'rt sich a ___ foo!"
10126{_}2235{_}"Well," replied the other, "never thee mind that;" and then, alluding to the subject they had been disputing about, he said, "Thae knows, Joe, aw know thae'rt reet enough; but, by th' men, aw'll not give in till mornin'."
10126{_}2236{_}CHAPTER XXI.
10126{_}2237{_}"Here, take this purse, thou whom the Heaven's plagues Have humbled to all strokes."
10126{_}2238{_}--King Lear.
10126{_}2239{_}In the afternoon of the last day I spent in Wigan, as I wandered with my friend from one cottage to another, in the long suburban lane called "Hardy Butts," I bethought me how oft I had met with this name of "Butts "connected with places in or close to the towns of Lancashire.
10126{_}2240{_}To me the original application of the name seems plain, and not uninteresting.
10126{_}2241{_}In the old days, when archery was common in England, the bowmen of Lancashire were famous; and it is more than likely that these yet so-called "Butts" are the places where archery was then publicly practised.
10126{_}2242{_}When Sir Edward Stanley led the war-smiths of Lancashire and Cheshire to Flodden Field, the men of Wigan are mentioned as going with the rest.
10126{_}2243{_}And among those "fellows fearce and freshe for feight," of whom the quaint old alliterative ballad describes the array:-  "A stock of striplings strong of heart, Brought up from babes with beef and bread, From Warton unto Warrington From Wigan unto Wiresdale--"  and, from a long list of the hills, and cloughs, and old towns of the county--the bowmen of Lancashire did their share of work upon that field.
10126{_}2244{_}The use of the bow lingered longer in Lancashire than in some parts of the kingdom--longer in England generally than many people suppose.
10126{_}2245{_}Sir Walter Scott says, in a note to his "Legend of Montrose:" "Not only many of the Highlanders in Montrose's army used these antique missiles, but even in England the bow and quiver, once the glory of the bold yeomen of that land, were occasionally used during the great civil wars."
10126{_}2246{_}But I have said enough upon this subject in this place.
10126{_}2247{_}My friend's business, and mine, in Wigan, that day, was connected with other things.
10126{_}2248{_}He was specially wishful that I should call upon an acquaintance of his, who lived in "Hardy Butts," an old man and very poor; a man heavily stricken by fortune's blows, yet not much tamed thereby; a man "steeped to the lips" in poverty, yet of a jocund spirit; a humorist and a politician, among his humble companions.
10126{_}2249{_}I felt curious to see this "Old John," of whom I heard so much.
10126{_}2250{_}We went to the cottage where he lived.
10126{_}2251{_}There was very little furniture in the place, and, like the house itself, it was neither good nor clean; but then the poverty-stricken pair were very old, and, so far as household comfort went, they had to look after themselves.
10126{_}2252{_}When we entered, the little wrinkled woman sat with her back to us, smoking, and gazing at the dirty grate, where a few hot cinders glowed dimly in the lowmost bars.
10126{_}2253{_}"Where's John?"
10126{_}2254{_}said my friend.
10126{_}2255{_}"He hasn't bin gone eawt aboon five minutes," said she, turning round to look at us, "Wur yo wantin' him?"
10126{_}2256{_}"Yes, I should like to see him."
10126{_}2257{_}She looked hard at my friend again, and then cried out, "Eh, is it yo?
10126{_}2258{_}Come, an' sit yo deawn!
10126{_}2259{_}aw'll go an' see iv aw can root him up for yo!"
10126{_}2260{_}But we thought it as well to visit some other houses in the neighbourhood, calling at old John's again afterwards; so we told the old woman, and came away.
10126{_}2261{_}My friend was well known to the poor people of that neighbourhood as a member of the Relief Committee, and we had not gone many yards down "Hardy Butts" before we drew near where three Irishwomen were sitting upon the doorsteps of a miserable cottage, chattering, and looking vacantly up and down the slutchy street.
10126{_}2262{_}As soon as they caught sight of my friend, one of the women called out, "Eh, here's Mr Lea!
10126{_}2263{_}Come here, now, Mr Lea, till I spake to ye.
10126{_}2264{_}Ah, now; couldn't ye do somethin' for old Mary beyant there?
10126{_}2265{_}Sure the colour of hunger's in that woman's face.
10126{_}2266{_}Faith, it's a pity to see the way she is,--neither husband nor son, nor chick nor child, nor bit nor sup, barrin' what folk that has nothin' can give to her,--the crayter."
10126{_}2267{_}" Oh, indeed, then, sir," said another, "I'll lave it to God; but that woman is starvin'.
10126{_}2268{_}She is little more nor skin an' bone,--and that's goin' less.
10126{_}2269{_}Faith, she's not long for this world, any how.
10126{_}2270{_}.
10126{_}2271{_}.
10126{_}2272{_}.
10126{_}2273{_}Bridget, ye might run an' see can she come here a minute.
10126{_}2274{_}.
10126{_}2275{_}.
10126{_}2276{_}.
10126{_}2277{_}But there she is, standin' at the corner.
10126{_}2278{_}Mary!
10126{_}2279{_}Come here, now, woman, till ye see the gentleman."
10126{_}2280{_}She was a miserable- looking creature; old, and ill, and thinly-clothed in rags, with a dirty cloth tied round her head.
10126{_}2281{_}My friend asked her some questions, which she answered slowly, in a low voice that trembled with more than the weakness of old age.
10126{_}2282{_}He promised to see to the relief of her condition immediately-- and she thanked him, but so feebly, that it seemed to me as if she had not strength enough left to care much whether she was relieved or not.
10126{_}2283{_}But, as we came away, the three Irishwomen, sitting upon the door- steps, burst forth into characteristic expressions of gratitude.
10126{_}2284{_}"Ah!
10126{_}2285{_}long life to ye, Mr Lea!
10126{_}2286{_}The prayer o' the poor is wid ye for evermore.
10126{_}2287{_}If there was ony two people goin' to heaven alive, you'll be wan o' them.
10126{_}2288{_}.
10126{_}2289{_}.
10126{_}2290{_}That ye may never know want nor scant,--for the good heart that's batein' in ye, Mr Lea."
10126{_}2291{_}We now went through some of the filthy alleys behind "Hardy Butts," till we came to the cottage of a poor widow and her two daughters.
10126{_}2292{_}The three were entirely dependent upon the usual grant of relief from the committee.
10126{_}2293{_}My friend called here to inquire why the two girls had not been to school during the previous few days; and whilst their mother was explaining the reason, a neighbour woman who had seen us enter, looked in at the door, and said, "Hey!
10126{_}2294{_}aw say, Mr Lea!"
10126{_}2295{_}"Well, what's the matter?"
10126{_}2296{_}" Whaw, there's a woman i'th next street at's gettin' four tickets fro th' relief folk, reggilar, an' her husban's addlin' thirty shillin' a week o' t' time, as a sinker--he is for sure.
10126{_}2297{_}Aw 'm noan tellin' yo a wort ov a lie.
10126{_}2298{_}Aw consider sick wark as that's noan reet--an' so mony folk clemmin' as there is i' Wigan."
10126{_}2299{_}He made a note of the matter; but he told me afterwards that such reports were often found to be untrue, having their origin sometimes in private spite or personal contention of some kind.
10126{_}2300{_}In the next house we called at, a widow woman lived, with her married daughter, who had a child at the breast.
10126{_}2301{_}The old woman told her story herself; the daughter never spoke a word, so far as I remember, but sat there, nursing, silent and sad, with half-averted face, and stealing a shy glance at us now and then, when she thought we were not looking at her.
10126{_}2302{_}It was a clean cottage, though it was scantily furnished with poor things; and they were both neat and clean in person, though their clothing was meagre and far worn.
10126{_}2303{_}I thought, also, that the old woman's language, and the countenances of both of them, indicated more natural delicacy of feeling, and more cultivation, than is common amongst people of their condition.
10126{_}2304{_}The old woman said, "My daughter has been eawt o' work a long time.
10126{_}2305{_}I can make about two shillings and sixpence a-week, an' we've a lodger that pays us two shillings a week; but we've three shillings a-week to pay for rent, an' we must pay it, too, or else turn out.
10126{_}2306{_}But I'm lookin' for a less heawse; for we cannot afford to stop here any longer, wi' what we have comin' in, --that is, if we're to live at o'."
10126{_}2307{_}I thought the house they were in was small enough and mean enough for the poorest creature, and, though it was kept clean, the neighbourhood was very unwholesome.
10126{_}2308{_}But this was another instance of how the unemployed operatives of Lancashire are being driven down from day to day deeper into the pestilent sinks of life in these hard times.
10126{_}2309{_}"This child of my daughter's," continued the old woman, in a low tone, "this child was born just as they were puttin' my husband into his coffin, an' wi' one thing an' another, we've had a deal o' trouble.
10126{_}2310{_}But one half o'th world doesn't know how tother lives.
10126{_}2311{_}My husban' lay ill i' bed three year; an' he suffered to that degree that he was weary o' life long before it were o'er.
10126{_}2312{_}At after we lost him, these bad times coom on, an' neaw, aw think we're poo'd deawn as nee to th' greawnd as ony body can be.
10126{_}2313{_}My daughter's husband went off a-seekin' work just afore that child was born,--an' we haven't heard from him yet."
10126{_}2314{_}My friend took care that his visit should result in lightening the weight of the old woman's troubles a little.
10126{_}2315{_}As we passed the doors of a row of new cottages at the top end of "Hardy Butts," a respectable old man looked out at one of the doorways, and said to my friend, "Could aw spake to yo a minute?"
10126{_}2316{_}We went in, and found the house remarkably clean, with good cottage furniture in it.
10126{_}2317{_}Two neighbour children were peeping in at the open door.
10126{_}2318{_}The old man first sent them away, and then, after closing the door, he pointed to a good-looking young woman who stood blushing at the entrance of the inner room, with a wet cloth in her hands, and he said, "Could yo do a bit o' summat to help this lass till sich times as hoo can get wark again?
10126{_}2319{_}Hoo's noather feyther nor mother, nor nought i'th world to tak to, but what aw can spare for her, an' this is a poor shop to come to for help.
10126{_}2320{_}Aw'm uncle to her."
10126{_}2321{_}"Well," said my friend, "and cannot you manage to keep her?"
10126{_}2322{_}"God bless yo!"
10126{_}2323{_}replied the old man, getting warm, "Aw cannot keep mysel'.
10126{_}2324{_}Aw will howd eawt as lung as aw can; but, yo know, what'll barely keep one alive 'll clem two.
10126{_}2325{_}Aw should be thankful iv yo could give her a bit o' help whol things are as they are."
10126{_}2326{_}Before the old man had done talking, his niece had crept away into the back room, as if ashamed of being the subject of such a conversation.
10126{_}2327{_}This case was soon disposed of to the satisfaction of the old man; after which we visited three other houses in the same block, of which I have nothing special to say, except that they were all inhabited by people brought down to destitution by long want of work, and living solely upon the relief fund, and upon the private charity of their old employers.
10126{_}2328{_}Upon this last source of relief too little has been said, because it has not paraded itself before the public eye; but I have had opportunities for seeing how wide and generous it is, and I shall have abundant occasion for speaking of it hereafter.
10126{_}2329{_}On our way back, we looked in at "Old John's" again, to see if he had returned home.
10126{_}2330{_}He had been in, and he had gone out again, so we came away, and saw nothing of him.
10126{_}2331{_}Farther down towards the town, we passed through Acton Square, which is a cleaner place than some of the abominable nooks of Scholes, though I can well believe that there is many a miserable dwelling in it, from what I saw of the interiors and about the doorways, in passing.
10126{_}2332{_}The last house we called at was in this square, and it was a pleasing exception to the general dirt of the neighbourhood.
10126{_}2333{_}It was the cottage of a stout old collier, who lost his right leg in one of Wright's pits some years ago.
10126{_}2334{_}My friend knew the family, and we called there more for the purpose of resting ourselves and having a chat than anything else.
10126{_}2335{_}The old man was gray-haired, but he looked very hale and hearty--save the lack of his leg.
10126{_}2336{_}His countenance was expressive of intelligence and good humour; and there was a touch of quiet majesty about his massive features.
10126{_}2337{_}There was, to me, a kind of rude hint of Christopher North in the old collier's appearance.
10126{_}2338{_}His wife, too, was a tall, strong-built woman, with a comely and a gentle face --a fit mate for such a man as he.
10126{_}2339{_}I thought, as she moved about, her grand bulk seemed to outface the narrow limits of the cottage.
10126{_}2340{_}The tiny house was exceedingly clean, and comfortably furnished.
10126{_}2341{_}Everything seemed to be in its appointed place, even to the sleek cat sleeping on the hearth.
10126{_}2342{_}There were a few books on a shelf, and a concertina upon a little table in the corner.
10126{_}2343{_}When we entered, the old collier was busy with the slate and pencil, and an arithmetic before him; but he laid them aside, and, doffing his spectacles, began to talk with us.
10126{_}2344{_}He said that they were a family of six, and all out of work; but he said that, ever since he lost his leg, the proprietors of the pit in which the accident happened (Wright's) had allowed him a pension of six shillings a week, which he considered very handsome.
10126{_}2345{_}This allowance just kept the wolf from their little door in these hard times.
10126{_}2346{_}In the course of our conversation I found that the old man read the papers frequently, and that he was a man of more than common information in his class.
10126{_}2347{_}I should have been glad to stay longer with him, but my time was up; so I came away from the town, thus ending my last ramble amongst the unemployed operatives of Wigan.
10126{_}2348{_}Since then the condition of the poor there has been steadily growing worse, which is sure to be heard of in the papers.
10126{_}2349{_}CHAPTER XXII.
10126{_}2350{_}AN INCIDENT BY THE WAYSIDE.
10126{_}2351{_}"Take physic, pomp!
10126{_}2352{_}Expose thyself to feel what wretches feel; That thou mayst shake the superflux to them, And show the Heavens more just."
10126{_}2353{_}--King Lear.
10126{_}2354{_}On the Saturday after my return from Wigan, a little incident fell in my way, which I thought worth taking note of at the time; and perhaps it may not be uninteresting to your readers.
10126{_}2355{_}On that day I went up to Levenshulme, to spend the afternoon with an old friend of mine, a man of studious habits, living in a retired part of that green suburb.
10126{_}2356{_}The time went pleasantly by whilst I was with the calm old student, conversing upon the state of Lancashire, and the strange events which are upheaving the civilised world in great billows of change,--and drinking in the peaceful charm which pervaded everything about the man and his house and the scene which it stood in.
10126{_}2357{_}After tea, he came with me across the fields to the "Midway Inn," on Stockport Road, where the omnibuses call on their way to Manchester.
10126{_}2358{_}It was a lovely evening, very clear and cool, and twilight was sinking upon the scene.
10126{_}2359{_}Waiting for the next omnibus, we leaned against the long wooden watering-trough in front of the inn.
10126{_}2360{_}The irregular old building looked picturesque in the soft light of declining day, and all around was so still that we could hear the voices of bowlers who were lingering upon the green, off at the north side of the house, and retired from the highway by an intervening garden.
10126{_}2361{_}The varied tones of animation, and the phrases uttered by the players, on different parts of the green, came through the quiet air with a cheery ring.
10126{_}2362{_}The language of the bowling-green sounds very quaint to people unused to the game.
10126{_}2363{_}"Too much land, James!"
10126{_}2364{_}cries one.
10126{_}2365{_}"Bravo, bully-bowl!
10126{_}2366{_}That's th' first wood!
10126{_}2367{_}Come again for more!"
10126{_}2368{_}cries another.
10126{_}2369{_}"Th' wrong bias, John!"
10126{_}2370{_}"How's that?"
10126{_}2371{_}"A good road; but it wants legs!
10126{_}2372{_}Narrow; narrow, o' to pieces!"
10126{_}2373{_}These, and such like phrases of the game, came distinctly from the green into the highway that quiet evening.
10126{_}2374{_}And here I am reminded, as I write, that the philosophic Doctor Dalton was a regular bowler upon Tattersall's green, at Old Trafford.
10126{_}2375{_}These things, however, are all aside from the little matters which I wish to tell.
10126{_}2376{_}As we stood by the watering-trough, listening to the voices of the bowlers, and to the occasional ringing of bells mingled with a low buzz of merriment inside the house, there were many travellers went by.
10126{_}2377{_}They came, nearly all of them, from the Manchester side; sometimes three or four in company, and sometimes a lonely straggler.
10126{_}2378{_}Some of them had poor-looking little bundles in their hands; and, with a few exceptions, their dress, their weary gait, and dispirited looks led me to think that many of them were unemployed factory operatives, who had been wandering away to beg where they would not be known.
10126{_}2379{_}I have met so many shame-faced, melancholy people in that condition during the last few months, that, perhaps, I may have somewhat over judged the number of these that belongs to that class.
10126{_}2380{_}But, in two or three cases, little snatches of conversation, uttered by them as they went by, plainly told that, so far as the speakers went, it was so; and, at last, a little thing befell, which, I am sure, represented the condition of many a thousand more in Lancashire just now.
10126{_}2381{_}Three young women stopped on the footpath in front of the inn, close to the place where we stood, and began to talk together in a very free, open way, quite careless of being overheard.
10126{_}2382{_}One of them was a stout, handsome young woman, about twenty-three.
10126{_}2383{_}Her dress was of light printed stuff, clean and good.
10126{_}2384{_}Her round, ruddy arms, her clear blond complexion, and the bright expression of her full open countenance, all indicated health and good-nature.
10126{_}2385{_}I guessed from her conversation, as well as from her general appearance, that she was a factory operative in full employ--though that is such a rare thing in these parts now.
10126{_}2386{_}The other two looked very poor and downhearted.
10126{_}2387{_}One was a short, thick-set girl, seemingly not twenty years of age; her face was sad, and she had very little to say.
10126{_}2388{_}The other was a thin, dark-haired, cadaverous woman, above thirty years of age, as I supposed; her shrunk visage was the picture of want, and her frank, child-like talk showed great simplicity of character.
10126{_}2389{_}The weather had been wet for some days previous; and the clothing of the two looked thin, and shower-stained.
10126{_}2390{_}It had evidently been worn a good while; and the colours were faded.
10126{_}2391{_}Each of them wore a shivery bit of shawl, in which their hands were folded, as if to keep them warm.
10126{_}2392{_}The handsome lass, who seemed to be in good employ, knew them both; but she showed an especial kindness towards the eldest of them.
10126{_}2393{_}As these two stood talking to their friend, we did not take much notice of what they were saying until two other young women came slowly from townwards, looking poor, and tired, and ill, like the first.
10126{_}2394{_}These last comers instantly recognised two of those who stood talking together in front of the inn, and one of them said to the other, "Eh, sitho; there's Sarah an' Martha here!
10126{_}2395{_}.
10126{_}2396{_}.
10126{_}2397{_}.
10126{_}2398{_}Eh, lasses; han yo bin a-beggin' too?"
10126{_}2399{_}"Ay, lass; we han;" replied the thin, dark complexioned woman; "Ay, lass; we han.
10126{_}2400{_}Aw've just bin tellin' Ann, here.
10126{_}2401{_}Aw never did sich a thing i' my life afore--never!
10126{_}2402{_}But it's th' first time and th' last for me,--it is that!
10126{_}2403{_}Aw'll go whoam; an' aw'll dee theer, afore aw'll go a-beggin' ony moor, aw will for sure!
10126{_}2404{_}Mon, it's sich a nasty, dirty job; aw'd as soon clem!
10126{_}2405{_}.
10126{_}2406{_}.
10126{_}2407{_}.
10126{_}2408{_}See yo, lasses; we set off this mornin'--Martha an' me, we set eawt this mornin' to go to Gorton Tank, becose we yerd that it wur sich a good place.
10126{_}2409{_}But one doesn't know wheer to go these times; an' one doesn't like to go a-beggin' among folk at they known.
10126{_}2410{_}Well, when we coom to Gorton we geet twopence-hawpenny theer; an' that wur o'.
10126{_}2411{_}Neaw, there's plenty moor beggin' besides us.
10126{_}2412{_}Well, at after that twopence-hawpenny, we geet twopence moor, an' that's o' at we'n getten.
10126{_}2413{_}But, eh, lasses, when aw coom to do it, aw hadn't th' heart to as for nought; aw hadn't for sure.
10126{_}2414{_}.
10126{_}2415{_}.
10126{_}2416{_}.
10126{_}2417{_}Martha an' me's walked aboon ten mile iv we'n walked a yard; an' we geet weet through th' first thing; an' aw wur ill when we set off, an' so wur Martha, too; aw know hoo wur, though hoo says nought.
10126{_}2418{_}Well; we coom back through t' teawn; an' we were both on us fair stagged up.
10126{_}2419{_}Aw never were so done o'er i' my life, wi' one thing an' another.
10126{_}2420{_}So we co'de a- seein' Ann here; an' hoo made us a rare good baggin'--th' lass did.
10126{_}2421{_}See yo; aw wur fit to drop o'th flags afore aw geet that saup o' warm tay into mo--aw wur for sure!
10126{_}2422{_}An' neaw, hoo's come'd a gate wi' us hitherto, an' hoo would have us to have a glass o' warm ale a- piece at yon heawse lower deawn a bit; an' aw dar say it'll do mo good, aw getten sich a cowd; but, eh dear, it's made mo as mazy as a tup; an' neaw, hoo wants us to have another afore we starten off whoam.
10126{_}2423{_}But it's no use; we mun' be gooin' on.
10126{_}2424{_}Aw'm noan used to it, an' aw connot ston it.
10126{_}2425{_}Aw'm as wake as a kittlin' this minute."
10126{_}2426{_}Ann, who had befriended them in this manner, was the handsome young woman who seemed to be in work; and now, the poor woman who had been telling the story, laid her hand upon her friend's shoulder and said, "Ann, thae's behaved very weel to us o' roads; an' neaw, lass, go thi ways whoam, an' dunnut fret abeawt us, mon.
10126{_}2427{_}Aw feel better neaw, aw do for sure.
10126{_}2428{_}We's be reet enough to-morn, lass.
10126{_}2429{_}Mon, there's awlus some way shap't.
10126{_}2430{_}That tay's done me a deeol o' good.
10126{_}2431{_}.
10126{_}2432{_}.
10126{_}2433{_}.
10126{_}2434{_}Go thi ways whoam, Ann; neaw do; or else aw shan't be yezzy abeawt tho!"
10126{_}2435{_}But Ann, who was wiping her eyes with her apron, replied, "Naw, naw; aw will not go yet, Sarah!"
10126{_}2436{_}.
10126{_}2437{_}.
10126{_}2438{_}.
10126{_}2439{_}And then she began to cry, "Eh, lasses; aw dunnot like to see yo o' this shap--aw dunnot for sure!
10126{_}2440{_}Besides, yo'n bin far enough today.
10126{_}2441{_}Come back wi' me.
10126{_}2442{_}Aw connot find reawm for both on yo; but thee come back wi' me, Sarah.
10126{_}2443{_}Aw'll find thee a good bed:  an' thae'rt welcome to a share o' what there is--as welcome as th' fleawers i May--thae knows that.
10126{_}2444{_}Thae'rt th' owdest o' th' two; an thae'rt noan fit to trawnce up an' deawn o' this shap.
10126{_}2445{_}Come back to eawr heawse; an' Martha'll go forrud to Stopput, (Stockport,)--winnot tho, Martha!
10126{_}2446{_}.
10126{_}2447{_}.
10126{_}2448{_}.
10126{_}2449{_}Thae knows, Martha," continued she, "thae knows, Martha, thae munnot think nought at me axin' Sarah, an' noan o' thee.
10126{_}2450{_}Yo should both on yo go back iv aw'd reawm,--but aw haven't.
10126{_}2451{_}Beside, thae'rt younger an' strunger than hoo is."
10126{_}2452{_}" Eh, God bless tho, lass," replied Martha, "aw know o' abeawt it.
10126{_}2453{_}Aw'd rayther Sarah would stop, for hoo'll be ill. Aw can go forrud by mysel', weel enough.
10126{_}2454{_}It's noan so fur, neaw."
10126{_}2455{_}But, here, Sarah, the eldest of the three, laid her hand once more upon the shoulder of her friend, and said in an earnest tone, "Ann!
10126{_}2456{_}it will not do, my lass!
10126{_}2457{_}Go aw MUN!
10126{_}2458{_}I never wur away fro whoam o' neet i my life,--never!
10126{_}2459{_}Aw connot do it, mon!
10126{_}2460{_}Beside, thae knows, aw've laft yon lad, an' never a wick soul wi' him!
10126{_}2461{_}He'd fret hissel' to deoth this neet, mon, if aw didn't go whoam!
10126{_}2462{_}Aw couldn't sleep a wink for thinkin' abeawt him!
10126{_}2463{_}Th' child would be fit to start eawt o'th heawse i'th deead time o'th neet a-seechin' mo,--aw know he would!
10126{_}2464{_}.
10126{_}2465{_}.
10126{_}2466{_}.
10126{_}2467{_}Aw mun go, mon:  God bless tho, Ann; aw'm obleeged to thee o' th' same.
10126{_}2468{_}But, thae knows heaw it is.
10126{_}2469{_}Aw mun goo!"
10126{_}2470{_}Here the omnibus came up, and I rode back to Manchester.
10126{_}2471{_}The whole conversation took up very little more time than it will take to read it; but I thought it worth recording, as characteristic of the people now suffering in Lancashire from no fault of their own.
10126{_}2472{_}I know the people well.
10126{_}2473{_}The greatest number of them would starve themselves to that degree that they would not be of much more physical use in this world, before they would condescend to beg.
10126{_}2474{_}But starving to death is hard work.
10126{_}2475{_}What will winter bring to them when severe weather begins to tell upon constitutions lowered in tone by a starvation diet--a diet so different to what they have been used to when in work?
10126{_}2476{_}What will the 1s.
10126{_}2477{_}6d.
10126{_}2478{_}a-head weekly do for them in that hard time?
10126{_}2479{_}If something more than this is not done for them, when more food, clothing, and fire are necessary to everybody, calamities may arise which will cost England a hundred times more than a sufficient relief--a relief worthy of those who are suffering, and of the nation they belong to--would have cost.
10126{_}2480{_}In the meantime the cold wings of winter already begin to overshadow the land; and every day lost involves the lives, or the future usefulness, of thousands of our best population.
10126{_}2481{_}CHAPTER XXIII.
10126{_}2482{_}WANDERING MINSTRELS; OR, WAILS OF THE WORKLESS POOR.
10126{_}2483{_}"For whom the heart of man shuts out, Straightway the heart of God takes in, And fences them all round about With silence, 'mid the world's loud din.
10126{_}2484{_}And one of his great charities Is music; and it doth not scorn To close the lids upon the eyes Of the weary and forlorn."
10126{_}2485{_}--JAMES RUSSEL LOWELL.
10126{_}2486{_}There is one feature of the distress in Lancashire which was seen strikingly upon the streets of our large towns during some months of 1862.
10126{_}2487{_}I allude to the wandering minstrelsy of the unemployed.
10126{_}2488{_}Swarms of strange, shy, sad-looking singers and instrumental performers, in the work-worn clothing of factory operatives, went about the busy city, pleading for help in touching wails of simple song--like so many wild birds driven by hard weather to the haunts of man.
10126{_}2489{_}There is something instructive, as well as affecting, in this feature of the troubled time.
10126{_}2490{_}These wanderers are only a kind of representative overflow of a vast number whom our streets will never see.
10126{_}2491{_}Any one well acquainted with Lancashire, will know how widespread the study of music is among its working population.
10126{_}2492{_}Even the inhabitants of our large towns know something more about this now than they knew a few months ago.
10126{_}2493{_}I believe there is no part of England in which the practice of sacred music is so widely and lovingly pursued amongst the working people as in the counties of Lancashire and Yorkshire.
10126{_}2494{_}There is no part of England where, until lately, there have been so many poor men's pianos, which have been purchased by a long course of careful savings from the workman's wages.
10126{_}2495{_}These, of course, have mostly been sold during the hard times to keep life in the owner and his family.
10126{_}2496{_}The great works of Handel, Haydn, Beethoven, and Mozart have solaced the toil of thousands of the poorest working people of Lancashire.
10126{_}2497{_}Anybody accustomed to wander among the moorlands of the country will remember how common it is to hear the people practising sacred music in their lonely cottages.
10126{_}2498{_}It is not uncommon to meet working men wandering over the wild hills, "where whip and heather grow," with their musical instruments, to take part in some village oratorio many miles away.
10126{_}2499{_}"That reminds me," as tale-tellers say, of an incident among the hills, which was interesting, though far from singular in my experience.
10126{_}2500{_}Up in the forest of Rosendale, between Derply Moor and the wild bill called Swinshaw, there is a little lone valley, a green cup in the mountains, called "Dean."
10126{_}2501{_}The inhabitants of this valley are so notable for their love of music, that they are known all through the vales of Rosendale as "Th' Deighn Layrocks," or "The Larks of Dean."
10126{_}2502{_}In the twilight of a glorious Sunday evening, in the height of summer, I was roaming over the heathery waste of Swinshaw, towards Dean, in company with a musical friend of mine, who lived in the neighbouring clough, when we saw a little crowd of people coming down a moorland slope, far away in front of us.
10126{_}2503{_}As they drew nearer, we found that many of them had musical instruments, and when we met, my friend recognised them as working people living in the district, and mostly well known to him.
10126{_}2504{_}He inquired where they had been; and they told him that they had "bin to a bit ov a sing deawn i'th Deighn."
10126{_}2505{_}"Well," said he, "can't we have a tune here?"
10126{_}2506{_}"Sure, yo con, wi' o' th' plezzur i'th world," replied he who acted as spokesman; and a low buzz of delighted consent ran through the rest of the company.
10126{_}2507{_}They then ranged themselves in a circle around their conductor, and they played and sang several fine pieces of psalmody upon the heather-scented mountain top.
10126{_}2508{_}As those solemn strains floated over the wild landscape, startling the moorfowl untimely in his nest, I could not help thinking of the hunted Covenanters of Scotland.
10126{_}2509{_}The all-together of that scene upon the mountains, "between the gloaming and the mirk," made an impression upon me which I shall not easily forget.
10126{_}2510{_}Long after we parted from them we could hear their voices, softening in sound as the distance grew, chanting on their way down the echoing glen, and the effect was wonderfully fine.
10126{_}2511{_}This little incident upon the top of Swinshaw is representative of things which often occur in the country parts of Lancashire, showing how widespread the love of music is among the working classes there.
10126{_}2512{_}Even in great manufacturing towns, it is very common, when passing cotton mills at work, to hear some fine psalm tune streaming in chorus from female voices, and mingling with the spoom of thousands of spindles.
10126{_}2513{_}The "Larks of Dean," like the rest of Lancashire operatives, must have suffered in this melancholy time; but I hope that the humble musicians of our county will never have occasion to hang their harps upon the willows.
10126{_}2514{_}Now, when fortune has laid such a load of sorrow upon the working people of Lancashire, it is a sad thing to see so many workless minstrels of humble life "chanting their artless notes in simple guise" upon the streets of great towns, amongst a kind of life they are little used to.
10126{_}2515{_}There is something very touching, too, in their manner and appearance.
10126{_}2516{_}They may be ill-shod and footsore; they may be hungry, and sick at heart, and forlorn in countenance, but they are almost always clean and wholesome-looking in person.
10126{_}2517{_}They come singing in twos and threes, and sometimes in more numerous bands, as if to keep one another in countenance.
10126{_}2518{_}Sometimes they come in a large family all together, the females with their hymn-books, and the men with their different musical instruments,--bits of pet salvage from the wrecks of cottage homes.
10126{_}2519{_}The women have sometimes children in their arms, or led by the hand; and they sometimes carry music-books for the men.
10126{_}2520{_}I have seen them, too, with little handkerchiefs of rude provender for the day.
10126{_}2521{_}As I said before, they are almost invariably clean in person, and their clothing is almost always sound and seemly in appearance, however poor and scanty.
10126{_}2522{_}Amongst these poor wanderers there is none of the reckless personal negligence and filth of hopeless reprobacy; neither is there a shadow of the professional ostentation of poverty amongst them.
10126{_}2523{_}Their faces are sad, and their manners very often singularly shame- faced and awkward; and any careful observer would see at a glance that these people were altogether unused to the craft of the trained minstrel of the streets.
10126{_}2524{_}Their clear, healthy complexion, though often touched with pallor, their simple, unimportunate demeanour, and the general rusticity of their appearance, shows them to be  "Suppliants who would blush To wear a tatter'd garb, however coarse; Whom famine cannot reconcile to filth; Who ask with painful shyness, and refused, Because deserving, silently retire."
10126{_}2525{_}The females, especially the younger ones, generally walk behind, blushing and hiding themselves as much as possible.
10126{_}2526{_}I have seen the men sometimes walk backwards, with their faces towards those who were advancing, as if ashamed of what they were doing.
10126{_}2527{_}And thus they went wailing through the busy streets, whilst the listening crowd looks on them pityingly and wonderingly, as if they were so many hungry shepherds from the mountains of Calabria.
10126{_}2528{_}This flood of strange minstrels partly drowned the slang melodies and the monotonous strains of ordinary street musicians for a while.
10126{_}2529{_}The professional gleeman "paled his ineffectual fire" before these mournful songsters.
10126{_}2530{_}I think there never was so much sacred music heard upon the streets of Manchester before.
10126{_}2531{_}With the exception of a favourite glee now and then, their music consisted chiefly of fine psalm tunes--often plaintive old strains, known and welcome to all, because they awaken tender and elevating remembrances of life.
10126{_}2532{_}"Burton," "French," "Kilmarnock," "Luther's Hymn," the grand "Old Hundred," and many other fine tunes of similar character, have floated daily in the air of our city, for months together.
10126{_}2533{_}I am sure that this choice does not arise from the minstrels themselves having craft enough to select "a mournful muse, soft pity to infuse."
10126{_}2534{_}It is the kind of music which has been the practice and pleasure of their lives, and it is a fortuitous thing that now, in addition to its natural plaintiveness, the sad necessity of the times lends a tender accompaniment to their simplest melody.
10126{_}2535{_}I doubt very much whether Leech's minor tunes were ever heard upon our streets till lately.
10126{_}2536{_}Leech was a working man, born near the hills, in Lancashire; and his anthems and psalm tunes are great favourites among the musical population, especially in the country districts.
10126{_}2537{_}Leech's harp was tuned by the genius of sorrow.
10126{_}2538{_}Several times lately I have heard the tender complaining notes of his psalmody upon the streets of the city.
10126{_}2539{_}About three months ago I heard one of his most pathetic tunes sung in the market-place by an old man and two young women.
10126{_}2540{_}The old man's dress had the peculiar hue and fray of factory work upon it, and he had a pair of clogs upon his stockingless feet.
10126{_}2541{_}They were singing one of Leech's finest minor tunes to Wesley's hymn:-  "And am I born to die, To lay this body down?
10126{_}2542{_}And must my trembling spirit fly Into a world unknown?
10126{_}2543{_}A land of deepest shade, Unpierced by human thought; The dreary country of the dead Where all things are forgot."
10126{_}2544{_}It is a tune often sung by country people in Lancashire at funerals; and, if I remember right, the same melody is cut upon Leech's gravestone in the old Wesleyan Chapel-yard, at Rochdale.
10126{_}2545{_}I saw a company of minstrels of the same class going through Brown Street, the other day, playing and singing,  "In darkest shades, if Thou appear, My dawning is begun."
10126{_}2546{_}The company consisted of an old man, two young men, and three young women.
10126{_}2547{_}Two of the women had children in their arms.
10126{_}2548{_}After I had listened to them a little while, thinking the time and the words a little appropriate to their condition, I beckoned to one of the young men, who came "sidling" slowly up to me.
10126{_}2549{_}I asked him where they came from, and he said, "Ash'n."
10126{_}2550{_}In answer to another question, he said, "We're o' one family.
10126{_}2551{_}Me an' yon tother's wed. That's his wife wi' th' chylt in her arms, an' hur wi' th' plod shawl on's mine."
10126{_}2552{_}I asked if the old man was his father.
10126{_}2553{_}"Ay," replied he, "we're o' here, nobbut two.
10126{_}2554{_}My mother's ill i' bed, an' one o' my sisters is lookin' after her."
10126{_}2555{_}" Well, an' heaw han yo getten on?"
10126{_}2556{_}said I.
10126{_}2557{_}"Oh, we'n done weel; but we's come no moor," replied he.
10126{_}2558{_}Another day, there was an instrumental band of these operatives playing sacred music close to the Exchange lamp.
10126{_}2559{_}Amongst the crowd around, I met with a friend of mine.
10126{_}2560{_}He told me that the players were from Staleybridge.
10126{_}2561{_}They played some fine old tunes, by desire, and, among the rest, they played one called "Warrington.
10126{_}2562{_}"When they had played it several times over, my friend turned to me and said, "That tune was composed by a Rev.
10126{_}2563{_}Mr Harrison, who was once minister of Cross Street Unitarian Chapel, in Manchester; and, one day, an old weaver, who had come down from the hills, many miles, staff in hand, knocked at the minister's door, and asked if there was 'a gentleman co'de' Harrison lived theer?'
10126{_}2564{_}'Yes.'
10126{_}2565{_}'Could aw see him?'
10126{_}2566{_}'Yes.'
10126{_}2567{_}When the minister came to the door, the old weaver looked hard at him, for a minute, and said, 'Are yo th' mon 'at composed that tune co'de Worrington?'
10126{_}2568{_}'Yes,' replied the minister, 'I believe I am.'
10126{_}2569{_}'Well,' said the old weaver, 'give me your hond!
10126{_}2570{_}It's a good un!'
10126{_}2571{_}He then shook hands with him heartily again, and saying, 'Well, good day to yo,' he went his way home again, before the old minister could fairly collect his scattered thoughts."
10126{_}2572{_}I do not know how it is that these workless minstrels are gradually becoming rarer upon the streets than they were a few months ago.
10126{_}2573{_}Perhaps it is because the unemployed are more liberally relieved now than they were at first.
10126{_}2574{_}I know that now many who have concealed their starving condition are ferreted out and relieved as far as possible.
10126{_}2575{_}Many of these street wanderers have gone home again disgusted, to pinch out the hard time in proud obscurity; and there are some, no doubt, who have wandered away to other parts of England.
10126{_}2576{_}Of these last, we may naturally expect that a few may become so reconciled to a life of wandering minstrelsy that they may probably never return to settled labour again.
10126{_}2577{_}But "there's a divinity that shapes our ends, rough-hew them how we will."
10126{_}2578{_}Let us trust that the Great Creator may comfort and relieve them, "according to their several necessities, giving them patience under their sufferings, and a happy issue out of all their afflictions."
10126{_}2579{_}LETTER AND SPEECHES UPON THE COTTON FAMINE     LETTERS OF A LANCASHIRE LAD ON THE COTTON FAMINE.
10126{_}2580{_}The following extracts are from the letters of Mr. John Whittaker, "A Lancashire Lad," one of the first writers whose appeals through the press drew serious attention to the great distress in Lancashire during the Cotton Famine.
10126{_}2581{_}There is no doubt that his letters in The Times, and to the Lord Mayor of London, led to the Mansion House Fund.
10126{_}2582{_}In The Times of April 14, 1862, appeared the first of a series of letters, pleading the cause of the distressed operatives.
10126{_}2583{_}He said:-  "I am living in the centre of a vast district where there are many cotton mills, which in ordinary times afford employment to many thousands of 'hands,' and food to many more thousands of mouths.
10126{_}2584{_}With rare exceptions, quietness reigns at all those mills.
10126{_}2585{_}.
10126{_}2586{_}.
10126{_}2587{_}.
10126{_}2588{_}It may be that our material atmosphere is somewhat brighter than it was, but our social atmosphere is much darker and denser.
10126{_}2589{_}Hard times have come; and we have had them sufficiently long to know what they mean.
10126{_}2590{_}We have fathers sitting in the house at mid-day, silent and glum, while children look wistfully about, and sometimes whimper for bread which they cannot have.
10126{_}2591{_}We have the same fathers who, before hard times came, were proud men, who would have thought 'beggar' the most opprobrious epithet you could have hit them with; but who now are made humble by the sight of wife and children almost starving, and who go before 'relief committees,' and submit to be questioned about their wants with a patience and humility which it is painful, almost schocking, to witness, And some others of these fathers turn out in the morning with long besoms as street-sweepers, while others again go to breaking stones in the town's yard or open road-side, where they are unprotected from the keen east winds, which add a little more to the burden of misery which they have to bear just now.
10126{_}2592{_}But, harder even than this, our factory-women and girls have had to turn out; and, plodding a weary way from door to door, beg a bit of bread or a stray copper, that they may eke out the scanty supply at home.
10126{_}2593{_}Only the other day, while taking a long stroll in the country lying about the town in which I live, I met a few of these factory-girls, and was stopped by their not very beggar-like question of 'Con yo help us a bit?'
10126{_}2594{_}They were just such as my own sisters; and as I saw and heard them, I was almost choked as I fancied my sisters come to such a pass as that.
10126{_}2595{_}'Con yo help us a bit?'
10126{_}2596{_}asked these factory girls.
10126{_}2597{_}.
10126{_}2598{_}.
10126{_}2599{_}.
10126{_}2600{_}I have heard of ladies whose whole lives seem to be but a changing from one kind of pleasure to another; who suffer chiefly from what they call ennui, (a kind of disease from which my sisters are not likely to suffer at all,) and to whom a new pleasure to enjoy would be something like what a new world to conquer would be to Alexander.
10126{_}2601{_}Why should they not hear our Lancashire girls' cry of 'Con yo help us a bit?'
10126{_}2602{_}Why should not they be reminded that these girls in cotton gowns and wooden clogs are wending their way towards the same heaven--or, alas, towards the same hell--whither wend all the daughters of Eve, no matter what their outer condition and dress?
10126{_}2603{_}Why should not they be asked to think how these striving girls have to pray daily, 'Lead us not into temptation,' while temptations innumerable stand everywhere about them?
10126{_}2604{_}Those of us who are men would rather do much than let our sisters go begging.
10126{_}2605{_}May not some of us take to doing more to prevent it?
10126{_}2606{_}I remember some poetry about the  'Sister bloodhounds, Want and Sin,'  and know that they hunt oftener together than singly.
10126{_}2607{_}We have felt the fangs of the first:  upon how many of us will the second pounce?"
10126{_}2608{_}In a second letter, inserted in The Times of April 22, 1862, the same writer says:--"Even during the short time which has elapsed since I wrote last week, many things have combined to show that the distress is rapidly increasing, and that there is a pressing need that we should go beyond the borders of our own county for help.
10126{_}2609{_}.
10126{_}2610{_}.
10126{_}2611{_}.
10126{_}2612{_}I remember what I have read of the Godlike in man, and I look with a strange feeling upon the half-famished creatures I see hourly about me.
10126{_}2613{_}I cannot pass through a street but I see evidences of deep distress.
10126{_}2614{_}I cannot sit at home half-an-hour without having one or more coming to ask for bread to eat.
10126{_}2615{_}But what comes casually before me is as nothing when compared with that deeper distress which can only be seen by those who seek it.
10126{_}2616{_}.
10126{_}2617{_}.
10126{_}2618{_}.
10126{_}2619{_}There have been families who have been so reduced that the only food they have had has been a porridge made of Indian meal.
10126{_}2620{_}They could not afford oatmeal, and even of their Indian meal porridge they could only afford to have two meals a day.
10126{_}2621{_}They have been so ashamed of their coarser food that they have done all that was possible to hide their desperate state from those about them.
10126{_}2622{_}It has only been by accident that it has been found out, and then they have been caught hurriedly putting away the dishes that contained their loathsome food.
10126{_}2623{_}A woman, whose name I could give, and whose dwelling I could point to, was said not only to be in deep distress, but to be also ill of fever.
10126{_}2624{_}She was visited.
10126{_}2625{_}On entering the lower room of the house, the visitors saw that there was not a scrap of furniture; the woman, fever-stricken, sat on an orange-box before a low fire; and to prevent the fire from going quite out, she was pulling her seat to pieces for fuel bit by bit.
10126{_}2626{_}The visitors looked upstairs.
10126{_}2627{_}There was no furniture there-- only a bit of straw in a corner, which served as the bed of the woman's four children.
10126{_}2628{_}In another case a woman, who was said to be too weak to apply for relief, was visited.
10126{_}2629{_}Her husband had been out of work a long time by reason of his illness; he was now of a fashion recovered, and had gone off to seek for work.
10126{_}2630{_}He left his wife and three children in their cellar-home.
10126{_}2631{_}The wife was very near her confinement, and had not tasted food for two or three days.
10126{_}2632{_}.
10126{_}2633{_}.
10126{_}2634{_}.
10126{_}2635{_}There are in this town some hundreds of young single women who have been self-dependent, but who are now entirely without means.
10126{_}2636{_}Nearly all of these are good English girls, who have quietly fought their own life-battle, but who now have hard work to withstand the attacks this grim poverty is making.
10126{_}2637{_}I am told of a case in which one of these girls was forced to become one of that class of whom poor Hood sang in his 'Bridge of Sighs.'
10126{_}2638{_}She was an orphan, had no relations here, and was tossed about from place to place till she found her way to a brothel.
10126{_}2639{_}Thank God, she has been rescued.
10126{_}2640{_}Our relief fund has been the means of relieving her from that degradation; but cannot those who read my letter see how strong are the temptations which their want places in the way of these poor girls!"
10126{_}2641{_}On 25th April a number of city merchants, most of whom were interested in the cotton manufacture, waited upon the Lord Mayor of London, with a view to interest him, and through him the public at large, in the increasing distress among the operative population in the manufacturing districts of Lancashire.
10126{_}2642{_}Previous to this, the "Lancashire Lad" had made a private appeal, by letter, to the Lord Mayor, in which he said:-  "Local means are nearly exhausted, and I am convinced that if we have not help from without, our condition will soon be more desperate than I or any one else who possesses human feelings can wish it to become.
10126{_}2643{_}To see the homes of those whom we know and respect, though they are but working men, stripped of every bit of furniture--to see long-cherished books and pictures sent one by one to the pawn-shop, that food may be had--and to see that food almost loathsome in kind, and insufficient in quantity,--are hard, very hard things to bear.
10126{_}2644{_}But those are not the worst things.
10126{_}2645{_}In many of our cottage homes there is now nothing left by the pawning of which a few pence may be raised, and the mothers and sisters of we 'Lancashire lads' have turned out to beg, and ofttimes knock at the doors of houses in which there is as much destitution as there is in our own; while the fathers and the lads themselves think they are very fortunate if they can earn a shilling or two by street-sweeping or stone breaking.
10126{_}2646{_}.
10126{_}2647{_}.
10126{_}2648{_}.
10126{_}2649{_}Will you not do for us what you have done for others--become the recipient of whatever moneys those who are inclined to help us may send to you?"
10126{_}2650{_}The Lord Mayor, having listened to the deputation, read them the personal appeal, and, "before separating, the deputation engaged to form themselves into a provisional committee, to correspond with any local one which circumstances might render it desirable to set on foot in some central part of the distressed districts."
10126{_}2651{_}Immediately afterwards, the Lord Mayor, on taking his seat in the justice-room, stated that "he was ready, with the assistance of the gentlemen of the deputation, to act in the way desired.
10126{_}2652{_}.
10126{_}2653{_}.
10126{_}2654{_}.
10126{_}2655{_}He could not himself take any part in the distribution.
10126{_}2656{_}All he could do was to be the medium of transmission; and as soon as he knew that some organisation had been formed, either in the great city of Manchester, or in some other part of Lancashire, in which the public might feel confidence, he should be ready to send the small sums he had already received, and any others that might be intrusted to him from time to time."
10126{_}2657{_}And thus originated the first general subscription for the cotton operatives, and which, before it closed, reached the magnificent sum of 528 pounds,336, 9s.
10126{_}2658{_}9d.
10126{_}2659{_}MR COBDEN'S SPEECH ON THE COTTON FAMINE.
10126{_}2660{_}On the 29th of April 1862, a meeting of gentlemen residents, called by Thomas Goadsby, Esq., Mayor of Manchester, was held in the Town Hall of that city, to consider the propriety of forming a relief committee.
10126{_}2661{_}'"The late Mr Richard Cobden, M.P., attended, and recommended a bold appeal to the whole country, declaring with prophetic keenness of vision that not less than 1,000,000 pounds would be required to carry the suffering operatives through the crisis, whilst the subscriptions up to that date amounted only to 180,000 pounds."
10126{_}2662{_}On the motion of a vote of thanks to the Mayor of Manchester, who was retiring from the mayoralty, Mr Cobden said:-  "Before that resolution is passed, I will take the opportunity of making an observation.
10126{_}2663{_}I have had the honour of having my name added to this committee, and the first thing I asked of my neighbour here was--'What are the functions of the general committee?'
10126{_}2664{_}And I have heard that they amount to nothing more than to attend here once a month, and receive the report of the executive committee as to the business done and the distribution of the funds.
10126{_}2665{_}I was going to suggest to you whether the duties of the general committee might not be very much enlarged--whether it might not be employed very usefully in increasing the amount of subscriptions.
10126{_}2666{_}I think all our experience must have taught us that, with the very best cause in the world in hand, the success of a public subscription depends very much upon the amount of activity in those who solicit it; and I think, in order to induce us to make a general and national effort to raise additional funds in this great emergency, it is only necessary to refer to and repeat one or two facts that have been stated in this report just read to us.
10126{_}2667{_}I find it stated that it is estimated that the loss of wages at present is at the rate of 136,094 pounds per week, and there is no doubt that the savings of the working classes are almost exhausted.
10126{_}2668{_}Now, 136,094 pounds per week represents upwards of 7,000,000 pounds sterling per annum, and that is the rate at which the deduction is now being made from the wages of labour in this district.
10126{_}2669{_}I see it stated in this report that the resources which this committee can at present foresee that it will possess to relieve this amount of distress are 25,000 pounds a month for the next five months, which is at the rate of 300,000 pounds per annum; so that we foresee at present the means of affording a relief of something less than five per cent upon the actual amount of the loss of wages at present incurred by the working classes of this country.
10126{_}2670{_}But I need not tell honourable gentlemen present, who are so practically acquainted with this district, that that loss of seven millions in wages per annum is a very imperfect measure of the amount of suffering and loss which will be inflicted on this community three or four months hence.
10126{_}2671{_}It may be taken to be 10,000,000 pounds; and that 10,000,000 pounds of loss of wages before the next spring is by no means a measure of the loss this district will incur; for you must take it that the capitalists will be incurring also a loss on their fixed machinery and buildings; and though perhaps not so much as that of the labourer, it will be a very large amount, and possibly, in the opinion of some people, will very nearly approach it.
10126{_}2672{_}That is not all:  Mr Farnall has told us that at present the increase of the rates in this district is at the rate of 10,000 pounds per week.
10126{_}2673{_}That will be at the rate of half a million per annum, and, of course, if this distress goes on, that rate must be largely increased, perhaps doubled.
10126{_}2674{_}This shows the amount of pressure which is threatening this immediate district.
10126{_}2675{_}I have always been of opinion that this distress and suffering must be cumulative to a degree which few people have ever foreseen, because your means of meeting the difficulty will diminish just in proportion as the difficulty will increase.
10126{_}2676{_}Mr Farnall has told us that one-third of the rateable property will fall out of existence, as it were, and future rates must be levied upon two-thirds.
10126{_}2677{_}But that will be by no means the measure of the condition of things two or three months hence, because every additional rate forces out of existence a large amount of saleable property; and the more you increase your rates the more you diminish the area over which those rates are to be productive.
10126{_}2678{_}This view of the case has a very important bearing, also, upon the condition of the shop-keeping class as well as the classes of mill-owners and manufacturers who have not a large amount of floating capital.
10126{_}2679{_}There is no doubt but a very large amount of the shopkeeping class are rapidly falling into the condition of the unemployed labourers.
10126{_}2680{_}When I was at Rochdale the other day, I heard a very sorrowful example of it.
10126{_}2681{_}There was a poor woman who kept a shop, and she was threatened with a distraint for her poor-rate.
10126{_}2682{_}She sold the Sunday clothes of her son to pay the poor-rate, and she received a relief- ticket when she went to leave her rate.
10126{_}2683{_}That is a sad and sorrowful example, but I am afraid it will not be a solitary one for a long time.
10126{_}2684{_}Then you have the shopkeeping class descending to the rank of the operatives.
10126{_}2685{_}It must be so.
10126{_}2686{_}Withdraw the custom of 7,000,000 pounds per annum, which has ceased to be paid in wages, from the shopkeepers, and the consequence must present itself to any rational mind.
10126{_}2687{_}We have then another class--the young men of superior education employed in warehouses and counting-houses.
10126{_}2688{_}A great number of these will rapidly sink to the condition in which you find the operative classes.
10126{_}2689{_}All this will add to the distress and the embarrassment of this part of the kingdom.
10126{_}2690{_}Now, to meet this state of things you have the poor-law relief, which is the only relief we can rely upon, except that which comes from our own voluntary exertions.
10126{_}2691{_}Well, but any one who has read over this report of Mr Farnall, just laid before us, must see how inadequate this relief must be.
10126{_}2692{_}It runs up from one shilling and a half-penny in the pound to one shilling and fourpence or one shilling and fivepence; there is hardly one case in which the allowance is as much as two shillings per week for each individual--I won't call them paupers-- each distressed individual.
10126{_}2693{_}Now, there is one point to which I would wish to bring the attention of the committee in reference to this subject--it is a most important one, in my appreciation.
10126{_}2694{_}In ordinary times, when you give relief to the poor, that relief being given when the great mass of workpeople are in full employment, the measure of your relief to an isolated family or two that may be in distress is by no means the measure of the amount of their subsistence, because we all know that in prosperous times, when the bulk of the working people are employed, they are always kind to each other.
10126{_}2695{_}The poor, in fact, do more to relieve the poor than any other class.
10126{_}2696{_}A working man and his family out of employment in prosperous times could get a meal at a neighbour's house, just as we, in our class, could get a meal at a neighbour's house if it was a convenience to us in making a journey.
10126{_}2697{_}But recollect, now the whole mass of the labouring and working population is brought down to one sad level of destitution, and what you allow them from the poor-rates, and what you allow them from these voluntary subscriptions, are actually the measure of all that they will obtain for their subsistence.
10126{_}2698{_}And that being so general, producing a great depression of spirits, as well as physical prostration, you are in great danger of the health and strength of this community suffering, unless something more be done to meet the case than I fear is yet provided for it.
10126{_}2699{_}All this brings me to this conclusion--that something more must be done by this general committee than has been done, to awaken the attention of the public generally to the condition of this part of the country.
10126{_}2700{_}It is totally exceptional.
10126{_}2701{_}The state of things has no parallel in all history.
10126{_}2702{_}It is impossible you could point out to me another case, in which, in a limited sphere, such as we have in Lancashire, and in the course of a few months, there has been a cessation of employment at the rate of 7,000,000 pounds sterling per annum in wages.
10126{_}2703{_}There has been nothing like it in the history of the world for its suddenness, for the impossibility of dealing with it, or managing it in the way of an effective remedy.
10126{_}2704{_}Well, the country at large must be made acquainted with these facts.
10126{_}2705{_}How is that to be done?
10126{_}2706{_}It can only be by the diffusion of information from this central committee.
10126{_}2707{_}An appeal must be made to the whole country, if this great destitution is to be met in any part by voluntary aid.
10126{_}2708{_}The nation at large must be made fully acquainted with the exigency of the case, and we must be reminded that a national responsibility rests upon us.
10126{_}2709{_}I will, therefore, suggest that this general committee should be made a national committee, and we shall then get rid of this little difficulty with the Lord Mayor.
